"I know they say the grass is greener on the other side, but that's a lie [...] It takes time, darling, teach a caged bird how to fly" – Caged Bird, Myles Cameron.

The familiar, short drive from the hippodrome to her family's old house, a route she had taken for so many years, now felt utterly new to Agatha. She knew every curve of that dirt road, yet everything had changed.

Darby had been the backdrop of her childhood, her youth, a predictable loop of coming and going. But it was clear that the town, like her own life, had followed its own unstoppable course. Where there was once open field and an uncluttered horizon, there were now residential developments, markets, businesses, and people — so many people. Years ago, driving these backroads, she knew every face she passed; now, most were strangers. She couldn't help but register the sheer extent of the growth. When she moved to California, she used to say that the entire town was just the ranch and a scattering of people. Now, it was a full-fledged town, teeming with new buildings, all fitting harmoniously among the pastures, the grazing cattle, the running horses, and the endless trees that framed the landscape.

As she drove, taking in the transformation, Agatha realized that the place she had always called home had matured, just as she had. A warmth spread through her chest, a sense of quiet welcome, like a silent, beautiful reunion between her past and her present. Her town had grown up. It was impossible not to dwell on what she had missed, how drastically everything had shifted. While Benito mentioned the town's growth, and Javier spoke of it with pride, she had never truly grasped the scale of the change. She hadn't thought it was significant, but now she saw it with her own eyes. Her small, isolated corner of the world had completely transformed and was now a sprawling city. A place she didn't know, yet a home that embraced her, even without knowing the woman she had become. It felt as if those thirteen years were an entire lifetime.

With both hands gripping the steering wheel, she absorbed her surroundings, breathing deeply. Her heart still pounded with the rush of old memories now blending with the vastness of the new. It was too much to process at once: the past made present, an entire life laid out before her — a life she had abandoned, and she still didn't understand how it had come to pass. The familiar feeling in her chest was now a knot she couldn't name, a clash of emotions that left an icy, unsettling restlessness in her stomach.

Nicholas was now sulking in the back seat. Even the gift shop hat hadn't managed to lift his spirits, and Agatha had practically dragged him back to the car. She had missed the race's conclusion, the riders on the podium, and Riandra's moment of victory. She knew returning to Darby meant encountering Rio, but she was utterly unprepared for the abruptness of their meeting and the intense jolt of looking into her eyes, even from a distance. It was a visceral punch to the gut, a world away from the easy familiarity she remembered.

Thirteen long years had passed — thirteen years without seeing that face. Years spent consciously stifling the urge to wonder about Rio: what direction her life had taken, what choices she had made, if she still raced, if her old dreams still held. Everything she had long suppressed now rushed forth, coursing through her veins and sending her heart hammering. She felt tiny, instantly engulfed by a maelstrom of past emotions, as if she had unwittingly opened a true Pandora's Box, its contents waiting for years to spill out.

Thirteen years. While some might call it a short span, to Agatha, it felt like an entire lifetime. She wasn't the same person; quite the opposite. She felt she had transformed into someone new, a woman who barely recognized the barefoot girl who once ran across that dirt ground. In a bitter triumph, she had realized her dream of separating herself from that girl, evolving into a successful career woman with a family in the big city. Yet, Rio, too, was entirely different. This realization — the anxiety of reuniting with someone she had deeply loved for years, only to find she no longer knew that person — was overwhelming.

The town, her former home, now a small, established city, stared back at her as if she were a complete stranger, which, in truth, she was. She was flooded with so many conflicting emotions she couldn't give any of them a name.

It didn't take long to arrive at the ranch. Her lips parted involuntarily just as her son gasped in the back seat. In the rearview mirror, she saw his scowl had vanished, replaced by a look of sheer wonder — a feeling she instantly shared.

Years ago, the main house commanded the landscape across miles of open land and tall grass. The stable, the corral near the henhouse, and her own small house were the only structures near the Vidals'. Now, two other houses, just as large as hers, stood beside it, forming a small village. In the distance, the stable had expanded significantly, the corral was joined by more outbuildings, and the main house appeared to have doubled in size, stretching further across the land. She was a new woman, and this was unequivocally a new home — nothing was the same; everything had irrevocably changed.

The moment she parked the car in front of the house, her mother opened the door, her eyebrows furrowed in palpable emotion. Agatha let out the breath she hadn't realized she was holding, a profound relief calming her entire heart. Outwardly, she was an accomplished woman, but the lost, confused girl inside still longed for the familiar comfort of her mother's embrace.

"Darling, I missed you so much!” Evanora exclaimed, her voice thick with emotion. Agatha collapsed into the hug, sighing deeply as she felt the familiar, gentle touch in her hair. Her mother’s worried hands swept over her face, gazing at her with intense tenderness. “Honey, it’s so good to have you here,” Evanora breathed out. Agatha could only nod, her face buried in the curve of her mother’s neck.

“I missed you so much, mom,” she confessed, her voice muffled. Her mother held her face, studying her with furrowed brows. Under that attentive gaze, Agatha felt utterly exposed, as if the entire defensive wall she had slowly constructed was crumbling to the ground.

“All that matters is that you’re home now,” Evanora said with a faint, reassuring smile. Agatha simply nodded in return.

The painful truth was that Agatha had never found the courage to be fully honest with her mother about her life or her marriage. For years, she had meticulously nurtured the beautiful fantasy that everything had unfolded perfectly: she had found the love of her life, a soulmate so flawless she seemed to be straight out of a romance movie. Her mother adored Tessa with fierce devotion, and Agatha knew she could never shatter that illusion. In a strange way, she felt someone needed to keep believing in it; it was the only thing that made her fractured reality feel even remotely real.

Lilia was aware, yet she didn’t know the whole story. During every visit, while Evanora was happily captivated by Tessa's refined manners and charm, Lilia kept a discerning eye on Agatha’s every movement. It was nearly impossible for Agatha to conceal the emptiness in her eyes. She tried, forcing smiles, attempting to genuinely enjoy the affection — even if it was all a performance. But it didn't take long for Lilia to notice that none of her smiles ever reached her eyes.

It was during a trip to Los Angeles for Nicholas's second birthday that Lilia, purely by chance, overheard one of the countless screaming matches they desperately tried to keep behind closed doors. From that point on, Lilia became her reluctant confidante — at least for the parts Agatha could bear to articulate, as some truths she couldn't even mentally form, let alone face.

Agatha knew she had to eventually confess the truth and explain the situation, especially given the car full of packed suitcases that clearly signaled a stay longer than just a week. But the knot in her throat remained tight. She couldn't fathom how to broach the subject, because admitting it to her mother would be admitting it to herself. Although she knew there was no turning back — a path she genuinely desired — she was adrift, unsure of her next move. She just craved some emotional refuge. Javier’s recent death already felt like a crushing weight; she didn't want her own distress to become the focus. Her family, and she herself, were already deep in mourning — the time for her to address her own chaos would come.

“Look at you, my boy! You came ready for the ranch!” her mother exclaimed, smiling brightly as Nicholas shyly opened the car door. His wide brown eyes took in the surroundings, partially hidden by the thick leather hat perched on his head.

“Who is this cowboy?” Lilia asked, stepping out with her hands on her hips and an inviting smile. Nicholas, awkward but unable to hide his excitement, smiled back, biting his lip as his arms swung. Agatha’s heart warmed, and she couldn't help but furrow her eyebrows in shared delight. “Where did you get that hat?”

Nicholas looked at his mother, who returned his smile. Agatha let out a sigh of profound relief; seeing him so animated was a balm. For months, her son had displayed nothing but listless apathy, and the fact that he had instantly begun to come out of his shell just by arriving at the ranch validated her decision. If not for herself, she knew she had done this for him.

***

The flurry surrounding Agatha’s arrival quickly subsided. Her mother had peppered her with questions — Why didn't you call? You could have told us, Agatha; you rented a car! Benito would have picked you up! How was the trip? And Tessa? Why didn't she come? — and Agatha answered each one calmly, carefully skirting around the topics she wished to avoid. Slowly, she began pulling suitcases from the car, aware of her mother’s careful, inquisitive gaze. Meanwhile, Lilia had opened the back gate, and Nicholas, content to retreat into his own silence, was peering through the fences, comfortably perched to watch the horses running and grazing in the vast open field.

Once the immediate rush was over and the essential items were inside, Lilia prepared a tray of banana muffins, a jug of orange juice, and some tea. They settled onto the porch under the day’s weak, tentative sun. A strong, cold breeze tousled her son's curls as he sat yards away on the fence, completely lost in the sight of the animals and all that endless green.

“He’s still not speaking?” Lilia asked, her tone sweet but laced with worry. Agatha let out a tired sigh, gripping the steaming mug of chamomile tea in both hands.

“Not a word,” she replied, pressing her lips together and taking a deep breath, but ultimately shaking her head. “But he lit up when we got here. He has a connection with horses; he was enchanted. I haven't seen him like that in a long time.”

“How is that possible, really?” her mother mused, sounding contemplative. Agatha looked back, watching her gaze at him in the distance, his tiny curls disheveled by the wind. “To love the ranch so much when he was raised surrounded by buildings... Javier certainly filled that boy's head with ideas.” Evanora's voice held a contained sadness. Agatha lowered her eyes, a clenching in her heart, yet a faint, nostalgic smile touched her lips, because it was true.

Every time her godfather visited Los Angeles, he arrived with hands full of gifts and a voice brimming with stories. His visits were frequent; he had a guaranteed place in her new family. Shortly after Agatha and Tessa celebrated their first anniversary, Benito, freshly eighteen, moved in with them in California to begin college. Javier, a perpetually proud and doting father, insisted on being present, constantly bridging the distance. She saw him more often than her own parents.

When Nicholas was born, this bond only deepened. Benito had graduated and lived just down the street, but Javier continued to appear. He treated her son as his own grandson, loving the boy fiercely. He spent afternoons rocking him in his lap, singing country tunes, strumming soft chords on his ever-present old guitar, and telling stories of the farm and horses as if he had spent months memorizing them just for Nicholas. The boy, despite his silence, couldn't hide his affection; his little brown eyes would shine when Javier arrived, and they would sit together for hours — Nicholas at Javier's feet, absorbing every story as if each word were a piece of a world he didn't know but desperately wanted to see.

Javier had promised to bring him to visit the ranch and see everything he described up close, but Tessa never permitted it. Despite the polite excuses she gave Javier, she would constantly grumble to Agatha that she wanted distance from those country folks and wouldn't let her son turn into a hick. Now, Agatha felt a silent sorrow for depriving Nicholas of that bond with the man who had been a grandfather figure to him. Seeing her son sitting on that fence, surrounded by the smell of the countryside, she realized that in his silence, he was slowly bringing to life everything he had spent years imagining.

“Does he know?” Lilia asked in a cautious tone. Agatha shook her head, causing Lilia's eyebrows to knit with sorrow.

“I didn't know how to explain. Everything happened so fast that suddenly we were just here,” Agatha admitted, taking a deep breath. “I haven't even had time to fully process it, to be honest. It hasn't fully sunk in. I don't know... it doesn't seem real.” She shook her head, frowning as she tried to gather her thoughts, the clenching in her heart intensifying. “I knew he was sick, but I didn't imagine...”

“No one imagined,” Lilia murmured, shaking her head mournfully. “He always made light of that heart murmur. Rio was the one who was constantly on top of him, but you know how he was; always stubborn as a mule, he never wanted to stop working on the ranch, he loved to get his hands dirty.”

Riandra. Agatha nearly sighed the name. Once again, her thoughts flew to Rio, blurring everything with endless questions. She couldn't stop wondering how she must be feeling. She couldn't begin to measure the pain of that loss; she had never imagined it. She questioned how Rio could possibly race at a time like this, yet a part of her understood perfectly — she needed to breathe.

“How is she?” Agatha asked in a faint voice, and instantly noticed her mother arch an eyebrow, clicking her tongue in sharp disapproval.

“A disaster,” Evanora let out with a weary sigh, her brow now deeply furrowed.

“You are too harsh on her,” Lilia countered, shaking her head. Evanora rolled her eyes while Agatha listened intently. “She always worked incredibly hard on the ranch, she took the lead with her own hands; this town flourished because of her.” Lilia then looked back at Agatha. “But she lost her way a little when he got sick.”

“She's a good girl, I know, a heart of gold,” Evanora conceded, and Agatha was tempted to smile at the way her mother still spoke of Rio as if she were a wild, clumsy child. “But she never managed her emotions well. She’s self-destructive, always a time bomb. Now it's alcohol, and the betting...” She let out a heavy sigh. “I don't know... She loses her way too easily, and nobody can talk any sense into her.”

“Betting?” Agatha asked, raising an eyebrow. Evanora nodded curtly.

“She makes a lot of money from the races; it’s gone to her head.”

“She needs an outlet,” Lilia interjected, predictably, the first to defend her. Her aunt had always taken Riandra under her wing, often saying no one truly understood her. “She has always been haunted by loss, even of people who haven't yet left.” Lilia returned Agatha’s gaze, and Agatha shifted uncomfortably in her chair. “It was a long journey after she lost her mother; it took time for her to find herself, but she eventually flourished. Javier was all she had. Benito rarely showed up; three times a year was a lot, and he always managed things from a distance. It’s easy to put yourself in her shoes; she panicked when Javi got sick; it hit her very hard.”

“You coddle her too much,” Evanora grumbled. “She’s not a child, Lilia. She's a grown woman, and she’s slowly throwing her life away.”

At that, Agatha frowned, a strange feeling tightening her chest, a knot of deep anguish.

Despite her long absence, she knew vaguely that Rio had established herself as a force of nature, so to speak. Before her marriage, in the years she still visited twice a year, Agatha had seen firsthand how Rio developed her tough exterior and commanded respect from everyone in town, especially the farm workers. She operated on her own terms and hated interference. She and Benito had butted heads for years, but despite his complaints, he admitted she had a knack for the business and that her visions had more than tripled the family empire. Javier, for the most part, despite the few reservations that worried him, virtually idolized her. Her godfather had once said Rio was a gift from the heavens, everything he had ever prayed for; she was more than his legacy, she was the very ground where his roots were planted—'Riandra is the root of this family, she is my arms, my legs, my heart, my pillar,' he’d told her.

In truth, Agatha had never worried about Rio in a cautionary sense. Of course, she thought about her more often than she cared to admit — what she was doing, how she would view a situation, what she might say. Some nights, she wondered if Rio still harbored feelings, if she had truly moved on or still dwelled on their past, as Agatha did, if she had made peace with everything they had shared. Despite never making peace with her own choices, Agatha wished happiness and solace for Rio; it was the least she deserved. But she had never, in all those years, worried about what Rio had become or what she might become.

As Lilia pointed out, Rio flourished. Although Agatha lacked details, Riandra had achieved a level of success so profound that Agatha knew about it vaguely, even when she tried not to. She transformed the vineyard into a globally recognized winery. Agatha had lost count of the number of times she found herself drinking Rio's wine at the lavish parties she attended with her wife, often noting the irony: Rio, who never cared for success and only wanted to live on the farm, was now bigger than all those who had once doubted her. Her wines graced the world’s most prestigious cellars, her horses were benchmarks in breeding across continents, and frequently, accompanying Benito to business meetings, Agatha heard the ranch referred to as Riandra's, not the Vidals'. She had built an empire.

Perhaps her own definition of happiness had momentarily blinded her — for years, success had been Agatha's own yardstick. Knowing Rio had reached such an unimaginable height, she viewed her with a mix of nostalgia and admiration, assuming she must be content and fulfilled. Only now, reflecting, did she see how all that, while impressive, might mean absolutely nothing to Rio — all she ever truly wanted was to grow the farm and be close to her family. Perhaps, like Agatha, she was grappling with her own ghosts and inner turmoil.

Her chest felt heavy with these thoughts, and she felt a physical tightness, choked by the void of their separation — a painful knot of no longer knowing her, having no idea who that woman was, yet her heart ached because, deep down, she had never stopped loving her.

“Don’t make that face,” her mother’s abrupt finger-pointing interrupted Agatha’s thoughts. She shook her head, knitting her eyebrows in confusion. “You always had a soft spot for her, and I understand, you share a history, but your life is put together now. Don't stick your nose in Riandra’s mess; you are much better off far away from it.”

“Our history ended a long time ago, mom, and you know that,” Agatha murmured, eyes lowered, trying to contain the sorrow in her voice, but she felt Lilia's gaze on her. “I'm just worried; she means a lot to me, and I can't imagine what all this must be like for her.”

“It’s sad enough watching her lose herself, but if she chooses to, she shouldn't drag anyone down with her,” Evanora grumbled. “It’s bad enough that Lilia keeps enabling her.”

“Everyone goes through bad times, Eva,” Lilia said with a look of compassion. Agatha shrank slightly and nodded in agreement.

“How long has she been going through a bad time?” Evanora countered rhetorically, shifting her gaze between them. “That’s my point. At some point, she’s going to waste away.”

“She needs support, and we are family,” Lilia’s tone, though gentle, was resolute.

“Lilia is right, mom,” Agatha said in a whisper, her face contorted in an uneasy expression as her mother raised an eyebrow. “She is family.”

“She doesn't even want to be with her own family right now, Agatha,” her mother retorted, emphasizing the point. “We are going to bury her father in a few hours, and she is racing at the hippodrome.”

“You talk as if that’s not who she is,” Agatha said with a slight shrug, her eyebrows knitted, almost pleading for understanding. “That's how she dealt with the loss of her mother. Everyone grieves differently; some people cry, others isolate themselves... She races.” Agatha shrugged again. “It's how she clears her head; she has always been like that.”

“She will clear her head,” Lilia said, nodding firmly. “She needs time now.”

“She always needs time...” Evanora muttered with a weary, rolling scoff. “When the fruit hangs too long, it falls off the vine rotten.”

“Eva…” Lilia gently warned, but her mother simply crossed her arms.

Evanora rolled her eyes in exasperation and turned her attention to her grandson, a silent gesture to end the conversation. But Agatha couldn't stop thinking about Rio. Her heart continued to hammer with the weight of what all this meant in light of their shared past, the strained present, and what was beginning to unfold for the future.

***

Twenty-Eight Years Ago

“I’m going to be grounded,” Agatha whispered plaintively as she scrambled down the almond tree whose branches reached her bedroom window. “It's way past midnight. You know we aren’t allowed out after bedtime.”

Rio, standing at the base of the tree, rolled her eyes in an impatient gesture.

“We’re not leaving the ranch, we’re just heading to the stables,” she said, shrugging, a smug little grin suggesting she thought she was supremely clever. “We're still technically at home.”

“That’s not the rule, and you know it,” Agatha countered sulkily, slapping the dirt and dust from her sweatpants the moment her feet touched the ground.

“If you didn't want to come, why did you climb down?” Rio challenged, crossing her arms, her eyebrows raised. Her smile was so full of herself that Agatha’s only response was an exasperated, playful shove. “Scaredy-cat,” Riandra teased, laughing, but immediately laced her fingers through Agatha's.

“What if your dad catches us?” Agatha insisted, her brows furrowed with worry. Rio let out an almost silent scoff.

“What would my dad be doing out of bed at this hour?” Rio retorted, arching a single eyebrow. “No one will know if you just keep that big mouth shut.”

“I’m not going to tell anyone,” Agatha declared defensively, but Rio merely puffed out a skeptical breath. “Hey! I never tell anyone!”

“Then how does Lilia know?” Rio challenged, and Agatha’s face instantly fell with a wave of guilt.

“She asked,” she replied with a defensive shrug, as if the question justified everything. Rio shook her head in mock disappointment. “What? Did you want me to lie? She promised she wouldn’t tell anyone, as long as I told her.”

“If we get grounded, it’s all your fault.”

“It is not!”

“Yes, it is.”

“No, it isn't!” Agatha stomped her foot. Rio laughed, tugging her along the path, their pace quickening.

“It doesn’t matter. It’ll be worth it, I promise,” Rio assured her, momentarily turning to walk backward as she pulled her. Agatha just rolled her eyes, but she couldn’t hide the excited smile playing on her lips. “Come on, hurry up!”

As they hurried through the ranch’s tall grass, Agatha nervously hissed about the pitch black night, grumbling that she couldn't see a foot in front of her. But Rio, with her instinctual nighttime vision, simply held her hand, capable of navigating the path even if she were blindfolded. When they finally reached the stable gates, Agatha was already scratching her shins and muttering complaints. Rio, unable to contain a sly grin, pressed a finger to her lips, signaling absolute quiet.

Riandra had been counting down the days for weeks. One of her father’s mares was pregnant, and every night, Rio would sneak off to the stables to stroke its belly. Richard, the vet, had insisted the foal was still days away, but Rio had ultimately been proven correct. Earlier that afternoon, Agatha's father and the other veterinarians had set up a maternity stall: Dalila was in labor.

Rio had managed to watch the birth, but as soon as the sun went down, her father had made her go home. Naturally, she hadn't stayed put. Now, she was dragging Agatha with her in the middle of the night to see the newborn foal.

“Wait, let her sniff you first,” Rio instructed, her voice soft but warning, as she saw Agatha start to clamber over the stall fence. Agatha turned her blue eyes to Rio attentively and began to climb back down. “Otherwise, she might get spooked.”

Smiling, Rio whistled softly, clicking her tongue in the same rhythm her father used. When the mare turned its muzzle toward her, Rio reached her arms over the fence, stroking it gently, feeling the animal’s mucus on her forearm — a gesture of affection and recognition.

“It’s me,” Rio said quietly, smiling at the mare as she pulled Agatha’s hand forward. “And Agatha. Just us.” She continued to stroke the muzzle, encouraging Agatha to do the same, which she did with a smile. “Come on,” Rio whispered, already starting to scale the fence. Agatha followed instantly, carefully repeating her every step so as not to startle the little filly with any sudden movement.

“My god, just look at him,” Agatha breathed out, her eyebrows furrowed with pure wonder. Rio nodded, a soft smile on her face, and crouched down near the new mother and her baby, who was resting nestled against her flank.

The small foal bore no resemblance to his mother — Dalila, a tall, dappled white mare with powerful muscles and a long face, had given birth to a black foal, black from ear to tail, identical to the stallion who sired him. The baby slept deeply, whinnying softly with slight twitches in his legs, apparently deep in a dream. Agatha couldn't help but smile. He was beautiful, skinny now, but clearly destined to be tall, and his thick coat already shone. He would surely become one of the most stunning horses on the ranch.

“He's beautiful, isn't he?” Rio asked, all gooey, sitting near the mare’s rear and stroking the small foal’s legs. In his deep sleep, he didn't even register the touch.

“Are we going to keep him?” Agatha asked, now settled on the hay, looking at Riandra with a serious expression. Rio, however, shrank back, looking down.

“My dad said no,” she replied dispiritedly, continuing to stroke the foal.

“Why not?”

“He said we already have too many horses; there's no way to manage caring for all of them,” she mumbled sulkily. Agatha pressed her lips together in sympathy. She knew that if it were up to Riandra, every single horse would stay on the ranch, and she wished they could; she knew no one would love them more than Rio. “And Sunny is pregnant too; we are already keeping her foal, he promised me.”

“At least we get to keep hers,” Agatha offered with a shrug.

“She’s my dad’s favorite,” Rio murmured, frowning. “She’s his main mare; I have to keep her foal. All her babies have to stay here, on the ranch. They’re ours.”

“But he has a point.” Seeing Rio's sulky face, Agatha flinched, her eyebrows knitting together. “They need good care; there's no way to manage all of them.”

“Of course there is! Look at the size of this ranch!”

Agatha had to press her lips together to stifle a smile as Rio, frustrated, launched into another grand monologue, listing her countless justifications for raising every horse they could possibly have — it was always this way. Rio was passionately in love with every one of them, recognizing each by name even from a distance. Amid a horde of black horses, she could instantly tell who was who, and sometimes Agatha wondered if Rio loved them more than her own family.

Everyone said Rio was wary, quiet, and suspicious, but Agatha never saw her that way. On the contrary, when they were together, Rio would talk up a storm, especially around the horses. She was full of smiles and open affection. Agatha wondered how anyone could think Rio was anything other than the sweetest person in the entire world. She wished everyone could see her as she did — the most incredible person she knew, her absolute favorite.

She treasured all moments like this, just the two of them, with Rio going on and on — grumbling, debating, pouring out all her ideas until her cheeks were flushed from talking so fast. She loved Rio with all her heart: the way she expressed herself, her huge eyes, her know-it-all confidence, the way she lifted her chin, certain that, despite any disagreement, she was always right. Rio was brave and expressive, and Agatha always wanted to be more like her, feeling lucky that, most of the time, she had her all to herself. And sometimes, watching those romance movies with the heroes living happily ever after, she wished that when they grew up, it would be the same for them — the two of them, together forever.

“You’ll see, when I grow up, we are going to keep every single horse on the ranch,” she finished her monologue, utterly resolute, then let out a resigned huff that made Agatha chuckle softly.

“And how are you going to take care of all of them?” she asked with a hint of disbelief that made Rio roll her eyes before laying her head down in Agatha’s lap.

“I don’t know, but you’ll see,” Rio stated with intense conviction. “The ranch will grow, the town will grow, and they’ll all fit here. I'm going to keep them all, and everyone is going to talk about me, about my horses. I’m going to be the greatest, fastest rider of all time, too.”

“You know you can’t race them.”

“That’s what my dad says, but he doesn’t understand,” she let out with a touch of frustration. Agatha looked down at her attentively, stroking her hair. “They like to run, too.”

“But most people who race don’t care about the horses, you know.”

“But I’m different, because I care,” she replied with a shrug. “When I’m running, I feel like a little bird, you know? It's like I'm flying, and everything gets easy. I can't explain it... Sometimes, I don't know what to say, and I don't seem to know what to think either; it’s strange, it makes me anxious... But when I'm running, Agatha, I swear, everything disappears, the whole world goes silent, and I can finally breathe.”

Agatha didn’t understand that feeling; she didn't struggle much with her own thoughts or feelings, but she knew Rio did — she got easily distressed, sometimes couldn’t speak clearly, or got confused when trying to express herself. She was often frustrated, and Agatha didn’t understand why, but she tried. It was as if Rio had a cyclone inside her head, and the internal storm only halted when she was mounted on a horse, running.

“There’s nothing in the world I want to be. I just want to run. I wish my dad understood,” she murmured in a whisper. Agatha sighed, her fingers gently stroking Rio’s face.

“He’ll understand sooner or later; he loves you too much not to,” she offered, but Rio shrugged.

“And you?” she asked, raising an eyebrow. Agatha knit hers in confusion. “What do you want to be when you grow up?”

In truth, Agatha didn't know. She wasn't like Rio. Although she loved the ranch and the animals, she couldn't picture herself doing all that work under the sun, with mud-caked boots — every time she helped her father, she counted the minutes until she could go back inside. She was sure she didn't want to be a veterinarian, and botany didn't hold much appeal. The fact was, she still didn't quite know what she wanted to be; she knew better what she didn't want.

“I don’t know,” she replied honestly, looking into her favorite brown eyes.

“You like to read; you could do that,” Rio said, smiling. Agatha laughed, shaking her head.

“I can’t just read books for the rest of my life, Rio. That’s not a job.”

“You could write, then.” At this, Agatha gave a hesitant nod.

“Maybe, I don’t know.”

“You can be my wife,” Rio said, her lips curved into a warm smile. Agatha ended up smiling too, feeling a strange flush on her cheeks.

“Do you think we’ll get married when we grow up? You and me?”

“I want to stay with you forever; I guess we’ll have to get married. That’s what people who want to be together forever do, right?” Her tone was one of genuine questioning. Agatha smiled and nodded. She wanted that too.

***

Present Day

Agatha hadn't explicitly mentioned her plan to stay. In fact, she hadn't said anything at all, so she couldn't fault her mother for not being prepared for an extended visit. Given she barely came home, staying longer than a weekend was undoubtedly the last thing Evanora expected. Agatha knew she was welcome for as long as she wished, but she also knew she would eventually have to explain herself, and that was the last thing she wanted to do right now. She longed to simply walk away from everything — no questions, no explanations — but understood that life wasn't so simple; she would have to face the music sooner or later.

Speaking the truth would make things easier — or at least, more comfortable. Javier had built two new houses on the ranch, side by side. One was a wedding gift, a subtle reminder that no matter what happened, she would always have a place here, a home of her own whenever she returned. Of course, she and Tessa had never even seen the house; her wife wouldn't set foot in Darby under any circumstances. Now, though, Agatha could simply ask for the keys and take shelter there with Nicholas, escaping the suffocating atmosphere of her childhood bedroom. That room was still stuffed with college books and countless photos documenting her trips and moments with Tessa over the years — everything left precisely as it was, untouched.

With every familiar detail she noticed, the more suffocated she felt, as if her younger self was staring at her, condemning her for the unhappiness she had willfully created. The room was the physical embodiment of everything she desperately wanted to leave behind. Perhaps speaking would be easier — her mother would understand — but she couldn't. The lump in her throat was nearly palpable, constricting her as if it were thorny vines. She barely knew what words to use, how to give voice to the truth, to let those ghosts become real. Naming them, acknowledging their existence, and admitting that, yes, she was suffering and had been suffering for so long that she no longer knew what she was doing with her life.

If it weren't for Nicholas, she wouldn't have the courage to attempt a change. A voice inside her — a thick, bitter mist dwelling deep in her core — always convinced her that she was only reaping what she had sown.

She felt she deserved this. Tessa wasn't inherently monstrous; Agatha was the one who had shaped her, the architect of her own circumstances. She had never known how to let go, how to truly choose, always caught between the past and the future. She had idealized her future so intensely that she got lost in the projection — caught between what she wanted and what she could have, she lost herself trying to have it all. Now, she was nothing — just a weary body enduring, striving to keep going.

But every time she looked at her son, seeing him wasting away in apathy, growing indifferent, his gaze empty and joyless, she knew she had to change — she had to act. She could endure her own suffering; she no longer cared about herself. But Nicholas, no. Her son deserved a life without fear, restrictions, or reprimands. All she wanted was his happiness, and she knew staying in California would guarantee the opposite. She may have deserved the life Tessa had created, but Nicholas did not.

The decision was impulsive, yet not unconsidered — in truth, it was something she had wanted and should have done years ago, long before the situation reached its current extreme. Still, it was terrifying. By leaving the divorce papers on the table and walking out with almost everything she could carry, she had made real what had only ever existed behind closed doors. She was finally admitting failure, acknowledging her mistakes, and confessing the suffering she had fiercely hidden for years. She was closing the book on her entire life to attempt a new story — with nothing of her old world remaining, just her and her son. Everything else was rubble, and perhaps that’s why she wasn’t ready to face it or put it into words — Agatha had just demolished an entire life.

Furthermore, she had virtually nothing. In a literal sense, she lacked money and property, her career had been abandoned long ago, and worst of all, she had no plan. That was the most terrifying part. She knew she had done what was necessary, but now she faced the utter emptiness of just herself. Would she stay on the ranch? What would she do next? She didn't know. She felt she needed, at the very least, to clear her head before revealing everything. She needed a plan, time to think and breathe — above all, she needed time. Time not just to rebuild, but to find and heal herself, and accept the truth instead of fearing it.

“I’m so sorry, dear.” Lilia’s voice broke through her paralysis, and Agatha turned to her, her eyebrows furrowed. “Your mother didn’t know—” Lilia shook her head. “Doesn't know, actually, how long you'll be staying, so she just tidied up the room.”

“I know,” Agatha murmured, exhaling heavily. “To be honest, Lilia, I don’t know either.”

“You don’t need to worry about that right now,” her aunt said gently, sitting beside her on the single bed that seemed impossibly larger than she remembered. “You need time to process everything; this isn’t the moment to worry about the long term.”

“But I need to know eventually,” Agatha sighed. “I can’t just keep staying here like nothing is happening. Tessa is already calling non-stop and—”

“And you won’t answer,” Lilia interrupted, her voice sweet yet firm. “It’s not the time.”

“I don’t know what she’s capable of.”

“She won't set foot on the ranch, that much is certain,” her aunt muttered with a hint of a smile. Agatha let out a small breath. “She doesn’t have the guts to pursue you here. Rio would send her packing in two seconds.” Despite the faint smile on Lilia’s lips, Agatha buried her face in her hands.

“That’s another thing I have to face,” Agatha murmured tiredly. “Riandra.”

“You yourself said she’s family and—”

“Yes, Lilia, to my mother,” she emphasized with exasperation. “What did you expect me to say? That I’m practically going into spontaneous combustion at the thought of speaking to her after thirteen years?!” Agatha laughed nervously, shaking her head. “You should have seen her face when she saw me; I—”

“You saw each other?!” Lilia’s eyes widened. Agatha took a deep breath.

“I stopped at the hippodrome before coming here.” Lilia opened her mouth to interrupt, but Agatha didn’t stop. “I didn’t know she was racing; I wasn't thinking. Nicholas was so excited about the horses, and I hadn’t seen him like that in ages. I just wanted to hold onto that moment. I didn't know she would be there, and then—” She stammered, her words rushing. “She saw me. She saw me, and she looked like I was a ghost, a demon, I don’t know.”

Lilia gave a small, warm chuckle, shaking her head as she put an arm around Agatha’s shoulders, pulling her into an affectionate squeeze.

“Thirteen years, Agatha. She wasn't expecting to see you. But she would never see you as a demon, you know that. She loves you, nena; she always has.” As Lilia finished, Agatha pulled away from the hug, shaking her head vehemently.

“Lilia, you can't romanticize what we had anymore; that doesn't exist,” she insisted, gesticulating wildly, reflecting the whirlwind of emotions in her chest. Her aunt stared back, completely unimpressed. “We aren't children anymore, two confused teenagers. That ended years ago. She doesn't—” Agatha stopped abruptly, inhaling deeply, and squeezed her eyes shut. “We aren't part of each other's lives anymore; we don't know each other.”

“Are you trying to convince me or yourself?” Lilia asked, and Agatha responded with a strained, humorless laugh.

What no one seemed to grasp was the monumental barrier standing between her and Riandra — a rampart that Agatha herself had meticulously built and maintained. Of course, she still loved Rio; she always had. Now, she understood the depth of her foolishness in ever thinking she could stop. Yet, she knew that love could not possibly be returned, not even in a platonic or friendly way. With her unfortunate gift for destroying everything she touched, she had ruined that connection, too — everything they had shared.

Before her marriage, every trip back to Darby was a trip back to Rio. She couldn't help it. Seeing her always made it impossible to ignore her feelings; the longing and desire were simply overwhelming, and a part of her refused to let go. Rio had let her go — she did so from the very beginning — but Agatha couldn't release her. For years, she kept Rio trapped in a relentless tug-of-war, leaving only to return and pull her back with full force. No one knew the truth: the empty promises to work things out, the fights that devolved into nights spent huddled in the stables, the drunken phone calls, and the whispered vows of love. No one knew that Rio had begged her to leave for good because Agatha, in her cowardice, kept holding on, wanting everything even while knowing she couldn't have it.

Agatha had broken Rio in an irreversible way. There was no fixing it, and no justification for her actions other than her own selfishness.

“It’s not that simple, Lilia,” Agatha finally managed, letting out a long sigh. “And as I said, we aren't children anymore. I’m not a part of her life anymore, and I genuinely think it's better and easier this way.”

“You will always be my children, nena,” Lilia said affectionately, stroking her hair. “What happened is irrelevant; she is family. And I am certain she will support you if you need it. I know how important she is to you.”

“I already have so much to worry about; I don't need to try to reconcile with the past. The present is already heavy enough.”

“That’s where you’re mistaken, my love,” Lilia countered. Agatha looked back at her, her eyebrows furrowed, practically begging for some wisdom, a single spark of hope that everything would be alright. “You need to make peace with yourself; that is the essential first step to moving on. You cannot move forward while carrying everything you left behind.” Agatha let out a frustrated grunt. Lilia looked at her with the same steady gaze she had when Agatha was ten years old. “You need to heal, Agatha.”

“I don’t know how to do that,” she whispered, her voice choked. Tears blurred her vision, and she finally slumped onto her aunt’s shoulder, who held her close.

“You just need time, my love, that is all,” Lilia murmured, stroking her back and resting her head against hers. “I promise everything will fall into place, but for now, you need time. You need to clear your head. One step at a time, and I will be here with you. You are home, nena. You are home and you are safe. We will figure out the rest.”

In that moment, Agatha could only think of all the time she had thrown away, the years of lies she had constructed and maintained. She desperately wanted to believe that everything would work out and that her personal demons would truly be left behind. But it was hard — she felt inextricably bound to the pandemonium, convinced she, too, would end up in ruins. But, god, she truly wanted to find herself again, to completely remake who she was.

She had no idea how to begin, but she knew she had to try — and she would, for herself and for Nicholas.

Chapter End Notes
