Chapter 9: words

Chapter Summary

agatha has a lot to unpack

Chapter Notes

i know its been intense aaaaand its about to get worse lol but it will be worth it, i promise. thanks for the comments and kudos so far, hope you like this one

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"I wait for the morning tide, the oceans to rise, but I lay here broken too heavy to float, I'm an emotionless wreck. So I tell myself this lie, I'm not scared to die, make molehills of mountais, there's gold in this ground but I ain't found it yet" — Let The Grass Grow, Ruel

Agatha always believed language held the world's greatest fascination, a belief that fueled her long-held dream of becoming a writer. It was astounding to her how this intricate code, born from human ingenuity and circulating globally, possessed the power to capture and convey all existence, from the mundane reality to the wildest fantasy. Her childhood was defined by countless hours lost in books, devouring everything from predictable romances to weighty russian classics. Even now, no book failed to enthrall her. Her captivation wasn't with the mere act of writing, but with the profound meaning it translated. While most people saw translation as a simple bridge between two different languages, Agatha viewed language itself as the ultimate act of translation, the very medium by which concepts and feelings took material form.

Yet, despite her profound love for words — and her fluency in over five languages — she couldn't summon a single term to contain the storm of emotions currently raging in her chest. She was grappling with a sensation beyond translation. Even drawing on the full palette of her knowledge, nothing felt adequate. It wasn't just bittersweet, nor was it simply anguish. It was a complex blend of nostalgia, sorrow, and comfort, mixed with confusion and a sharp, painful prick that threatened to make her eyes brim over. A sort of tender ache that simultaneously tormented and comforted her.

The root of this turmoil was the chilling realization that no vocabulary could truly capture the feeling of watching an entire life drain away — not due to a lack of choices or opportunities, but because the choices had been entirely her own.

The deep fascination with words that once consumed her eventually morphed into a quest for grander ideals, a relentless pursuit of the new: new sensations, new vocabulary, new experiences to stack upon all those she had already lived. This insatiable hunger for life, even when the ultimate purpose remained unclear, led her astray. Sacrificing her writing, her family, and her home in pursuit of a hazy ambition, she transformed into someone she barely recognized, a mere figurehead — like a doll left in a box — vulnerable to idealized standards that she now recognized as utterly ridiculous.

It was a bitter pill to swallow, recognizing after all this time that Rio had been right from the beginning. Agatha had judged Rio's perceived lack of ambition, yet Rio always knew precisely what she wanted. Riandra never chased abstract ideals or indistinct dreams that only sparkled in her mind's eye; she wanted to be with her father, to see the ranch thrive, to ride her horses — and she never allowed herself to be pulled away by an unfamiliar vision of life. Though people were quick to label her a lazy good-for-nothing, there Rio stood, achieving tremendous success in everything she undertook, while Agatha had nothing left but her son — a treasure, at least, that no one could take away.

As her mother quietly slipped away to the Vidal mansion to assist with the wake arrangements, Agatha moved through the halls of her own house. She paused at every detail, absorbing the photographs, the changes, the subtle etchings of time, yet found herself still speechless. All she could manage was the enormous, looming presence in her chest, an emotion that felt capable of devouring her whole.

When her mother announced she had to leave to help the kids — a term that almost brought a smile to Agatha’s face, given how her mother still saw them all as children, even though Rio was a grown woman and so was Benito — Agatha seized the chance to unload the car. She hastily removed all the suitcases and belongings she had brought, trying to move quickly so her mother wouldn't pry too much about the volume of her luggage. She knew a conversation was due, but not yet; she needed a moment to breathe.

Lilia, as always, helped her without complaint, doing her best to lighten the mood with optimistic comments that, at the moment, felt like pure wishful thinking. Still, Agatha was grateful. This simple act of support was the only solace in her chaos, the anchor of still having a home, even if it no longer felt familiar.

"That's not going to fit in your room," Lilia remarked, slightly winded, as she assessed the pile of suitcases, hands on her hips and brow furrowed in search of a solution.

"I really shouldn't have brought all this," Agatha sighed regretfully, but her aunt merely gave her shoulder an affectionate squeeze.

"There's always the basement," Lilia offered with a knowing smile. This time, Agatha couldn't suppress a small, weak laugh, shaking her head.

"I am not going down there," she stated, still chuckling.

"It's not so bad anymore, you know? Riandra came by the other day and sorted the whole thing out; she spent the entire day tidying and refused any help."

There she was again, a permanent fixture in Agatha’s life. Agatha suppressed a sigh. Avoiding Rio was easy from thousands of miles away, but here, she knew it was impossible. Rio was an undeniable part of her life; she was family. A wave of self-reproach washed over Agatha. It was humiliating to realize that while she had been living her extravagant life in California, it was Rio who had been looking after her family.

"Of course she helped," Agatha muttered with a heavy, grudging sigh.

"You need to get used to it, dear," Lilia cautioned gently, her voice retaining its constant warmth.

"I know, we've discussed this," Agatha replied, rolling her eyes and sighing once more. "But it's still hard. I knew it wouldn't be easy, especially with everything happening, but... it's overwhelming, and it hurts me too; the pain has never truly gone away."

"You've changed, nena, she's changed too, but ultimately, you two are still exactly the same," Agatha rolled her eyes, and Lilia laughed softly. "It's her place, her farm," Lilia added with a shrug, which Agatha conceded with a reluctant nod. "And whether you believe it or not, she still loves you very much."

"Oh, here you go again, romanticizing things between us..."

"I am not," Lilia countered, pointing her index finger at Agatha. "I’m not speaking in that way, but you both mean a lot to each other, you always have and always will. Just as you were always important to your godfather" And the mere mention of him made Agatha’s eyes well up. "and she has always been important to our family, even if your mother still grumbles; she adores that girl."

"Ever since we broke up, she’s always picked on her. Even after my wedding, that never changed." Agatha rolled her eyes once more, leaning her body against the hallway wall for support.

"You’ll see, they’re like cat and mouse," Lilia chuckled. "But every evening, Rio shows up with fresh fruit, and your mother just melts with worry." Agatha managed a faint smile; she could easily visualize the scene.

Even after her mother started criticizing Rio, the girl never let go of that almost mother-daughter bond. She worried about Agatha's mother and cared for her, and Agatha knew Rio had been a constant presence after her father’s death — more present than Agatha herself. Rio, with her perpetually big heart — it was sweet, but right now, it made Agatha feel worthless.

Agatha intensely disliked this line of thought. For years, she had successfully sealed off her past, burying those memories in a dark, forgotten corner of her mind. But here, she was cornered; avoidance was impossible. Even the familiar smell of the grass and the wet earth brought Riandra to mind, making her feel omnipresent.

It was an ugly emotion, but Agatha inexplicably harbored resentment toward Rio. She carried a tangled mess of unresolved feelings and a sharp bitterness, knowing that Rio had never once yielded to despair. Agatha was the one who instigated the breakup, left, chased her dreams, and ultimately created her own downfall, while Rio had forged ahead, turning obstacles into success — making a killing — the world’s most profitable lemonade, apparently, given that she was now swimming in wealth earned through sheer labor, having multiplied an already immense family fortune.

Agatha felt she had made every wrong turn, and the regret was acute — even while she genuinely felt happy that Rio had found peace and the success she always wanted. The sting was that Riandra had fully fine without her, yet Agatha was haunted by regret over every single path she had taken.

"She's worried about Rio," Lilia continued, pulling Agatha out of her introspection. "We all are, but she’s a tough nut to crack."

"What exactly is going on with her?" Agatha asked finally, careful not to betray the small flicker of concern forming in her own chest.

"Life, nena. Everyone copes differently."

"Well, thank you for that detailed explanation," Agatha said, her false smile practically dripping with sarcasm.

"We told you, she’s going through a rough patch, but I have faith she'll pull through; she just needs time."

"She always needs time," Agatha grumbled, earning a sharp look from her aunt, which made her throw up her hands in surrender.

"I know this is difficult for you, but it’s not your place to judge," Lilia warned. Agatha swallowed hard. "You need time too right now, and no one here is judging you."

The silence hung between them. Agatha bit her cheek and reluctantly nodded her agreement.

"Basement, then?" she asked, feigning enthusiasm. Her aunt looked satisfied and nodded.

"Basement it is."

The house's basement was vast, encompassing most of the subterranean space, nearly the size of the whole house. For years, it had been the designated place for forgotten farm clutter. Agatha couldn't recall the last time she'd ventured down there, and the thought was bitter because it brought back painful memories.

When she and Rio were children, the basement was their secret lair. Surrounded by junk, they spent hours there, making up elaborate scenarios, drawing on the walls, and scattering toys. As they grew older, the scribbles were replaced by school notes; books and workbooks mingled with the forgotten things. Despite the chaos, it was their sanctuary. The whole farm was theirs, but the basement felt different — it was their exclusive refuge. Agatha mentally rolled her eyes at herself, cheeks flushing as she reluctantly remembered everything they’d done down there, hidden from sight during those long hours supposedly spent studying.

Given how her life was now twisting, determined to force her to face the past, it was almost inevitable that she’d end up in the basement first. The universe, truly, was teaching her a harsh lesson.

"See?" Lilia said with a smug grin as they reached the bottom of the stairs, both breathing heavily from carrying the bags. "It looks charming, doesn't it?"

Agatha had to steady herself on the railing, dumbfounded, unable to process the sight.

It bore no resemblance to the old basement. It was completely transformed. Yes, all the old trinkets were still there, but it looked less like a storage pit and more like an actual room — Riandra's meticulous touch was obvious. With every passing minute, Agatha felt more ambushed by her presence.

The former mess was now meticulously organized. Agatha almost let out a nervous laugh. Rio had not just cleaned the basement; she had completely rehabilitated it, turning it into a warm, inviting haven.

In the corner stood her parents' old bed, now illuminated by soft, cozy lights, with the familiar rug from her childhood room laid beneath it, carving out a distinct sleeping space. Boxes served as makeshift nightstands, and next to them, her teenage desk stood near a worn sofa and armchairs, which surrounded yet more boxes of old curiosities repurposed as a coffee table. Shelves and bookcases were packed tight with belongings and farm relics, and even her childhood bicycles were neatly lined up in the background, almost like deliberate decor. Rio hadn't just tidied the space; she had arranged all those miscellaneous items so they not only made sense but harmonized into an inviting little room. It felt like a meticulously arranged dollhouse. The walls were painted a gentle lilac, adorned with old photographs in antique frames. Rio had thought of everything. Agatha felt diminished — Rio had transformed the basement into the exact space they had envisioned when they were young girls.

The room, while feeling entirely new, simultaneously offered a complete, physical timeline of her life.

She had been back for less than four hours, and every single moment was overshadowed by Riandra's ever-present influence: from Nicky’s fascination with her horse to her mother’s complaints, from Lilia’s concern to the old photos in the halls, to this cursed basement, and finally, to her own shattered emotional state. She yearned for a minute of peace, but it seemed the universe was having its fun with her.

"I told you," Lilia said, a wide smile spreading across her face as she noticed Agatha’s stunned immobility.

"It's perfect," Agatha admitted, her eyebrows softening with affection. She immediately began to drag her bags, trying to shield herself from her aunt's view, desperate to hide the fact that her eyes were already brimming with tears.

But Lilia possessed uncanny insight and knew her niece too well. She followed Agatha, watching affectionately as Agatha finally collapsed onto the sofa, letting her head drop back, eyes tightly shut.

"What is it, nena?" Lilia asked, her tone gentle and full of empathy. The sound of her childhood nickname only twisted Agatha's lips into a bitter smile; even that simple word felt painful now.

It was everything, all at once. The catastrophic, years-long relationship with Tessa, sustained only by pride and dependence. Her professional career in ruins. The agonizing admission that she had been utterly foolish to sacrifice everything — her dreams, her achievements, her sense of self — all because of crippling guilt over Tessa. It was the divorce and the chilling certainty that Tessa would seek retribution. It was Nicholas: the sadness of rarely seeing him smile or hearing him speak, the deep anxiety about his development, and the current terror of not being able to afford the specialized treatments she had fought her wife to acknowledge for years. It was coming home with absolutely nothing and a child dependent on her, with no roadmap for the future. It was the recent, devastating loss of her godfather, who had always been a father figure to her, a grandfather to Nicholas, and who had consistently ensured she felt connected to her family, even from a distance. And finally, it was Rio.

Rio, and the entirety of her past significance: the crushing weight of their history, the ghost of their unlived future, and the mutual pain they had inflicted. Rio, and the consuming guilt over every word spoken and every action taken. Rio, with her tender heart, who forgave her unfailingly, even when forgiveness was unwarranted. Rio, who had fought for their relationship until Agatha herself had to beg her to leave. Rio, and that distinct pang in her chest whenever she thought of her, the love she abandoned, the sharp agony of closing her eyes and wishing things had been different, and the profound sorrow that she would never know if happiness was possible, because the past was sealed.

"I don't want to keep repeating myself," Agatha mumbled, keeping her eyes shut to hide the wetness, swallowing past the painful lump in her throat. "It's just so difficult to be back here."

"I know, honey," Lilia said softly, her expression sad as she stroked Agatha's arm. Agatha let out a sigh of deep skepticism, but Lilia merely affirmed her own words. "We'll figure it out."

Agatha weakly shook her head, leaning back into the faded yellow sofa, letting her gaze drift over every detail of the room. It was agonizing, yet simultaneously offered a strange sense of belonging — a true double-edged sword.

“Can I stay here?" she asked in a bare whisper. Lilia tilted her head, slightly confused. "I can't stay in my old room." That room, like the rest of the house, held too many photos of her and Tessa, chronicling their college days and the slow unraveling of their marriage. "Besides, Nicholas needs his own space; I don't want to disrupt that."

"Whatever you need, dear," Lilia assured her, letting out a puff of air, now looking slightly invigorated. She clapped her hands on her legs, surveying the room. "It's a nice little nook," she said with a nod. "Though we should probably replace this furniture," she added, laughing softly and patting the sofa. Agatha managed a more relaxed sigh. "But we can deal with that later."

"Thank you."

"I love you, nena. I’ll be with you every step of the way."

It didn't take long for them to arrange all of Agatha’s things in her new temporary bedroom. Amidst soft laughter over every rediscovered antique as they unpacked boxes to create improvised shelving for her clothes, they organized her belongings: clothing, the books she used for work, her laptop, her succulents, and framed photos of Nicholas, her father, and Javier. It wasn't much, and far from perfect, but it was good, and that was enough.

Checking the time, they realized they had little time left before the wake. In a brief glance exchanged with Lilia, Agatha knew exactly what she needed to do. Her chest tightened immediately.

With a heavy sigh, Agatha climbed the wooden stairs leading to the small porch door, and found him quickly — her little prince. She felt Lilia's hug settle around her shoulders and rested her head on hers as she watched him gripping the fence, arms outstretched, frantically trying to catch the horses' attention. None seemed willing to give him a moment of their time. Agatha managed a faint smile. It was the first time in ages she had seen him so relaxed, so engaged, climbing back and forth across the fence as the wind tousled his brown curls.

All she truly wanted now was to be a good mother; everything else could wait.

With bare feet, something she hadn't done in years, Agatha walked slowly toward her son, taking in the entire atmosphere just as he was. She felt the soft dirt beneath her feet, the tall grass brushing her legs, and the summer-scented air. Ironically, the day was beautiful, and she felt the warmth seep into her bones. She paused just before the fence, closing her eyes and turning her face toward the sun, letting herself absorb it all; this entire place — her home.

When she opened her eyes, seeing him there, clicking his tongue to call the horses, Agatha felt a surge of warmth in her heart — she longed for him to find happiness here, just as she once had.

"Hey, kiddo," she said softly, careful not to startle him. Still clinging to the fence, he turned his face toward her. He was calmer than she'd ever seen him, without the usual, almost permanent furrow of concentration between his eyebrows; in fact, there was a faint hint of a smile on his face. "They don't want to play right now, do they?"

Nicholas pouted but then shrugged, content simply to watch them. Agatha smiled, though faintly, and approached him cautiously. He didn't pull away, as he often did, and she ran her fingers gently through his hair.

"They're distracted right now; they prefer to play in the morning," she explained, and he turned his face fully toward her, his large eyes fixed on her. "We can try playing with them tomorrow, if you like."

He raised his eyebrows in clear inquiry, and Agatha let out a long, slow breath.

"I know you don't really understand everything that's happening, and I apologize for that," she began. He remained silent but watched her intently. "But I wasn't happy at our house." He nodded, and it was Agatha's turn to frown, her heart sinking with the knowledge that, yes, he knew she was suffering. "I need to stay here for a while, with my mother."

Nicholas, still watching her, nodded as if he understood, and Agatha smiled, stroking his cheek; he affectionately leaned his head into her hand.

"Do you want to stay here with me? On the farm?" she asked in a bare whisper, and he immediately lifted his head, nodding fiercely, which finally brought a real smile to her face. "Come here," she prompted, and he turned, letting her gather him into her arms.

He was growing big and was a bit heavy to hold now, but that didn't stop her from pulling him tight against her chest, where she sighed with relief as his head settled on her shoulder. Agatha couldn't even remember the last time they had a moment like that.

In silence, she carried him to the porch and sat down on the floor. Nicholas sat nestled between her legs, his back resting against her chest, his head tilted back so his brown eyes were fixed on her face, intently watching her.

"You know your mother and I argue sometimes." He nodded. "And that makes me very unhappy. When we spend too much time sad instead of happy, we need... a break." She took a deep breath under his unblinking gaze. "Your mother and I, we won't... We won't be together anymore, and that's why I don't want to go back home."

He remained perfectly still, not moving a single muscle, but his intense gaze told her he understood.

"That's why we need to stay here for a while." He nodded in an almost imperceptible movement, and Agatha continued stroking his hair. "But that's not the only thing I needed to talk to you about." Now he raised his eyebrows with curiosity. Agatha's heart sank; she struggled to find the right words, desperate not to cause him pain.

Agatha shook her head, carefully selecting her words while trying to suppress the whirlwind of emotion churning within her.

"Do you remember the stories I told you about grandpa Richard?" Nicholas nodded, pointing toward the sky. Agatha had to look away from him to continue speaking. "Yes, he went to live in the sky because he lived here with us for a very long time, and it was his turn to live up there, with the sun and the stars." She paused, then met his favorite little brown eyes again; they were clearly trying to grasp her meaning. "You know he and Uncle Javi were the best of friends, right?" He nodded. "Your uncle told you lots of stories about their adventures, remember?" He confirmed this, and Agatha sighed, nodding to herself. "Uncle Javi missed him so much that he went to the sky too, so they could have more adventures together."

As his brow furrowed, his eyebrows knitting together in a troubled expression mixing sadness and incomprehension, she felt something shatter inside her. The conversation was already difficult, but seeing it affect her dearest love made it exponentially worse.

Agatha watched him struggle to swallow, his face crumpling further, his eyes glistening, his lower lip trembling, and his cheeks beginning to flush red. She knew that look all too well:

"No, no, no, my love." She held his face gently, using her thumbs to wipe away the first tentative tears that escaped his brown eyes. "It's alright, I promise, he's happy." But Nicholas shook his head forcefully, pointing to himself, and Agatha let out a painful gasp, her own eyes swimming. "I know he didn't say goodbye, but he didn't have time." He sniffled, his lips pouting, shaking his head, and she held him tightly against her, shutting her eyes hard. "He loves you so much, my love, and he will always be thinking of you."

***

Agatha was unfamiliar with the city cemetery; it was one of the few, if not the only place, she had never had cause to visit. Now, she recognized how fortunate she had been to spend so much of her life without setting foot there. It wasn't unattractive; quite the opposite, it was a wide, open area dotted with blossoming trees. Yet, the scattered gravestones and gazebos made her body rigid with tension with every step, her heart constricting as if clamped by a vise.

The grounds were predictably crowded with mourners carrying ribbons and flowers. Throughout her life, Agatha had never known anyone as universally loved and admired as her godfather. He had given everything to that town — creating employment, funding housing, investing in various ventures, and tirelessly helping those in need. There was genuinely no one who disliked him. Javier had served as a father figure to most people she knew; he was essentially the town's benefactor, yet he ensured everyone felt like an essential part of the community.

With her lips pressed tight, she fought back tears as memories flashed across her mind while she approached the large Vidal family gazebo. She recalled her godfather's robust laugh, his infectious smile, and his gentle way of speaking; the playful way he would mess up her hair even after she was an adult, and the powerful bear hugs that always lifted her off the ground. He had been a father to her and a grandfather to Nicholas, who now clung tight to her side, his small hand holding hers in an almost painful grip.

Javier had stepped up after her father’s death, providing for her mother when she couldn't work the fields, and remaining a constant presence even when Agatha was in California. He was rough around the edges, but in the best way. Agatha almost smiled faintly, remembering his blunt disdain for Californian ostentatiousness, his complaints about the abundance of buildings, the lack of open land, and his grumbling about the frantic pace of the people. Yet, she had never met anyone more optimistic or good-natured — he always had a smile to share. He was pure goodness, the kind rarely encountered today. He had a boundless heart, and she knew his absence would wound her deeply for years, just as her father's still did.

As she neared the cluster of people, she spotted Benito sitting apart on a bench, his face buried in his hands. Kate was beside him, her head resting on his shoulder while she gently stroked his back for comfort. Agatha had missed them both and wished they could reunite under any other circumstances.

Before she could stop him, Nicholas pulled his hand free and darted toward Benito. Seeing the boy, Benito managed a faint smile, opening his arms to embrace him tightly.

"Hey, frijolito," he murmured, his voice thick with emotion, resting his head against Nicholas's. "I missed you."

"I'm so sorry," Kate said compassionately, her lips pressed together, as she stood to hug Agatha. Agatha allowed herself to relax into Kate’s arms, taking a deep breath and forcing back her tears. "I know this is incredibly hard for you, too."

"You have no idea," Agatha replied, burying her face further into Kate's shoulder.

"Riandra!" Agatha froze as she heard her own son shout the name. As if hit by an electrical shock, she spun around, her blue eyes wide, seeing Nicholas still in Benito's arms, pointing toward the gazebo, just as he had done at the racetrack.

There she stood, at the top of the gazebo steps, instantly commanding attention among the crowd. Agatha’s entire body began to tremble, not just from seeing her, but from hearing her name spoken by her own son. How in the world did he know who she was?

A rush of conflicting feelings slammed into her like an adrenaline spike — Nicholas speaking after months of silence, hearing that name, seeing Rio there: stoic, her eyes distant, her face utterly drained of emotion. Agatha felt a physical blow, as if she had been sucker-punched. She was catatonic, frozen, fixated on Rio's remote presence. Language had failed her again; there was no word to describe the discordant clamor inside her, as if all her warring emotions were screaming in her head.

How was it possible? To look at someone she had known more intimately than anyone for years and now perceive her as a total stranger. She did not recognize this Rio, this woman with harsh features, her expression empty. There was no trace of her past tenderness; she seemed fossilized, like a body devoid of a soul. Agatha didn't know this woman and wondered if anyone could recognize her, or if this coldness was who she was now; a piece of stone.

"Yes, that's her," Benito's voice cut through her trance, instantly capturing her gaze. She watched him gently lower Nicholas's hand in a silent warning to stop pointing, though the boy was bouncing with excitement in his arms. "That's the Riandra from Uncle Javi's stories."

Nicholas, still squirming, looked at Benito as if asking permission, but Benito shook his head:

"Now is not the right time," he said softly, and finally had to put Nicholas down, unable to manage the boy's escalating excitement and frustration.

"Nicholas!" Agatha called out sharply, but it was useless; he took off running. Before Agatha could react, Kate sprinted after him, quickly picking him up and trying to subdue his burst of energy.

Agatha moved to help, but Kate just shook her head, indicating that everything was fine —  and she knew it was; Kate had always been wonderful with him; she knew exactly how to distract him.

When Benito attended college in California, he stayed with Agatha for years, becoming an ingrained part of her daily life. Even after moving into his own place — just a house on the same street — he remained a fixture. He was always at her home, present for all of Nicholas's major milestones, and even Tessa, with all her aversion to hillbilly ways, regarded him as family. He was a brother to Agatha, just as Kate had been since she and Benny became inseparable.

They knew everything, just as she knew everything, too.

"My bad," Benito said, his tone heavy with exhaustion, as he sank back onto the bench, breathing hard. "I never thought he would actually recognize her."

Agatha’s heart hammered. She felt utterly rooted to the spot, stunned and paralyzed.

"My dad used to tell him stories about her, and he’d show him pictures now and then. I assumed you knew."

"I had no idea," Agatha managed, her voice barely a whisper.

Of course, she hadn't known. Had she suspected Riandra's name was being uttered in her home, she likely would have sanitized the entire apartment just to stop her wife from entertaining the thought. She couldn't fathom how Javier had managed to pull this off without detection, yet she wasn't surprised. Rio had always been the apple of his eye; he idolized the daughter he had, of course he would fill her son’s mind with tales of her greatness so that Nicholas, even without meeting her, would know her existence and feel affection for her. Javier’s constant wish was for them to be a complete family.

"I didn't mean to—"

"It was the first thing he's said in months," Agatha interrupted, still reeling from shock, unsure how to process the feelings, her entire body still trembling.

"I'm sorry, Agatha, I know that—"

"No, it's fine," she insisted, though she was clearly far from okay. She remained fixed on Rio, who was as motionless as she was. Feeling Benny gently take her hand, she finally drew a deep breath and shook her head, forcing herself to look at him. "You don't need to — I'm the one who should say that."

Benito simply shook his head.

"You lost him, too, Agatha. We all did."

"I know, and that's why it hurts so much; it shouldn't be this way."

"I know," he said, his expression utterly crestfallen. She slid onto the bench beside him, resting her head on his shoulder. "I'm sorry too, not just about him, but I know you're going through a lot right now, and I really am sorry." Agatha shook her head again.

"We're not going to talk about that right now, it's not the time," she stated. He looked at her, his eyebrows furrowed, his eyes full of sorrow. It pained her to see the immense suffering he was trying to hide. "This moment is about him, and we're sad enough as it is, you know he would hate to see us like this."

"How did you ever get through it?" he asked, his voice choked, and Agatha lowered her head, thinking of her own father.

"Time," she answered, her lips pressed tight, squeezing his shoulder in a comforting gesture. "The pain never truly leaves, but time helps."

He looked up at her, his eyes glistening with unshed tears. When he finally buried his face in her shoulder, crying, Agatha held him tight, gently stroking his face and letting her own tears fall as well.

***

Ten Years Ago

Beyond the massive, empty space that had consumed her entire being, Agatha was certain that guilt would be her relentless shadow for the rest of her life.

She hadn't returned to Darby since her wedding. She and Tessa were perpetually busy with work, and Tessa would never step foot in Montana, not even under duress. Though Tessa had a measured fondness for Benito and Javier, it wasn't enough for her to abandon her sophisticated ways. Agatha barely retained a memory of the farm; her only connection was through occasional photos Benito sent — horses, new crops — and Javier's anecdotes. Everything else was a blurred recollection from years past. Now, this long absence was a source of profound guilt.

She had given up trying to persuade Tessa. She always asked her to visit, but one trip had been enough: when they got engaged, Tessa conceded, accepting it as the necessary thing to do before marriage. That was the only time, and they didn't even stay forty-eight hours. Agatha hadn't really needed to comply, but she couldn't tolerate her wife's incessant complaining, who looked perpetually ready to faint from the smell of the horses. Agatha, I love you with all my heart, but I am never coming here again, Tessa had declared. Agatha had laughed then, but the promise became grim reality.

It wasn't that Agatha was eager to visit, either. Ever since she had decisively put an end to the chaotic relationship she and Rio shared for years, she couldn't bring herself to revisit that part of her past. She lacked the courage, didn't want to confront the feelings it might stir, didn't want to expose herself, and certainly didn't want to risk upending her life again. Rio would always matter, but that was the extent of it. They needed that distance; she felt she owed it to both Rio and Tessa.

Yet, her lack of visits to Darby hadn't meant severing ties with her family. Tessa consistently arranged flights for Agatha's parents, hosting them with meticulous care and affection, and Javier was a familiar presence in their home. She hadn't needed to go to Darby to be close to them, but now, she utterly despised herself for her failure to visit.

She cursed every canceled trip, every invented excuse to her parents. She cursed having stayed away for so long that her father, desperate to see her, took the road that day, euphoric to collect her at the airport, even amidst a fucking storm.

She and Tessa had been fighting — she couldn't even recall the topic, probably something trivial as always. But as soon as the argument concluded, Agatha stubbornly declared she was going home to Darby. Ignoring her wife’s hysterical objections, she booked the first flight to Montana, neglecting to check the weather. Upon landing, she found herself completely marooned. The car rental counter was shut, phone service was non-existent, the lights flickered, and everyone was huddled together, gazing at the rain that resembled a biblical flood.

The rain was so heavy that Agatha could barely see a foot in front of her. The water hammered the asphalt with an unprecedented force, blurring everything, compounded by a thick fog that made her feel isolated in a watery vacuum — nothing but rain surrounding her.

She called her parents, informing them she had arrived but would be delayed due to being stranded. Her father dismissed her concerns, saying he would come for her anyway, despite her pleas for him not to.

He never made it to the airport.

The slick road caused him to lose control, sending the car careening down an embankment halfway through his journey.

Agatha hadn't seen him in months and hadn't been granted one final look. Now, she was consumed by a profound pain  —an all-encompassing anguish knowing she would never see him again.

Agatha would never again hear his loud laughter or feel his tight embrace. She wouldn't stand on tiptoes, grinning from ear to ear, to thank him for that special coffee he never disclosed the source of. She wouldn't hear his voice anymore, couldn't call him late at night to chat about her day and hear about the farm. She would never see him again, and it was precisely because he was so desperate to see her.

No words existed to articulate her feelings. No human language could capture the depth of her despair, the suffocating pressure in her throat, or the pain that had become physically debilitating. She hurt so badly that sometimes she felt utterly nothing — her body, exhausted by suffering, simply went numb.

She hadn't been able to eat for days, and the mere thought of leaving her bed brought on nausea. How could she possibly live knowing her father died yearning to see her, consumed by missing her? How could she bear it?

Agatha wondered how she even had tears left to shed. She had cried nonstop for days, all day and through the nights, crying so much her eyes ached and her head throbbed. That anguish had completely overwhelmed her, a sensation so vast and consuming that she could no longer feel anything but the pain of loss and the all-consuming guilt.

She hadn't spoken for several days. It had been her father's wish to be interred in Houston, his birthplace, alongside his family. The transfer of the body, as well as organizing the wake and coordinating invitations for all their significant contacts, had consumed days. Agatha had been too distraught to handle the logistics; Tessa had managed everything. Yet, Agatha could barely bring herself to look at Tessa. She couldn't look at anyone, least of all herself.

Agatha felt a powerful resentment toward Tessa — for all the excuses, for the sheer disdain for Agatha's roots, and for convincing her that her family and home were insignificant. Her deepest regret was her own failure: for not fighting harder, for not asserting her needs, and for not making sure she was present for the things that mattered to her father, even if they were inconvenient for her. She had broken her promise to ride with him again, never visited her grandparents' old home, and hadn't given him the grandchild he wanted so much.

She barely had the strength to stand. She couldn't focus on anything around her, utterly lost in the hollow space within. She was so numbed that she didn't recall entering the chapel, didn't register the family friends' words of comfort, and hadn't even managed to acknowledge those who came to pay their respects. She was merely a physical presence, her soul capitulating.

Tessa exhausted every effort to console her, but Agatha could barely look at her, or Lilia, or her mother. Every time someone approached, she would flinch and withdraw. She was incapable of speech, unable to eat, and struggled simply to draw breath.

"Your godfather has arrived," Tessa murmured affectionately beside her, but Agatha kept her gaze averted, only lifting her eyes to the chapel entrance. The minimal movement sent a spike of pain through her head.

Javier entered alongside Benito, both dressed in black, wearing their familiar farm boots — the very style her father wore. She watched them respectfully remove their hats, noting their anguished expressions, their eyes heavy with shared grief. But Agatha couldn't move; she was paralyzed. Then, she saw her — and it was the first moment in days that she truly managed to breathe.

She remembered their last meeting two years ago, vividly. Since then, they had successfully avoided each other; since that final conversation, Agatha had pretended Rio no longer existed in her life.

But there she was. Like her father and brother, she wore leather boots, dark jeans, and a black collared shirt. She mirrored their actions: removed her hat, made the sign of the cross, and then, her brown eyes locked onto Agatha's.

Her mind must have short-circuited, because the next thing Agatha knew, she was wrapped in Rio’s arms. Rio froze for a few seconds, then instantly embraced her tightly, squeezing her against her chest, resting her head on top of Agatha's, and gently swaying their bodies as if trying to rock her to sleep.

Agatha hadn't realized how desperately she needed this, but god, she did. All her catatonia shattered in Rio's embrace. She began to sob uncontrollably, her tears soaking Rio's shirt, crying loudly and allowing herself to break completely, unafraid of vulnerability because in Rio’s arms, she felt utterly safe. She inhaled deeply against Rio’s shirt, pulling in that familiar scent of damp earth and grass. Though her body shook with sobs, she felt herself relax; she could breathe again, and the heavy pounding in her chest finally subsided.

"I am so sorry," she heard Rio's low voice against her hair. "I am so sorry, Agatha." Agatha couldn't speak; she couldn't bear to let go. Instead, she lifted her face to look at Rio, tears streaming as she found solace in those brown eyes, which were now overflowing with the deep affection she had desperately craved and denied herself for years.

"It was me," Agatha managed to choke out, barely a whisper. Rio's eyes widened. She shook her head vehemently and pulled Agatha back into her chest, offering fierce comfort.

"Don't you dare ever say that again," Rio insisted. Her tone, though low and affectionate, was firm. "It was not your fault, I swear. It wasn't. Please, believe me, it was not your fault."

Rio started to pull away, but Agatha held on tighter, burying her face in Rio's neck, feeling Rio take a steadying breath. Riandra relented, squeezing her back, stroking her back and kissing the top of her head.

"I am here, cariño." The use of the endearment made Agatha's body convulse with a sob. "I am right here."

Agatha lost all sense of time. They remained locked in the embrace, almost fused together, as Agatha wept and Rio held her tight. She had never felt so secure, so protected. She ignored the stares, the shock on her mother's face, the obvious discomfort etched on her wife's expression — she simply did not care. Rio was there, and that was her only reality.

Agatha cried until she found calm, sobbed until her breathing stabilized, until her heart resumed a tolerable rhythm instead of the frantic panic that had racked her all week. Rio held her, silently consoling her, until she could regain her composure.

"It wasn't your fault," Rio repeated as they finally drew apart, gently cradling Agatha's face and looking deep into her eyes. Staring into those brown orbits, Agatha finally felt the truth sink in. "He loved you more than anything in the world, and I know you loved him just as fiercely. I know you, mi cariño." Agatha's heart constricted upon hearing that old nickname. "I know your heart. I know you love him above all else, and you would never, ever, hurt him. Sometimes, men die and there’s nothing we can do about it."

Tears still running down her face, Agatha offered a weak nod.

"Come on," Rio urged. "You deserve to say goodbye to him. He would want that."

Agatha allowed herself to be guided by her. Rio wrapped her arms around her body as she led them through the chapel, ignoring all the stares and the sepulchral silence that had settled since Agatha had run into her arms. Nothing mattered now except what Agatha was feeling; she could swallow all those feelings later — she didn't care about the distorted look of that bleach-blonde or Evanora’s bulging eyes at seeing them together. Agatha was what mattered now.

This was unplanned. Rio had only come to offer condolences, intending to be brief and inconspicuous. She hadn't even planned to meet Agatha's eyes, but life intervenes, and Agatha desperately needed her. Rio would never have refused.

Agatha was shaking in her arms. Rio held firm, guiding her and pressing her close, even though she felt as though she had been kicked in the stomach simply by being near Agatha, and enduring her wife’s cold, sharp stare. The last thing Rio wanted was to feel this intimacy again, but denying Agatha anything felt impossible.

When they reached the coffin, Rio kissed Agatha's head, letting her lean against her body as she wept. Rio remained still with her for long minutes until she sensed Agatha’s breathing steady. Then, taking Agatha’s hand, she placed it on the solid wood of the casket, enduring Agatha’s tears soaking her shirt.

"Don't let him leave without saying goodbye. You need this to find peace."

When Agatha knelt before the coffin, resting her head against the wood, Rio knelt with her, staying by her side until Agatha could finally collect herself.

After that, Rio simply left. She didn't say goodbye, she didn't stay for the burial or the reception — she just departed without a single word. Later, when Tessa took her hand at the graveside, Agatha closed her eyes and wished it was still Rio holding her.

***

Present Day

That encounter at the chapel had been the last time Agatha saw Rio, a decade earlier. It remained the clearest memory she had of her: the warmth, the nurturing care, the soft words. Rio had cast aside all dignity just to be there. Agatha understood why she hadn't stayed; after everything, she wouldn't have stayed either.

Now, they stood face-to-face, separated only by the large, imposing coffin. Rio's brown eyes fixed on Agatha's in a stare that sent a chill down her spine — and not from familiarity. There was no hint of tenderness, no affection, no sign of remembrance; her eyes were vacant, as though she were looking at a stranger.

Agatha was willing to set aside her own ego if necessary; she would never let pride interfere at a time like this. Regardless of their past, they were family. However, the way Rio was looking at her made movement impossible. It was clear: Rio did not want her there.

Agatha did not recognize this person, this cold version of Rio who recoiled from her, whose posture was rigid and gaze unforgiving. Agatha met her stare, her eyebrows subtly knit together in an unspoken appeal, but Rio remained utterly unmoved. Even as tears began to slide down Agatha's cheeks, Rio didn't blink. For the first time ever, she felt no comfort in those familiar brown eyes — only raw anguish.

The painful eye contact was broken only when a woman placed a hand on Rio's shoulder, offering a sympathetic look. The woman was tall, with a close-cropped haircut, and carried an air of intimacy; they were clearly close. Agatha didn't know the woman, but she observed the undeniable softening of Rio's features in her presence.

Agatha felt insignificant.

***

After enduring several polite but unwelcome conversations and words of comfort, Rio finally found a measure of relief as the large crowd began to fade away. She simply couldn't handle the pitying glances and the well-meaning phrases. They were utterly meaningless, and she resented the attention and the misplaced care. She didn't need any of it.

She knew her father was respected and beloved, and she acknowledged the grief of everyone who had shared a lifetime with him: the farmhands who had watched her grow and helped build the operation, the later employees who had become part of the ranch family, the townsfolk, and all those grateful for her family's presence. She accepted that his passing was painful for many, but he was her father. They had lost a friend, a mentor, a godfather, or a colleague, but she had lost everything. She had lost the only person who loved her without condition. She felt utterly unmoored — she had lost an entire world, and no one else's pain could possibly compare.

It was easier for everyone else. They hadn't built a life with him, hadn't been there for his absolute best and worst moments, and didn't know him as she did. For them, they were two sides of the same coin, two souls in different bodies — this loss was simply too personal to measure.

Rio desperately wanted to feel empathy for them, for everyone who genuinely cared for her father. God, she wished she could even feel empathy for her own brother, but she couldn't. All she felt was a sharp resentment, convinced that she was the only one who had truly dedicated her life to him.

She sat on the grassy ground, her head resting against the stone gazebo, a bottle of vodka clutched in her hand. Rio let the tears stream freely down her face, not even bothering to wipe them away; they wouldn't stop, and she had no idea how to make them.

She had never been good with words, but she felt that even if she were, no word existed capable of measuring what she was feeling.

"I'm sorry, dad," she choked out, murmuring into the marble of the gazebo as she pressed her head against it. "I wish I had lived up to more. I wish I had been everything you wanted."

"You always were," Olivia's voice said, causing Rio to lift her head. The brunette sat down beside her, gently taking the bottle from Rio's grip. "He adored you, Rio. He loved you beyond anything, you know that."

"I should have done more."

"You did everything you could. You stood by him through everything, good and bad. There is absolutely no one in this world he loved more than you."

"But I—" Rio started to protest, but Olivia cupped her face in her hands, looking at her with deep affection.

"Everyone is flawed, Riandra, and you are no different. He loved you exactly as you are."

With a stifled sob, Rio let herself be pulled into Olivia's embrace. She desperately needed the comfort; she still had to face the inevitable reception and that ridiculous custom of celebrating the spirit — as if there were anything worth celebrating right now.
