Chapter 10: loneliness

Chapter Summary

this one is a bit intense

Chapter Notes

a lot of angst in this one but things will get better, i promise

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"Matches burn after the other, pages turn and stick to each other, wages earned and lessons learned but I, I'm right where you left me" – Right Where You Left Me, Taylor Swift

Agatha sighed, her breath catching as she walked along the dirt ground contoured by the trimmed matting toward the Vidal estate. With every step that brought her closer to the enormous house, she felt increasingly small, as if the massive structure were ready to swallow her whole.

Rising right in the middle of the entire property, the Vidal mansion was monumental, a colossus of dark wood and rough stone that stood against the sky — it was imposing. The steeply pitched roofs, of slate and dark metal, looked like overlapping swords pointing skyward, and the contrast of the dark wood of the walls, almost black under the now fading sunlight, with the robust grey rock of the base, was one of a sophisticated yet rustic beauty. You couldn't say the house had merely aged; it had matured. Each pine beam and piece of stone looked deeply settled, becoming one with the soil itself. It gave the uncanny impression that the house hadn't been constructed, but rather had sprouted organically from the soil, belonging there irrevocably.

As a child, Agatha recalled none of this imposing magnitude. She always knew it was a manor, yet it never felt overwhelming or ostentatious. It was her second home, a place of warmth. But now, standing on the final few steps leading to the wide-open doors — a silent, hollow invitation — she felt she was crossing the threshold of a fortress. Her hand grew slick with sweat against the small, trusting palm of Nicky, who walked silently beside her, turning his head to side to side, wide-eyed, trying to absorb every single detail.

The house, while large enough to avoid feeling cramped, was nonetheless full. Though the crowd was much smaller than at the wake, it was packed with those who had been truly close with Javier. The country music her godfather liked drifted softly in the background, small tables laden with finger foods and drinks were scattered among the entrance hall furnishings, where people clustered, conversing in a hushed, suffocated atmosphere, all trying to honor his wish for a celebration.

Javier's great-grandparents, being Mexican, had instilled the tradition of the celebration of the spirit. They held that death was not a tragedy, but merely a phase in the soul’s eternal cycle through different planes. When a spirit departed, it required celebration for its evolution, enveloped in positive energy to ensure a peaceful, swift passage.

In truth, everyone was present purely out of deference to his will, because no one felt like celebrating. Agatha’s chest felt heavy, and a tight knot constricted her throat. All she craved was to going to bed and weep, but she couldn't betray his final request. Yet, the necessity of celebrating this moment pierced her to the very core, and she couldn't begin to grasp the emotional toll this tradition must be taking on Rio and Benito.

Her eyes scanned the room until they landed on Kate and Benito near the left side of the staircase. As she drew near, her lips parted in recognition of a familiar face she hadn't seen in many years:

"Agatha!" Wanda exclaimed, her eyes widening with surprise, her gaze holding a blend of shock and unmistakable affection. "My god, it’s been years..."

Wanda was no longer anything like she remembered, but at the same time, she hadn't changed at all — she had only matured. Her long blonde hair was now brown with soft bangs; time’s passage had merely made her face more strikingly becoming. She wore a loose black slip dress, appropriate for mourning, but a stark departure from the vivid exuberance of her youth. She was beautiful, and Agatha felt a sudden rush of tears, realizing how deeply she missed her — it clenched her heart. The last time they’d met was years ago, when she married Tessa; contact had dissolved after that. Agatha had been swallowed by California, and the farm life was firmly in the past.

It was, of course, that precise instant the universe chose to hit her with crystal clarity: out of every choice life had offered, she had consistently managed to choose the worst possible path. She used to believe she was simply going with the flow, accepting the natural direction of things. Now, she saw she had actively abandoned an entire life. With every tick of the clock, she felt more and more like the prodigal son.

"My god, Wanda! You haven't aged a day," Agatha managed through a weak, choked laugh, her vision hazy with unshed tears. She watched her friend's eyebrows draw together in a look of genuine sympathy.

"Neither have you," Wanda replied easily, extending her arm to grasp Agatha's shoulder in a warm gesture of greeting and solace. "The circumstances are terrible, but I truly am happy to see you."

A part of Agatha ached, knowing that thirteen years ago, she would have received the world’s tightest hug instead of a shoulder squeeze.

"Me too. I should have come sooner," she said, clearing her throat to stop the phrase from sounding like a confession of regret. Wanda simply offered a polite shrug.

"Hey, Agatha," Yelena chimed in cheerfully, holding a beer. She seemed to be the only person in the group blissfully unaware of the heavy atmosphere.

"Yelena!" Agatha replied with a small smile. "It's been a while, huh?"

"You two know each other?" Wanda asked, gesturing between them, her brow furrowed in genuine confusion.

"Yelena has been to California with my father a few times to handle horse deals," Benito explained. "We always spent some time together."

"Ah, right," Wanda said, nodding in realization. "You went with him last time, I remember. Wow, I never connected those dots."

"Most of the time I go with Rio, so you probably didn't link it," Yelena said with an easy shrug. At that, Agatha noticed every gaze in the circle — save for Yelena’s clueless one — instantly freeze on her. Wonderful, she thought, wishing she could dig a hole right there and vanish forever.

"Hi, little man," Yelena said, crouching down to Nicky’s level. Nicky offered his hand for a high-five, and Yelena affectionately ruffled his hair. "You've grown." This earned her a slight upturn of the boy's lips — almost a smile.

"Is he your son?" Wanda asked in an affectionate tone, tilting her head to the side in a clear expression that she found him adorable. Agatha was a mother; it was obvious that every expression of enchantment for her boy made her proud.

"Yes, Nicholas," Agatha confirmed, looking down at her son, who returned her gaze. "He's six."

"He's only a year younger than mine," Wanda replied with a smile, lifting her head slightly and scanning the area. "I'd love for you to meet them, but they're definitely off running around somewhere."

"You had twins?" Agatha asked, suddenly captivated. Her eyes fixed on the thick wedding band adorning Wanda's left hand for the first time.

Agatha knew her lifelong best friend had married Riandra's best friend. She'd received an invitation, but hadn't attended. Tessa hadn't wanted to go, and Agatha hadn't wanted to attend alone. That's when the distance between them began — and she knew, shamefully, it was her own fault. She accepted the blame, even if Tessa had influenced her; Wanda was her friend, not Tessa's. Agatha felt a growing bitterness whenever she reflected on her own marriage.

"Billy and Tommy."

"I got one," a voice announced out of nowhere, right behind her.

Agatha hadn't heard that voice in years, but she knew immediately it was Natasha. She spun around, just in time to see Natasha's green eyes fly wide as if she were seeing a demon, before she had the decency to regain her composure in a split second.

Agatha nearly let out a nervous laugh — what a warm welcome.

"Natasha," Agatha managed, her lips tight in a cordial greeting. The blonde gave a quick, brief nod, her gaze not lingering.

"Agatha," she returned in a dry, indifferent tone, immediately turning her eyes back to her wife.

Truly, some things never change.

"I wasn't running!" the light-brown-haired boy whined softly. Wanda simply looked at him, one eyebrow arched. "I wasn't!"

"Where's your brother?" she asked, crossing her arms. Natasha, meanwhile, was clearly fighting to suppress a laugh, her arms wrapped around her son's shoulders.

"I don't know," the boy sniffled.

Wanda and Natasha exchanged a meaningful look, and Agatha felt a pang of envy for that easy, overflowing synchronicity and affection between them.

"You handle this one, I'll go get the other," Natasha said, her voice shifting completely to a casual, affectionate tone. She gave her wife a loving kiss on the forehead.

Wanda nodded. As Natasha left, the little boy started to speak, but his mother cut him off instantly, as if she could read his mind:

"You can go outside if you promise you won't run," she said, her tone measured yet affectionate.

"I wasn't running!" he grumbled. Wanda simply raised her eyebrows and crossed her arms again.

"Thomas..."

The boy pouted and rolled his eyes.

"Just a little bit," he conceded, holding his thumb and index finger close to indicate a small amount. Everyone in the circle held their breath, clamping their lips shut to avoid laughing and undermining the reprimand. "But I promise I won't do it again."

"Then you may go." The moment Wanda finished speaking, the boy sprinted away, narrowly bumping four different people. Wanda let out a frustrated grunt and shook her head, though a hint of a smile played on her lips. She turned back to Agatha. "That's my Tommy. An angel, as you can see." Agatha offered a weak laugh.

"Are both of them like that?" Agatha asked, curious. Wanda shook her head.

"No, Billy is more well-behaved, but when they're together, they just become one thing."

The conversation continued to flow in a strangely superficial manner. Years had passed, and they were essentially strangers now. Benito rarely visited the farm, Kate had never been, and Yelena was an absolute airhead. Naturally, the topics remained firmly rooted in trivialities, leaving Agatha feeling like a fish out of water.

Wanda and Yelena were commenting on the farm and town news, while Agatha offered vague summaries of the last few times she’d seen Javier. She carefully circled around anything remotely personal, focusing on how new and different everything felt to her.

Wanda was discussing the school, which Rio had expanded to twice its original size. Agatha understood that since Rio had invested in free instruction in four languages — another one of her benevolent projects, seemingly sparing no expense for the town's improvement — many families were relocating to Darby. Agatha found it amusing, even ironic, that Darby was becoming a beacon of education. She was about to offer praise when a silhouette caught her eye, causing her to look right over her friend's shoulder.

Rio.

Agatha's lips parted involuntarily as if to call out, and then she frowned, paralyzed by indecision. Simultaneously, an enormous, raw urge compelled her to rush across the room and speak to her. She didn't know what she would say, or even what she could say, but the need was sudden and overwhelming — she simply had to hug her, hear her voice, and be there for her. This wasn't about the accumulated emotional baggage of the past years; it was about this moment, and the strong bond they had always shared. Agatha didn't want to rely on the reports from her aunt or mother about Rio's state; she wanted to hear it directly from her. She wanted to hear everything Rio had to say — even if it hurt, which it likely would.

The emotional wall she had constructed over the years began to crumble just by seeing Rio standing there, a few steps away. Deep down, she had always known it would be like this. It was why she had to leave for so long; she had made her choice, and there was no going back. A part of her still clung to Rio like a magnet; if she hadn't put distance between herself and Darby, she never would have been able to break free. Now, she realized that all those years and all that distance had meant nothing at all. Here she was, desperate to melt into Rio's embrace.

Then, Rio turned her head slightly, a beer bottle clutched in her hand, and their eyes locked.

Agatha’s breath hitched. She felt instantly swallowed by those brown eyes, which bore into her from across the room, empty yet profound, like the depths of a black hole.

This harshness was new. She had never witnessed it before. Despite all the turmoil they had shared over the years, Rio's brown eyes had always been soft, kind, always overflowing with love — that unique, shared understanding that belonged only to them. Now, it was utterly different. Agatha felt assaulted, rendered tiny by the cold intensity of Rio's stare. A chill ran through her; it was as if a protective layer of her spirit had peeled away, leaving her raw and exposed. Her chest tightened painfully, as though her heart were being crushed by two firm hands.

"Are you alright? You just went pale," Benito murmured, placing a hand on her shoulder. Agatha quickly turned to him, nodded, and then looked back toward the spot where Rio had been standing.

But Rio was gone. She had vanished like a ghost, as if she had never been there at all, and Agatha had simply imagined her presence. Frustrated, uneasy, and sad all at once, Agatha let out the heavy breath she hadn't realized she was holding.

She was home, but she definitely didn't feel like it.

***

"What's with the funeral vibe, guys?" Yelena greeted them, a crooked smile distorting her face as she stepped into the kitchen. Rio, nursing a beer and leaning against the counter, simply subjected her to a slow, unmoving stare.

"I’m drunk enough to rip her head off," Rio muttered in a low growl after a long pull from her bottle, glaring at Natasha, who sighed softly.

"This time, I'll not only cheer you on, I’ll join in."

"I’m just trying to break the ice," Yelena said gently, nudging Rio with her shoulder as she leaned beside her to grab a beer from the ice bucket in the sink. Rio turned her gaze on her, sharp as a predator ready to pounce. "It's what he wanted, Rio. It's his celebration."

"And I did the whole thing exactly as he requested," she muttered grudgingly, gesturing with the beer bottle. "But to demand I be in a celebratory mood— I think that's pushing it." She narrowed her eyes at the younger Romanoff.

"It's not about being festive, it's about—"

"I know what it’s about, Yelena," Rio cut her off harshly. Natasha watched silently. "But I also know what I feel, and I can't change it, because he’s not coming back."

"You don't have to stay for the whole thing," Natasha offered gently. Rio looked at her askance, considering the offer. "You've done the duty—invited everyone, welcomed the guests. You don't need to stay, and honestly, I think it's better if you don't."

"I'm going to break something if I stay here another hour," Rio muttered, rubbing her forehead in distress, shaking her head before taking another deep drink of beer.

"Benito is here; you're free to leave," Yelena said with a shrug. Instantly, Rio's face hardened, followed by a choked, enraged sound and a sharp slam of her fist on the marble countertop.

"Your inability to know when to keep your mouth shut is amazing," Natasha sighed, shaking her head and taking a deep breath, frustrated that Yelena had reignited the very issue she was trying to defuse.

"And the prodigal son comes home," Rio’s voice dripped with sarcasm, her face contorted by a resentment so potent it felt like a stone lodged in her chest. "My father had to die for him to finally return. Isn't that rich?" she sneered, her eyes distant, the hurt forming a hard lump in her throat. "My father would move heaven and earth to fly to California almost every month just to spend time with him, yet he only sets foot here to bury the old man."

"He did visit sometimes," Yelena offered, slumping her shoulders. Natasha smacked her own forehead. "What? It's true, he did."

"Twice a year?" Rio countered with a thoroughly unimpressed look, tilting the bottle back to drain it, then immediately opening a fresh one. "Wow. Son of the year, truly." Natasha glanced at Yelena, who visibly shrank; she hadn't intended to steer the conversation this way. "But you know what’s even worse? She’s here."

"Who?" Yelena asked, her face wrinkled in genuine confusion. This time, Natasha threw her arms up in total exasperation.

"You have got to be kidding me!" she exclaimed with a nervous laugh. "Who, genius? Who else is here after not stepping foot on the farm for over ten years?"

"Oh..." Yelena finally caught on, twisting her face into a wince that clearly conveyed, "My bad."

Rio ignored them both, lost in her own thoughts and emotions, the bitterness of her pain tasting metallic in her mouth. An anguish was consuming her, leaving her feeling ripped apart from the inside out.

She wouldn't diminish anyone else's sorrow, but the sight of them here, after so many years of absence, felt preposterous — absurd, and deeply unfair. It was unfair that they were there crying over that loss when they never made the slightest effort to be present. No one had been there; only her. She was the one who stayed, who stood by her father until the very end; no one else did.

She could barely recall the last time she'd seen her brother, certainly over a year ago. She only knew about his life through her father's endless chatter, always boasting to the world about his educated boy making a success of himself in the city. Benito even managed the family business from a distance, handling the finances and paperwork as if everything they had built with their shared sweat was just numbers on a spreadsheet. To him, the sweat, the people, and the earth of the farm — everything she and her father worked on — was merely money. He didn't know what it was like to tread dirt paths daily, to spend days meticulously monitoring the aging of the wines, or to travel personally to vouch for the lineage and care of the horses. He was a preppy city boy playing cowboy who couldn't even manage to show up, not even for the father who constantly longed to share with him everything he shared with her.

Rio refused to even think about Agatha; the mere thought made her head throb. Thirteen years. Thirteen long years without setting foot in Darby, and now she was here, acting as though they were still family, her tear-filled blue eyes trying to pierce Rio's soul, as if she still had the right — as if no time had passed at all.

"You've clearly had enough to drink," Natasha interrupted Rio's turbulent thoughts, reaching out to take the beer bottle. Rio dodged the attempt with a frown, swaying slightly, and bumped into Yelena, who steadied her instantly. "You've been silently bug-eyed for over three minutes. I know you're about to combust; it's time to stop."

"I just buried my father; I know when to stop," Rio retorted, her voice edged with bitterness.

"Rio—" Natasha started, but Rio abruptly cut her off.

"Don't you think it's completely absurd?" Rio asked, her voice tight with raw outrage. Natasha opened and closed her mouth, narrowing her eyes, unsure what specific thing she meant now. "How does she have the balls to show up here after all these years, weeping as if she cares so much? Where the hell was she?" she asked rhetorically, her eyebrows drawn together in a choked anguish that made her voice tremble. "Where was she when he was sick? And Benito?"

"Rio, you know I'm not Agatha's biggest fan, but that's not fair," Natasha tried to say gently, her eyebrows knitted together, but Rio kept shaking her head, drinking swig after swig. "He was her godfather, he raised her like a daughter, she grew up here, you know she feels terrible, she might not have even known he was sick."

"He was there almost once a month, how could she not know?"

"You knew him better than anyone; he would never have told her, and you know that."

"She would have known if she were present, if she cared enough," Rio practically spat the words out. "My father, with an entire empire to keep running, was constantly traveling back and forth, even sick, just to see those two who think they're too high and mighty to step on dirt ground."

"Benito is your brother," Natasha reminded her, giving her a pointed look, refusing to be intimidated by Rio's hardened expression. "He's his father, too, Rio. Do you honestly think he isn't hurting? That he isn't just as broken as you are?" Rio opened her mouth to argue, but Natasha stopped her, pointing a finger. "You loved your father above everything, but Benito loves him too. Choosing a different life path doesn't change his feelings for his family, and you know it." She stressed the final part, pressing her index finger against Rio's chest. Rio, tight-lipped, glared at the gesture before meeting her eyes again. "You are drunk, you are broken, and you are consumed by the pain of loss. You are resentful of the people you love not being here when you had to face everything alone."

"I'm not—"

"You love them both," Natasha stated with finality. Rio clamped her mouth shut with a huff; her expression darkened further by the second. "And that is why this is hurting you. Because you love them, and when you needed support most, they failed to show up. This isn't just about your father, they both loved him, and he loved them so much he did everything he could to be with them; they are grieving that loss too. But beyond the grief, you are deeply wounded, and that is why you are so angry."

"What kind of love is that?" Rio murmured in a low voice, thick with acidity. She turned her back sharply, leaning on the edge of the sink, and cracked open another beer. As she took the first gulp, she felt a heavy lump lodge in her throat, and her eyes began to water.

She hated herself for this, but Natasha was right. She loved them, and she felt pathetic for it, like a child desperate for attention who is adored by everyone except the people she most longs to please. That unreciprocated love made her feel invisible. They both left, neither had cared about leaving her behind, and they moved on with their lives for years. Even her father had joined them, visiting and praising them, while she stayed behind, all alone.

"What kind of love is that?" she repeated, spinning back around to face the sisters, whose eyebrows were drawn together in profound compassion, which only made her feel worse. "What kind of love is it when you love all alone?"

"They are family, Rio," Natasha said with a shrug. "Life moved on, yes, but time doesn't erase that bond. It doesn't undo this kind of connection. They love you too." Rio laughed contemptuously and drank even more. "They do love you. They're practically begging for your attention, but you're too hurt to notice."

"I am not hurt," Rio spat the words out firmly. "I am angry!" She slammed the bottle onto the marble sink with such force that Yelena feared the glass would shatter. "I'm angry because they were living their movie-perfect little lives in California while I was here with my sick father! I took care of him and where the hell were they?! It's simple to cry over spilled milk; it's easy to cry sentimental tears now that he's dead! He's dead!" she nearly screamed, tears finally flowing unchecked down her face. "He's dead," she repeated, her voice dropping to a low tone, her gaze vacant. Yelena stepped forward to hug her, but Rio immediately recoiled.

Her father was dead, and she was utterly alone.

As she finished that beer and opened yet another, Natasha took a deep breath, shaking her head. There was nothing left to say or do. Only time could heal, but that didn't ease the tightness in Natasha's chest as she watched Rio so broken. God knew how hard she was trying to intervene, yet every day, Rio seemed to sink deeper.

"Let's go," she said to Yelena in a near-whisper. Her sister stared back, eyes wide, as if Natasha had suggested the most ridiculous thing possible.

"I am not leaving her here like this," Yelena hissed in a quiet rush. They were just steps away from Rio, who seemed utterly engulfed by her own despair, tears streaming compulsively, present only in body, her soul completely vacant.

"She needs space to breathe," Natasha said, her eyebrows knitted with compassion. "I'm going to get her out of here in an hour, tops. There is no chance she can cope with all this the way she is."

"And what should I do?"

"Keep an eye on Agatha and Benny; don't let them talk to her," Natasha instructed. "It's not the right time; it will only hurt everyone." Yelena nodded regretfully. "I'll go find the boys and let Wanda know we're leaving soon."

***

Nineteen Years Ago

Agatha had been clinging to the railing of the Vidal porch for well over an hour, her head resting on the wood She was breathing deeply, her palms clammy with nervous sweat, trying desperately to steel her courage to step inside. She knew what lay ahead would be difficult, but there was no more time; she had postponed it as long as she could.

Since childhood, Agatha had felt the pull to escape that town. Although she found happiness there, an inner certainty told her she was meant for something far greater. The thought of settling down, committing to the same routine day after day for the rest of her life, always left her profoundly restless. The world was vast, far exceeding the boundaries of the farm, and she had an instinct within her to fly away. She craved greatness, to achieve monumental things, to be recognized across the globe. Darby was simply too small to contain her ambition.

But then, there was Rio. The mere thought of her made Agatha inhale sharply, feeling her heart sink in her chest.

All she ever wanted was for Rio to open her mind to the world around her, but Rio’s roots were so deep in that place that not even Agatha's greatest efforts could convince her. Agatha's lifelong goal had been to make Rio see the potential of the world beyond, yet Rio’s roots were so deeply anchored to the land that even Agatha's most passionate arguments couldn't sway her. For years, Agatha pleaded, speaking of going to college, of the places they could explore together, of a life traveling. But Rio's dreams stopped at the farm gate. She dismissed school, approaching everything with minimal effort, refusing to apply to any university, expertly changing the subject, and always deferring the conversation until later. While Agatha fought to free Rio, Rio fought with equal tenacity to keep Agatha bound to her. Neither was willing to yield an inch. 

Agatha loved Rio with every fiber of her being, a certainty she felt down to her core. This was no mere adolescent crush; she knew Riandra was the love of her life. While she was eager to embrace and experience every sensation the world had to offer, she doubted anything could ever surpass the intensity of their connection. Yet, she understood life demanded more than love — it required dreams, fulfillment, and ideals. Loving Riandra did not mean neglecting her own identity. She loved Rio, yes, but she also loved life itself and needed to live it fully. Giving that up was a sacrifice she knew she would forever regret.

Her acceptance letter had arrived a month ago — in three days, she would leave for California, to attend her dream school, and Rio still didn't know.

In her defense, announcing the news earlier had been impossible, and even now, it felt wrong. She was finally forcing herself to speak because, with her departure imminent, she could delay no longer. The moment was agonizingly bittersweet: the best news of her life coincided exactly with the moment Rio received the worst news of hers.

Agatha had biked to the post office to retrieve the letter, arriving home gasping for air, her legs burning from the intense ride. She was already tearing into the envelope before she reached the front door. Her hand was on the doorknob when she finally pulled the paper free, unfolded it, and her eyes raced down the page until she landed on the triumphant line: "Congratulations! UCLA is delighted to offer your admission."

It was an involuntary reaction, something stronger than herself — she instantly let out a scream, jumping joyously in circles before barreling into the house, nearly shouting her parents' names. She found them sitting on the sofa, her mother's face buried in her hands while her father gently stroked her shoulders in comfort. Agatha froze dead in her tracks. Knowing her mother was weeping like that meant something truly serious had happened.

"What happened?" Agatha gasped, the momentum of her excitement broken. She kept her arms behind her back, quickly hiding the letter from view. Her heart, which had been racing with euphoria moments before, now pounded with a fresh wave of worry that struck her head-on as she looked between them.

"Maria has been hospitalized," her mother managed to say in a barely audible voice. Agatha's lips fell open in shock, every trace of her earlier joy draining instantly from her body.

"I thought she was better," she said, looking back and forth for an explanation.

"She was coughing nonstop this morning, her chest was wheezing, she was burning up with fever and couldn't breathe," her father explained, looking dismayed and letting out a heavy sigh. Agatha's brow furrowed with deep concern. "Javier rushed her to the hospital. It's pneumonia; she was barely getting any oxygen in her blood."

"But it's nothing critical, right?" Agatha asked, her voice tight with uncertainty. "She was sick because she wasn't getting treatment, she didn't know what it was, but pneumonia—" She stuttered, desperately seeking reassurance in her parents' eyes. "It's just pneumonia. She'll be fine, won't she? She's in the hospital now."

She didn't get better; in fact, she only wasted away, and two weeks later, her condition progressed to a systemic immune infection, and she passed away.

From the day of Maria’s hospitalization, Agatha was a rooted at Rio’s side — and naturally, she did not breathe a word about her acceptance letter. It was simply not the time. As pleased as she was with her achievement and the university admission, she knew the news would be a devastating blow to Riandra, who was already overwhelmed. Rio's mother was in the hospital; all she needed was support, and Agatha provided it.

As Maria wasted away in the hospital, Rio's appetite vanished. She lost three pounds in a week, stopped speaking, and barely caught a wink of sleep. It was Agatha who gently insisted she eat, preparing her favorite comfort foods. It was Agatha who bathed her, combed her hair, and brushed her teeth with her. It was Agatha who tenderly stroked her face, whispered stories into her ear, and kissed her cheek until she finally fell asleep. It pained Agatha to her very core to watch Rio wither away in life while her mother withered away in a hospital bed.

Her parents were happy about the letter and began making the necessary arrangements for her departure. Yet, this wasn't the exultant joy she had always envisioned; there was no space for it now. Everyone was heavy-hearted over Maria's fate and deeply concerned for the entire Vidal family, as Javier, Benito, and Rio seemed utterly lost without the household matriarch. Agatha's father had to awkwardly step in to manage the farm and guide negotiations he barely knew about. Her mother and Lilia helped maintain the house and care for Benito, while Javier only left the hospital when physically forced out. Everything was dissolving into chaos.

Maria died two weeks after her admission. Another two weeks had passed since her burial, and the situation had only worsened. The entire family was consumed by sorrow, and Rio remained inconsolable, catatonic — the he only person she would speak to was Agatha. This was why Agatha had been paralyzed outside the door for so long, lacking the courage to go in and tell the love of her life she was leaving precisely when Rio needed her most to stay.

But she could not wait any longer; she was departing in three days. She had to talk to Rio — but god, she didn't know if she had the strength to break her heart, knowing her own was already aching, and Rio's pain would be exponentially worse.

Being honest, courage still failed her, yet she had resigned herself to her destiny. She knew she couldn't say no; she would leave regardless of the pain it caused herself or Rio. Since she was so certain of her path, the least she could do was face the consequences of her choices.

When she finally pushed the large porch door open, the groaning echo of the creaking wood sent a chill down her spine. Agatha took a deep breath, feeling that familiar wave of sadness wash over her. The Vidals had lost their matriarch, a loss unparalleled, but Agatha had lost her godmother, too, her second mother. It hurt in a way she never imagined possible, because until that day, she had never conceived of a world without her godmother — without the slow country music always playing on the radio, the kitchen radiating warmth and the scent of cookies, her godmother's loud, melodic voice laughing and speaking Spanish, the open doors and windows, and her attentive, warm, honey-brown eyes, always eager to engage in conversation. The grief was not theirs alone; the house itself seemed mournful; silent, dark, and closed up, conducting its own quiet mourning.

Her footsteps tapped on the wooden floors, and as she ascended the staircase, she gritted her teeth at the loud clatter of each step. The silence was so profound she could hear her own heart beating. It felt empty, yet she knew they were all there — each family member shrunk into their private corner, processing their grief individually.

Rio's bedroom door stood ajar. Biting her lip in apprehension, Agatha peeked her face through the crack. She saw Rio, still in her pajamas, hunched in the cushioned window seat, hugging her bent knees, her chin resting upon them. Agatha felt a stark pang in her chest and a weight that locked her ankles in place; something deep inside begged her to stop, not to do what she was about to do.

The last thing she wanted was to inflict more pain. If she could, she would enclose Riandra in a glass dome, a snow globe where leaves, trees, and horses replaced the snowflakes, isolating her from all suffering, ensuring her happiness. Agatha wished she could simply tuck Rio into her pocket and carry her everywhere. She wanted to take her, to show her that life extended beyond the farm, that she could find happiness elsewhere, that this could be a new beginning. Yet, she knew Rio felt the exact opposite: the more Agatha longed to leave, the more Rio desperately wanted her to stay. She didn't want to hurt Rio, but she knew she would — and she knew that would break her own heart too.

"How long have you been there?" Rio's raw, husky voice startled her out of her deep thoughts. Agatha quickly shook her head, blinked rapidly to gather herself, and opened the door just enough to slip inside.

Riandra's voice had been strained for days, deepened and hoarse from incessant sobbing. She looked so small, seeming to shrink a little more with each passing day. She was frail, her features puffy and swollen. Agatha involuntarily frowned as she sat down next to her in the window seat. Rio instantly cuddled into her side, resting her head in Agatha's lap. Agatha ran her fingers through Rio's hair, feeling her bury her face against her stomach, her arms wrapping tightly around Agatha's torso as if to anchor her permanently. Agatha recognized the unconscious gesture as an unconscious omen.

"Did you shower today?" Agatha asked, her voice barely a whisper, brimming with tenderness. Rio nodded, pulling her face away from Agatha's stomach, now lying so she was looking up at Agatha's face.

Rio's skin was flushed, and the pouches beneath her eyes were puffy from hours of crying and rubbing, but she looked marginally better — at least, better than she had yesterday. Her large brown eyes gazed up intensely, their sweetness so strong Agatha could almost taste it, like warm, melted caramel.

"Have you eaten?" Agatha asked, nervously chewing the inside of her cheek, made shy by the hypnotic intensity of Rio’s stare. Rio’s gaze overflowed with love.

"I’m fine," Rio murmured, rolling her eyes, but Agatha's brow remained furrowed as she continued stroking her face. Now, Agatha's blue eyes were completely drowned in the brown orbits above her.

Riandra gently placed her hand over Agatha's, taking it delicately to press a kiss against her skin. Agatha offered a faint smile and nodded.

"I'm just worried."

"I know," Rio said, tightening her lips. A sudden seriousness crossed her expression, making Agatha’s heart pound.

Rio sat up, facing her and bending her leg again. Agatha felt her heart leap to her throat. Did she know? How could she know? Was this going to be okay? Amidst the nervousness, a flicker of hope emerged: maybe Rio would understand this was only temporary, that it changed nothing about her love.

"I know the last few days have been hard," Rio murmured, reaching out to hold Agatha's hand between hers. Agatha, who had been looking down, instantly lifted her gaze, anxious for what was coming. "I know I haven't—I haven't been..." Rio trailed off, letting out a heavy breath. "It's hard," she finished, and Agatha nodded. "I don't know when this will pass or if it ever will, but I need you to know I don't think I would have survived this if you hadn't been here."

Agatha felt the air seize in her lungs. The pain was a sharp stab to her chest.

"I'm trying to get better. I'm eating, I..." She interrupted herself again, struggling for words. Agatha couldn't look at her anymore, her eyes burning, her vision blurring with the tears she fought to hold back. "I know things will get better. I won't be like this for long, I promise. I want to get better for you, too. My mother is gone, but you are here, and I want to live, truly live, with you, and not just wither away like this."

Agatha bit her lip. She couldn't meet Rio's gaze, the tears already streaming down her face. All she could do was squeeze her eyes shut tight.

"Cariño, why are you crying?" Rio whispered, her voice thick with sweetness. She held Agatha's face while Agatha tried to pull away. "Hey, what happened? Why are you crying?"

With her eyes still closed, Agatha attempted to move Rio's hands, but the warmth of her touch made Agatha's hands tremble. The sob that tore from her was involuntary, making her whole body shudder. She heard Riandra gasp and, almost instantly, pull Agatha's body close, nestling her against her chest.

Agatha couldn't speak. She just stayed there, held against Rio's body. Rio remained silent, too, her chin resting on the crown of Agatha's head, gently rocking them, holding her with a purposeful tightness — the way that always made Agatha feel completely safe.

"I'm sorry," Agatha muffled into Riandra's thick olive-green sweater, breathing in her familiar scent. "I'm sorry, I'm sorry, I'm sorry..." she repeated endlessly, her voice growing more choked with every syllable.

Rio pulled her back just enough to hold her face with both hands, pressing her forehead to Agatha's. When Agatha finally opened her eyes, still blurred by tears, she met the profound confusion on Rio's face, a confusion mixed with palpable concern and wide brown eyes.

"Agatha, what happened?" Rio asked, her tone entirely understanding and worried, not accusatory.

"I'm leaving for California in three days," she blurted out, her words tripping over each other. She watched, almost in slow motion, as the concern in Rio’s eyes drained away, replaced by an inexpressive blankness. Rio’s hands dropped from her face, and she pulled back slowly, staring at Agatha with tightly knitted eyebrows.

"What?" Rio asked, her voice distant, her face twisted into a shape that was almost a literal question mark, as if she'd just heard the most ridiculous thing on earth.

Agatha didn't know if her breathlessness was due to nerves or if she was choking on her own tears  —perhaps both — but the band-aid was off now, and she couldn't retreat. God, it hurt, but this was the path she chose. She knew the pain was inevitable, but she was going anyway. It would hurt at the beginning, but it would pass, and they would be fine — Agatha desperately repeated this internally, trying to take a steady breath under Riandra's intense and confused gaze.

"I received my acceptance letter the day your mother was hospitalized." She hadn't meant to speak so fast, but the words, stuck in guilt for an entire month, spilled from her lips, seeking release. "I never wanted to hide it. I wanted to run and tell you, but it wasn't the right time, and I truly thought she'd recover, but she didn't." Agatha paused, her hands and lips trembling under Rio's unwavering, silent, and indecipherable gaze. "I honestly wanted to tell you, but I didn't want to cause you any more pain. You always knew I wanted this, that I would go. I just didn't know how; I didn't know how to break the news." She was so breathless she felt as if she'd run a marathon. "It's UCLA. I got a full scholarship."

"You actually applied?" Rio asked, the confusion in her tone clear. Agatha straightened her posture when she noticed a flicker of hurt cross Rio's face.

"You knew I was going to; I always talked about it."

"You said you wanted to go there, not that you would."

"Rio, I spent my entire high school life building my resume for this, and insisting you do the same. I wanted you to apply with me, so we could go tog—"

"I don't want to go to fucking California!" Rio shouted harshly, standing up so abruptly that Agatha flinched. She had never heard Rio speak with such force, and despite the shock, Agatha frowned.

"But I do," she replied, her tone softer but firm.

"We promised we would do this together," Rio said in denial, beginning to pace. Agatha stood up, facing her with her eyebrows drawn together. "You promised!"

"I tried, Rio!" she responded, her voice rising slightly. Rio froze, her face contorted with pain as she stared. "I printed all the forms applications for you! I spent days, weeks, months talking to you! God, Rio, I spent years! But you never wanted to listen, you never cared, even though it was my dream!"

"Because it's not mine!" Rio screamed back, and Agatha took an involuntary step backward.

"And I'm supposed to give up my dream because it isn't yours?" Agatha questioned, her voice a thin whisper. Rio opened and closed her mouth a few times, staring blankly.

"Agatha, that's not what I said."

"If this isn't what you want, that's fine; I won't force you. But you can't keep me chained here either. I said we would do this together, and I tried everything I could to get you to do this with me. But what am I supposed to do, Rio? Force you to leave?" she said, her eyebrows knitted, moving closer to Riandra, who simply shook her head, avoiding her gaze. "You know me better than anyone. You are my person, my soulmate." At this, Rio's fierce expression softened, and she didn't pull away as Agatha approached. "You know I've spent my whole life dreaming of this, of this exact moment." Rio avoided her eyes again. "I really need to go. Please, don't make me choose."

"I wouldn't," Rio said with a dry, humorless laugh. "I know exactly what you would choose, and I don't need to hear you say it. You are already making your choice."

"This is the exact opposite of what I'm trying to do, Riandra!" Agatha burst out in exasperation, throwing her arms down at her sides. "You want to stay, and I want to go. That doesn't change my feelings for you; it doesn't change anything between us. I still love you; I still believe in us, I believe we can make this work. We just—" Agatha let out a heavy sigh. "We just need time to figure out the how. And this is the time. I'll leave, you'll stay, but that changes nothing. I don't want it to change."

"Agatha, please," Rio pleaded, her voice hoarse and choked, her eyes glistening with tears. "You don't have to go." Agatha opened her mouth, but Rio rushed on. "You got into UCLA; with your resume, your grades, you can get into any university in Montana. You can still fulfill your dream and be here with me."

"That is exactly the point you don't understand," Agatha said, shaking her head. "I want to go. That is my dream."

"Agatha, I'm begging you," Rio insisted, looking deep into her eyes. Agatha swallowed hard, watching the tears track down Rio's face.

"Rio, I—"

"I'm begging you, Agatha," she repeated quickly, gripping Agatha's waist firmly with both hands. Agatha felt a knot tightening in her throat. "I'm begging you, cariño, please don't leave. We will figure something out, I promise. We will find a way. Give me time; I promise I will try to understand. Please, Agatha."

"Rio," Agatha whispered in a faint voice, feeling her own eyes fill with tears.

"There is no us if you aren't here."

"Don't say that," Agatha instantly scolded her, tears now streaming down her face, too. "There will always be an us; there's no world without us. I don't exist without you."

"Then don't go, please."

Agatha bit her lip. Her vision was clouded by tears, yet she clearly saw the profound apprehension on Riandra's face, the painful way the brown eyes clung to hers, and how Rio's tears perfectly mirrored her own. Agatha could feel the sheer desperation of that gaze deep inside her; she felt it burning from within her chest — something inside her was shattering just from enduring that look.

"Rio, I can't."

***

Present Day

Agatha felt an immense sense of relief thanks to Yelena; if it weren't for her easygoing manner and comforting presence, she would surely have left already.

She had only been there for just over two hours, and out of respect, she planned to stay a little longer, at least until Benito and Riandra delivered their speeches. She acknowledged that everyone processed grief differently, but the entire celebration concept felt fundamentally wrong to her, a feeling she knew Javier's children shared — they were here for him, not by choice.

The truth was that everyone was sad. Conversations were limited to cold, brittle superficialities and strained smiles, as no one had the energy for genuine cheer under the circumstances. Still, it wasn't all bad.

Yelena wasn't a close friend, but Agatha held a certain fondness for the girl and knew how important she was to both Kate and Benito. Natasha's sister was a regular presence in their lives. Agatha wondered if Natasha, here in Darby, with her sour face, knew about this, that her little sister spent long days at her house in Los Angeles. She was a good kid; Agatha liked her, despite the chaos she often brought.

Natasha's sister kept the dialogue flowing between Agatha, Benito, Kate, and Wanda. Agatha caught up on many things that the years had kept her from knowing — about everything, about the farm, about Rio, Wanda, and Natasha. It was sad that it was like this, with a forced cordiality, but still, it was somewhat comforting.

Agatha hadn't spotted Riandra again. Though she refused to admit it, she'd spent a good amount of time nodding along, pretending to listen, while her eyes secretly scanned the room for her. But Rio was nowhere. Once more, Agatha felt she must have hallucinated her presence, as if she'd seen a ghost. The blend of frustration, anxiety, and sadness had been building since she arrived in Darby — since that initial sighting at the racetrack.

Wanda was talking about the school, about the construction Rio had started for a major expansion; from what Agatha understood, ever since they had introduced the free teaching of four languages into the curriculum — another one of Riandra's boons who apparently spared no expense for the town's growth — many families from other cities were moving there, and Agatha found it funny, even ironic, that Darby was becoming a reference in education. She was about to pay a compliment when she saw the short-haired brunette, the same woman who was near Rio at the wake with her hand on her shoulder.

She was a beautiful woman, Agatha had to admit. She was tall and slender, her brown hair casually cropped at the nape of her neck, and her skin tanned. She carried herself with poise, had broad shoulders, and wore a suit, yet she exuded strong femininity. Agatha didn't realize how long she had been staring, trying to read the woman, until their eyes briefly met. The woman's eyes were brown, like Rio's, though not quite as captivating, but she was, undeniably, a striking woman. After a few seconds, Agatha looked away, grabbed a glass of rosé from the side table, and turned to Yelena:

"Who is that?" Agatha whispered to Yelena mid-conversation. The blonde momentarily stopped listening to Wanda, turning her head in the direction Agatha had indicated.

"That's Olivia," Yelena replied with zero subtlety. Agatha nearly let out a curse but forced herself to maintain a neutral expression, even as everyone else turned to see who she'd inquired about. "She's awesome, I love her," Yelena added with genuine affection, taking a swig of her beer. Agatha managed a forced smile.

"Olivia Benson," Wanda clarified with a slight smile, knowing her sister-in-law's attention span was akin to a goldfish's. "She's the town sheriff, transferred here three years ago."

"Was she close to Javier?" Agatha asked, affecting a lack of knowledge. Wanda nodded, sipping her drink.

"She and Rio are very close," Wanda said, looking at the brunette with a certain warmth that sparked a twinge of jealousy in Agatha. "Javier adored her; everyone does. She's been really good for Riandra."

"That's wonderful," Agatha said. Despite the knot in her throat, her tone was sincere; it was a relief to know Rio had someone there for her, that she wasn't completely isolated. "It's important, especially at a time like this."

"I saw her once," Benito commented casually. "I didn't realize she and Rio were close."

"It's not like you know much about your sister," Yelena commented, completely oblivious to the wide-eyed glares directed at her.

Benito opened and closed his mouth, casting his eyes downward. Agatha saw Wanda's discreet pinch on Yelena's forearm, which made the blonde jump, her eyes widening:

"What?" she demanded, turning to Wanda, who fixed her with a severe frown. "What did I do?"

"So, Agatha," Wanda, whose face immediately softened from the scowl she’d aimed at Yelena, turned sweetly back to Agatha. Agatha almost laughed, knowing Wanda was desperately trying to derail the subject and dissipate the grey cloud that had settled over their already somber day. "How are things in California?"

"Great," she answered automatically, forcing a smile and taking a long drink of her rosé. She wished she could drown in that glass. "Good as usual."

"Your wife couldn't make it?" Wanda asked casually. As Agatha swallowed the wine, it felt like she was ingesting a glass full of shards of glass.

She felt Benito's hand gently, discreetly, pressing against the small of her back in a silent show of support. She focused on that grounding touch, preventing herself from spiraling into the turmoil inside.

"I had to accept years ago that my marriage is a ménage à trois: me, her, and her work," Agatha replied with false charm, swirling the wine in her glass, her eyes fixed on the swirling liquid. "She genuinely wanted to come. Javier was always very present in our lives, at our home, and she was very fond of him, but unfortunately, she couldn't."

"That's a pity," Wanda said, her eyebrows furrowed in genuine sympathy. Agatha suppressed a nervous laugh but kept her mask intact. "He never spoke much about her, but I can only imagine how close they must have been; he was always with you."

The more Wanda talked and asked about Tessa and shared family moments involving Javier, the more Agatha felt overwhelmed. The worst of it was knowing Wanda was doing this with good intentions, genuinely believing in the facade— in Agatha's successful marriage, her happiness, her picture-perfect family living the capitalist dream in a California penthouse. Everyone in Darby believed Agatha was living the dream, the fairy-tale life, everything she had ever wanted. Agatha felt the air was trapped in her lungs, as if she were expanding without end. Her ribs ached; she felt she might asphyxiate at any moment because she was utterly incapable of letting out.

"Are you alright?" This time, Kate interrupted. Her arms were around Nicholas's shoulders, but her eyes were filled with worry as she looked at Agatha. Agatha opened and closed her mouth a few times, putting her glass back on the side table.

"I feel a little dizzy," Agatha admitted, placing a hand on her head. "I think I need some air."

"Do you want me to—" Benito offered, but she cut him off with a shake of her head.

"No need," she said, already moving away. "I'll be right back."

***

Rio couldn't gauge how long she'd been sitting on the porch swing, her face buried in her hands, systematically emptying the built-in stone cooler — her father's cooler, his brand of beer. She hated the stuff; it was thin and watery, and she used to complain every time he brought home a case. Now, she would give anything just to share one more of those beers with him, talking away the night right here, side by side, as they always did.

"I am so sorry," she murmured to herself with a mocking voice and her face a grimace of pain. "Hijos de puta! He was my father," Rio growled, venomously talking to the air. "You're sorry? What about what I feel? What do you think I feel?" She threw one of the charred logs from the dead fire pit as far as she could in a fit of solitary rage. "He was my father."

"I really am sorry."

That voice. Rio's entire body went rigid instantly. She would recognize that voice anywhere, no matter how much time passed. Rio stood up and squeezed the beer bottle so tightly that the tendons in her fingers turned white, but aside from that, she couldn't move, she didn't turn, she didn't look back. She stood, frozen, staring out at the lawn, the Harkness house in the distance, the dirt road. Her eyes scanned everything in sight, afraid to make any movement that might break the spell. She wouldn't turn; she didn't want to.

"I am so sorry for your loss, Rio." Rio inhaled deeply. The voice was clearer now, closer, and her hands began to tremble, her vision blurring. "When I heard, you were the first person I thought of, because you're right." Rio swallowed hard, still rigidly still; she couldn't blink, couldn't budge. "He was my godfather, my second father, a grandfather to my son, a best friend to my mother and Lilia, a benefactor to this whole town, but he was your father. More than that, he was your soulmate, your best friend, your hero. I've never witnessed anything like the connection you two shared." Agatha fell silent, waiting for a response, but Rio offered none. "Every single time he spoke about you, his eyes would shine." Rio felt a bitter desire to laugh, but suppressed it. "You were his sun and stars, his greatest pride, his deepest love. Benito lost his father too, but I know it's different. Because you, Rio, you lost a piece of your soul."

Rio stayed put, letting the silence stretch as tears silently tracked down her face. Perhaps, if she waited long enough, Agatha would simply leave, as she always did. After all, leaving was what she did best.

Agatha. God, it had been so long since she'd thought about her. That wound was scarred over, finally, after years of being inflamed and open. She had finally managed to close that wound.

Still facing away, Rio let out a heavy sigh, finished the remaining beer in quick, desperate gulps, and threw the bottle, hearing the glass shatter dramatically across the open field.

Who did she think she was?

Letting out a weak, scornful laugh, Rio spun around, shaking her head, intending to grab a fresh beer, but she froze the moment she faced the porch. Agatha was still there.

After thirteen years, she was here, standing right in front of her. Agatha, with her blue eyes that resembled a storm-tossed sea breaking on sharp rocks, her eyebrows knitted in a silent plea for surrender, clutching herself. She stared at Rio with an almost palpable anguish, a look so deeply painful it sent a shiver down Rio's entire body. Agatha, flesh and blood, on her porch.

Of course, she knew Agatha was there —  at the racetrack she thought it was hallucination, but at the cemetery, she was certain. She just hadn't believed Agatha would have the audacity to enter her house. But this was the celebration of her father's spirit; so she should have taken that into consideration. Of course, perfect, sweet Agatha, with her enormous heart and perfect life, filled with so much compassion, would come to console Rio on the farm—  this was so quintessentially Agatha that Rio felt stupid for not anticipating it.

"What in god's name are you still doing here?" Rio spat out harshly, her eyes narrowed at Agatha, who flinched immediately, her mouth opening and closing soundlessly.

"Rio, I—" Agatha took a large step closer, one arm tentatively outstretched as if to touch or hug her. Rio recoiled instinctively as if she were about to be punched.

"Seriously, what do you think you're doing?" Rio asked incisively, frowning, increasing the distance between them. She was utterly unmoved by the shadow of hurt that crossed Agatha's face. "Are you not finished? Is there another speech you need to give?"

"I just wanted to talk to you," Agatha murmured in a thin voice. Rio lifted her shoulders dismissively.

"You just talked," she said bluntly. Agatha nodded, lowering her head. "You spent, I don't know, five or six minutes speaking, and I listened. Thank you so much for the words." She forced the last part out with a strained cordiality, watching the blue eyes narrow at her. "Is there anything else you feel you need to say?"

"I don't know, Rio." Agatha shrugged, letting out a small sigh and shaking her head. Her expression was melancholy, even sad, still profoundly distressed. Rio hated that Agatha was still so transparent to her after all these years. She hated feeling that familiar anguish as if it were her own. "You are my family. I wanted to speak with you, to know how you're feeling, if you need anything at all."

Rio frowned, tilting her head toward Agatha with an expression that was almost comically confused, and watched Agatha flinch at the loud, scornful laugh that erupted from her throat:

"I'm your family?" Rio scoffed, shaking her head. "It's been over ten years since we last spoke, thirteen years since you've set foot on this farm. You have a son I don't even know. This has been an entire lifetime, Agatha. What kind of family is that?"

"Rio—"

"I don't know you," Rio interrupted, her voice resolute. "You don't know me anymore. I appreciate your consideration for my feelings, but you can save your empathy for your mother, Lilia, and my brother. I am nothing to you, Agatha; you don't need to put on an act."

"I'm not acting, Riandra, I—"

"You've said quite enough, don't you think?" Rio interrupted, raising her voice, making Agatha flinch again. "You and I have had every necessary conversation, and you made it abundantly clear that we don't fit into each other's lives. I am nothing to you, Agatha. I know my father was important to you, and you were important to him, and this is his celebration. So pay your respects and get out, because my father is dead, this house is mine now, and you are not my family. I don't want you here."

"Is everything alright?" The charged staring match between the brown and blue eyes was broken by Natasha's voice. Rio stepped even further away from Agatha, turning toward her friend, who stood with her wife in the porch doorway.

"Yes, everything is perfectly fine," Rio said, her voice instantly calm. She glanced back at Agatha, who was staring at her with wide eyes, completely frozen, like a stone statue. "I haven't seen Agatha in about ten years, can you believe it? We were just catching up, but she's just leaving now, right?"

"Yes," Agatha replied, her voice distant, still bewildered. "I was, I—I need to, I—"

"Nicholas is in the living room with Kate, let's go," Wanda said sweetly, holding out her arm toward Agatha, who, still stiff, moved toward her.

As soon as they were out of sight and through the porch door, Rio opened another beer, arching an eyebrow as she noticed Natasha staring at her, arms crossed.

"What?" Rio asked with false innocence.

"That was low, even for you," Natasha stated. Rio rolled her eyes and took a long swig of beer.

"She'll be sad for the rest of the day, and by tomorrow morning, she'll be on the road with her perfect little boy, heading back to her perfect wife, in her perfect mansion in the City of Angels where every dream comes true, far away from the mud, the weeds, and the country bumpkins of the farm." She spat the words out with bitterness, but Natasha continued to stare, an eyebrow arched. "I lost my father, Natasha. I have the right to be an asshole for a few days."

"That was fucked up, Rio," Natasha said, shaking her head. "I'm not going to give you a pass this time; I already told you. I know you're hurting, but that doesn't give you the right to hurt other people."

"If you're going to lecture me, I'd prefer to be alone," Rio murmured, turning her back and leaning against the porch railing again, knowing that her best friend, unlike Agatha, would willingly grant her space.

***

As the bedroom's bathroom door swung open, Natasha caught the delightful scent she adored. She looked up from her book, breaking into a lazy smile upon seeing her wife in a nightgown, smoothing lotion onto her neck and arms, then bending to apply it to her legs.

"I know you're staring," Wanda called out, smiling from the bathroom. Natasha let out a soft laugh, set her book on the nightstand, and scooted further into the middle of the bed for a better view.

"Of course I'm staring," she replied with a silly grin, watching Wanda prop a leg on the sink while continuing to lotion, rolling her eyes playfully at Natasha. Even after all these years, she still felt like a lovesick teenager around her; she knew she had won the lottery. "Don't you think it would be smarter to take the nightgown off? It'd be easier to cover your whole body."

The lotion bottle was launched in her direction. Natasha laughed, catching it easily, twirling the tube of cream in her hands. It was the almond and honey one, her favorite; Wanda knew that, which was exactly why she used it daily. Natasha’s silly smile stretched even wider. She loved being a married woman.

"How can you even think about that after today?" Wanda asked, drawing her eyebrows together as she came toward the bed. As soon as she settled under the covers, Natasha wrapped an arm around her, snuggling her close.

"It's just that you smell so good," she teased, wiggling her eyebrows and burying her face in the curve of Wanda's neck. This earned her several light slaps from her wife, who, despite the faux complaint, had her lips curved in a smile and was gently stroking the back of Natasha's neck.

"I'm serious," Wanda insisted. Noticing her serious gaze, Natasha adjusted herself to face her. "That was... Intense."

"What exactly?" Natasha questioned. Between the burial and Riandra throwing up drunk on a horse, so many difficult things had happened throughout the day she truly couldn't keep track.

"Agatha." The name made Natasha wince. "It would have been less painful if Rio had just slapped her face."

"Can you really blame her?" Natasha countered, her face screwing up like a naughty child caught in the act. Wanda’s eyes widened in reprimand. "Honey, I'm not saying Rio was right, I told her myself it was fucked up as hell, but can you honestly put yourself in her place?"

"Oh, here we go again," Wanda muttered, rolling her eyes and leaning back against the headboard. "She could commit a murder in front of you, and you'd still find a way to defend her."

"You know that's not true," Natasha murmured, shaking her head. "You're the one who seems to have empathy for everyone except her."

Wanda huffed, and Natasha sighed deeply. Lately, it had been very difficult to talk about Rio.

"It was awful, I know it was. I told her that," Natasha continued under her wife's close, analytical gaze. "I didn't defend her actions; I didn't coddle her. But Wanda, for god's sake, I can understand why she feels this way." Wanda arched an eyebrow, silently asking for an explanation. "When was the last time Benito was actually here?" Her wife's green eyes drifted away for a moment, and when she looked back with no answer, Natasha lifted her own eyebrow, emphasizing her point. "Agatha hasn't set foot in Darby for thirteen years, and Rio barely sees her brother. Yet, despite both of them showing no interest in being here, Javier was in California almost every month, always singing their praises, treating Benito like prince charming and Agatha like a saint. How do you think that makes Rio feel? All she ever wanted was to make her father proud, but he was always pointing something about her."

"It wasn't like that, honey." Wanda countered, shaking her head. "He had high expectations for her, yes, and he criticized because he wanted the best. But he was obsessed with her; sometimes it felt like Benito didn't even exist. Everyone knew his constant travelling was overcompensating; he never connected with him like he did with Rio. How often did you hear him talk about his Benny? It was always Riandra, everything Riandra. Some people worked with him for years thinking she was an only child."

"Of course he played favorites. Benito had to be dragged by the hair to come here. Money was never the most important thing to Javier; he loved the farm, just like Rio. He never had that bond with Benito; the first chance the boy got to leave, he took it."

"You are being completely biased."

"I am not!"

"He wanted to go to college," Wanda insisted firmly. "And she resented him for that; it was Rio who cut him off."

"With all the money they have, Wanda, with all the schools in the world, that boy could have gone to Switzerland if he wanted, anywhere, but he had to go to California? He had to go to the same place as her?"

"Whether Riandra likes it or not, Agatha is part of her family. They stopped talking, but Agatha never stopped being present in Benito's life; they've exchanged letters since he was a child and she left for California. She's always been important to him, too." Natasha rubbed her forehead and sighed, but Wanda, avoiding a fight, stroked her face. "She was hurt by his choice; she's the one who retreated. She pushed him away, and all Javier wanted was to bring his two kids back together, to bring everyone back together."

"She was left alone, honey," Natasha said, shaking her head. She knew Rio's actions were wrong, but she also understood the depth of her pain. "Every time Javier went to Los Angeles and spent weeks with Benito, with Agatha, her wife, and her kid, Rio stayed here, completely alone. When her father got sick, she took care of him alone. Yes, the way she spoke to Agatha was really screwed up, but in three days at most, Agatha and Benito will fly back to California, and Rio will be here, alone again, and this time, her father isn't coming back home."

Wanda sighed heavily, taking her wife's hands between hers and raising them to her lips, pressing a long kiss against her skin. Their eyes met, and Wanda’s eyebrows lifted in shared understanding.

"She's not alone; she has us," Wanda promised, and Natasha sighed again. "She will always have us, our boys, Yelena. She's part of our family."

"You could still be easier on her."

"I love her, you know that," Natasha nodded. "She's family to me; she's the godmother to both our sons. I love her deeply, and that's exactly why I can't stay silent, Natasha." Seeing Wanda's eyes well up, Natasha looked down. "I love her too much to watch her destroy her own life. I refuse to go through this again; I don't want to bury another Vidal."

"Don't even say that," Natasha whispered, closing her eyes tightly.

"You're the one who needs to stop being silent, who shouldn't go easy on her, because out of everyone, you're the only one she truly listens to."

"She just needs time."

"You sound exactly like Lilia; you two are blind," Wanda muttered in frustration, but seeing her wife's sad look, she sighed, shaking her head in a silent apology for raising her voice. "She needs help."

"She was fine, she truly was. She had stopped drinking; you know she did!" Wanda nodded with a sigh. "But then he got sick, and she—" Natasha buried her face in her hands. This time, it was Wanda who nestled her wife against her chest, kissing her head.

"I'm sorry. I shouldn't have said that," she murmured into Natasha's hair. "I know she'll be okay; she has us. She recovered once; she will recover again. She'll be okay."

Chapter End Notes
