Chapter 11: home

Chapter Summary

well, it's like what they say: home is where your heart is

Chapter Notes

i put some warnings in the tags about sensitive topics that would be addressed in the story, and this is where we start getting into sensitive stuff like ableism, domestic abuse, violence, and emotional manipulation. in the next chapters, we'll be dealing with alcoholism. i'll always drop a trigger warning so you can read carefully if these are personally sensitive subjects for you.

this one was a hard one write in english but i hope the writing is ok

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"I can go anywhere I want, anywhere I want, just not home and you can aim for my heart, go for blood, but you would still miss me in your bones" – My Tears Ricochet, Taylor Swift

In her bath robe, twisting her hair up in the towel, Wanda bit her lips in a smile at the wink from her wife, who was leaning against one of the counters in just her pajama shorts and a gray top, blowing on a steaming mug of coffee in her hands. As Wanda moved closer to get her own cup, a soft laugh escaped her when Natasha’s arms wrapped possessively around her waist. A contented grunt rumbled from her wife as she buried her face in her neck.

"You smell so good," Natasha murmured, inhaling deeply and tracing her nose along Wanda's skin.

Wanda offered a soft laugh in return, allowing herself to melt into the embrace. She tenderly stroked Natasha's back, running her fingers lightly over the faint red marks left by her nails. She couldn't help but roll her eyes at her own habit of unconsciously marking her wife, like an overeager teenager.

"Natasha..." she warned softly as she felt her wife's hands slide up her thighs beneath the robe. Natasha had the nerve to pull back just an inch, meeting Wanda's gaze with an expression of feigned innocence while giving her a gentle squeeze. "The boys will wake up any minute."

"Our boys?" Natasha scoffed with a disbelieving laugh. "They sleep like logs. You know they won't get up before noon unless we wake them."

"They certainly get that from you," Wanda muttered with an eye roll, giving her wife a light push. Natasha reluctantly unwrapped her arms. To reward the compliance, Wanda gave her a quick, chaste kiss. "Will you be able to pick them up from school today? I’ll need to stay all day," she asked, pouring coffee.

"I’ll talk to Yelena," Natasha replied with a yawn, and Wanda raised an eyebrow at her, settling onto a counter stool. "We're hosting that Chilean businessman at the winery today. I have no idea how long we'll be stuck in that protracted discussion; Rio is not eager to close the deal."

"Why?" Wanda asked, interested. "You said she wants to expand into South America. It would be a good deal, wouldn't it?"

"He wants a large monthly supply of barrels for his hotel chain, which is great, but he also insists on the artisanal wines, and you know how she is," Natasha sighed, rolling her eyes. "If we sell the artisanal wines in bulk, they stop being artisanal, they just become signature wines."

"She has a point," Wanda murmured with a shrug. "It’s not like she needs the business. He’s definitely more interested than she is." Natasha nodded at this.

"Benito is pushing to close the deal, but Rio will only agree if he sticks to the signature wines. She refuses to compromise or restrict the production of the artisanal stuff." Wanda furrowed her brow, sympathetic to the bored tone of her wife, who constantly had to mediate between the Vidal siblings. "Having Benito here should make it easier to decide quickly. Otherwise, we'd go through the whole visit, discuss everything, have to relay it all to him, and then deal with endless back-and-forth emails and phone calls, as if them hiring a New York law firm wasn't headache enough." Natasha groaned in annoyance, and Wanda offered a quiet laugh. "When we finally close a deal, we still have to send it to them, and then it comes back to us, and—" She cut herself off with a heavy sigh, ignoring the small, amused smile of her wife, who watched her with a mixture of admiration and affection. "Honestly, I know it’s unrealistic to centralize everything in Darby, but things would be so much easier if we could."

"Have you talked to her about that? About centralizing this more administrative part here?"

"Yes, and we're trying to figure it out, maybe getting one or two key people from each department based here to manage the scattered communications, but it’s complicated. Even with all the money, it’s not everyone who wants so settle in Darby," Natasha admitted with a shrug. Wanda stood up and walked over to gently caress her face. "And if we do that, Benito would be the first one who’d have to stay here."

Wanda's eyes widened momentarily. She exchanged a knowing, significant look with her wife, who simply nodded in confirmation.

"How is she handling with him being here?" Wanda asked, her tone careful.

"She looks like she's about to spontaneously combust at any moment," Natasha replied with a sigh. Wanda let out an involuntary, apologetic puff of laughter, shaking her head. "She hasn't even been sleeping at home; she stayed at the winery office yesterday."

"They still haven't talked?"

"Not that I know of. She’s avoiding him like the plague, and the poor boy keeps wandering around asking about her everywhere," Natasha muttered with a shrug. Wanda shook her head and took a sip of her coffee.

"It’s been five days now, hasn't it?" Natasha nodded, and Wanda let out a heavy sigh. "He hasn't mentioned leaving, has he?"

"No, he's just staying, handling things from here." She lifted her shoulders. "I know she hates it, but honestly, it’s much more easier with him here."

"I get why he wants to stay," Wanda said with a sympathetic look. "They're the only family they have left. She's going to have to talk to him eventually."

"I know, but..." Natasha let out a weary sigh, and Wanda clearly saw the worry clouding her face. "It’s Rio," she said, as if that explained everything.

It was darkly amusing how Rio often caused them more stress than their own children.

"She's getting worse, though, isn't she?" Wanda pressed. Natasha tilted her head, clearly reluctant to answer.

"Honey, it's been less than a week since her father died. Of course, she's struggling."

"The point is, she'll keep spiraling if she continues to run from everything. She can’t hide forever," Wanda said with exasperation. Natasha nodded, always choosing her words carefully where Rio was concerned. "What if he decides to stay for good? He has every right to; it’s his home too, and it's completely understandable that this loss would make him want to reconnect and establish roots here."

"Do you seriously think that’s a possibility? Benito staying on the farm?" Natasha's tone was purely disbelieving, but Wanda simply maintained an impassive expression and nodded, causing her wife's eyes to widen for a moment as she considered the idea. "I honestly just can’t picture it."

"They are the only family left; it's just the two of them now. Rio is all Benito has. You know Javier only ever wanted them to get along again, and I believe Benito wants that too, that’s why he hasn’t left." Natasha nodded, considering this, and Wanda pressed on. "They're actually quite similar. They've both spent years running away from this mess between them. Sometimes, a tragedy can lead to unexpected good things. Maybe he wants to stop running and finally fix their relationship."

"I have no idea what Rio thinks. I doubt she's even entertaining the possibility of him staying; she's definitely just expecting him to disappear any moment now. She doesn't want him here."

"But the farm belongs to him too, and he's her brother. Do you think he's wrong for wanting to stay and reconnect with her?"

“No, of course not,” Natasha denied vehemently, agreeing with Wanda. "I just think Rio isn't emotionally available for that right now."

"She's never available for anything, Natasha! All she knows how to do is escape and suppress any feeling that causes her the slightest discomfort." Wanda's irritation was now palpable, and Natasha sighed. "That's exactly why she's spiraling and drinking compulsively, because instead of confronting her problems, she pretends they don't exist and nothing affects her."

"Baby, it hasn't even been a week," Natasha murmured softly, the exasperation clear in her own voice. "She needs time."

"She always needs time."

“This time she really does,” Natasha defended. It was Wanda's turn to sigh as she settled back onto the counter stool.

"I think if Rio would just open up a little, so much good could come out of this," Wanda said, her tone tired yet compassionate. Natasha knit her brows, watching her. "She won't admit it, but she bottles everything up, and it clearly hurts. Everyone can see the pain, and that's why she acts this way. Javier’s death is an immense loss, a wound that will take a long time to heal, but she could finally move past all that bitterness, not just toward Benito, but toward Agatha, too."

"Agatha?" Natasha asked, confused.

"She's still here," Wanda confirmed, and Natasha nearly choked on her coffee.

"In Darby? She didn't leave?"

"No. I saw her at the market yesterday afternoon with her son," Wanda recounted. Natasha's eyebrows pinched together in a mixture of shock and confusion. "She didn’t look well, either."

“I had no idea she was still here. I'm sure Rio doesn't know, or she'd be complaining about it everywhere," Natasha murmured with a nervous chuckle. "What in the world is she doing here? Benito I can understand, but her?"

"I don't know, but I felt bad, you know?" Wanda said. Her wife stared at her, eyebrow arched, prompting a sigh. "Oh, honey, she looked so sad, so lost. I honestly think she’s struggling with something."

“Did you talk to her?”

"I did," Wanda admitted with a guilty grimace, and Natasha shook her head in disapproval. "She said everything was fine, but I didn’t buy it. You can see it in her eyes, you know? Something's off; she’s not okay. I told her if she needed to talk—"

"You didn't!" Natasha cut her off, sounding accusatory. Wanda let out a small groan. "Wanda!"

"Natasha!" she pleaded softly, full of pity, ignoring her wife’s protests. "We have no idea what's going on in her life. She could be going through something; she clearly isn't well."

"And what exactly does that have to do with us?"

"Natasha," her tone became one of gentle reprimand. Natasha responded with an unsatisfied grunt. "She was my best friend for years. Don't you see that something serious must be wrong? She stayed away for thirteen years, and now she shows up, bag and baggage, son in tow, and has been here for nearly a week? She didn’t mention leaving, only that she was taking some time to be here."

"That's just great," Natasha sighed, shaking her head. "Rio is going to lose it."

"She’s a grown woman! She needs to start getting over things! That's why I said if she makes the slightest effort, things could improve. She could finally drop these old grudges and move forward. I'm sure her father would have wanted that."

"If things keep spiraling like this, I'm the one who's going to need a stiff drink."

"Don't even start. Don't joke about that," Wanda murmured reprovingly, giving her wife's shoulder a light tap. Natasha merely rolled her eyes. "You should talk to her about it. She listens to you." The suggestion made Natasha's eyes widen, and she laughed nervously.

"Of course, it all comes back to me."

"You know we have four kids," Wanda said, a hint of a smile playing on her lips as she moved closer to her wife. Natasha, eyebrows arched in mild amusement, wrapped her arms around Wanda's waist. "Billy, Tommy, Yelena, and Riandra." Natasha let out a puff of laughter. "Of course, it always falls to us. They're ours."

Natasha shook her head slowly, then sealed her lips against Wanda's.

***

Agatha stared blankly at the wall calendar, unable to process that five days had passed since she arrived in Darby. The time hadn't flowed so much as stalled, leaving her frozen in a state of paralyzing inertia while the rest of the world spun on without her.

She existed on autopilot, her life a mechanical routine focused on caring for herself and Nicholas. Even in his typical silence, her son was already exhibiting signs that he knew something was wrong.

In stark contrast, Nicholas was thriving, enjoying the farm life immensely. Agatha would wake to find him already up, enjoying his grandmother's pies or sitting next to her on the porch, playing contentedly in the dirt with a wide smile. Sometimes Benito would take him off to explore the farmlands, and Nicholas would return with muddy boots and tired eyes, ready for a deep night's sleep. Despite her inner turmoil, seeing him wrapped in such serene happiness anchored a part of her, urging her to stay, even as another part screamed that she needed to leave as fast as possible — though she had no idea where she would go.

But Nicholas was worried about her. Agatha sensed it in the curious way his little brown eyes tracked her movements, as if he was waiting for her to speak, to explain. Yet, Agatha said nothing; like her son, she had retreated into silence. Her mother, Evanora, had also noticed her behavior, but knowing Agatha so well, she was wisely giving her space. Still, Agatha knew she couldn't postpone the truth forever. She couldn't spend another week pretending time wasn't moving and the outside world didn't exist. She knew it did, because for five days, her phone hadn't stopped ringing.

Though she kept it on silent, she occasionally saw the screen light up with yet another incoming call. Tessa was calling over sixty times a day, Alex was calling too, and Benito had already warned her that he had been forced to admit she was at the farm because Tessa was threatening to call the police over her sudden disappearance. Agatha knew chaos was reigning. Her delusion of a family vacation was over, but she wasn't ready to face it because she simply had no idea what to do.

In hindsight, she felt foolish for her decision: leaving the signed divorce papers on the table and loading all her belongings into the car. A part of her had desperately needed to do it — to finally make a decision for herself and stop accepting what she didn't deserve. Yet, she didn't have the courage to face the fallout. She felt lost and stupid, racing against the moment she would be cornered. What would she say? What could she possibly do? She had no answers, barely understanding her own feelings, paralyzed completely by fear.

But she couldn't continue this way. She couldn't keep pretending she didn't exist. She had to take control of the situation, even if she had no plan. For her own sake, and for Nicholas, she had to stand her ground. It was for him that she finally answered the phone the moment it vibrated on the table.

"Agatha?" Tessa's voice, strained with exasperation, cut through the line. Agatha closed her eyes tightly, knowing what was coming. "Agatha, are you insane? Do you think this is some kind of joke? Have you completely lost it?" Her wife was screaming, and Agatha swallowed hard.

A huge knot of insecurity formed in her throat, but she refused to back down. She couldn't, not after going this far.

"I was dying to hear your voice too, my love," she said, her tone dry and dripping with sarcasm.

"You think this is funny? You think this is a joke? I was about to call the police, Agatha! You vanished for five days without a single word!"

"I tried to call you more than twenty times, Tessa, but you didn't answer the phone."

"I was working!" she snapped. Agatha let out a small, disbelieving, sarcastic breath. "Just because I missed the damned phone call, you thought it was fine to pack up all your things and disappear off the face of the earth with my son without saying anything?"

"Our son. He’s my son, too. He grew inside me; he came from me. He is not your son alone. You barely even see the boy, despite living with him."

"Where is this coming from? Have you completely lost your mind? I barely see him? You must be joking! My whole life is spent working to give my son, to give you, the very best! And this is my thanks? You disappearing with my son? That is kidnapping, you know. I could report you, Agatha."

"Then why don't you?" she murmured tiredly, knowing it was unwise to provoke her further.

"What possessed you? Vanishing with Nicholas in the middle of a nervous breakdown and leaving divorce papers on my desk?"

"Have you signed them yet?"

"I am not signing a damn thing! I won't indulge this selfish freak-out! You are getting on the first flight I can book out of that goddamn place, coming home, and ending this charade! I am at my limit! Five days, Agatha! Even Alex didn't know where you were! I had to call Benito just to locate you. What if I had disappeared with our son for five days? You've blown up at me for far less, and I am still trying to be understanding."

"I am not coming home until you sign the divorce papers." Her voice was firm, contrasting sharply with the continuous trembling of her hands.

"You don't actually want this, Agatha. You know you don't."

"I know exactly what I want. I won't return until you grant me the divorce. If you want me back so badly, sign it, email it to me, and I’ll take the next flight to Los Angeles."

"You are my wife, Agatha," Tessa bit out, her harsh tone making Agatha's palms sweat and her eyes sting with unshed tears. "You are the mother of my son. We have a life together. I don't know what foolish idea you've gotten into your head, but I am not throwing years of my life away because of your selfish breakdown."

"Selfish?!"

"Shut the fuck up, I'm talking!" Tessa's shriek cut her off, and this time, Agatha's entire body shook. "You spent five days out in the sticks thinking you could do whatever you pleased while I worried sick like an idiot! Now you're going to listen!" She swallowed hard. "What is it you want, huh? Why this tantrum? What is missing this time? You don't have to throw a fit every time you want something; you can just talk to me! Unlike you, I try to listen! I've spent years doing exactly what you wanted! You must think I'm stupid, don't you?" Agatha remained silent, trembling, as she heard Tessa take a sharp breath. "I will not sign that damn divorce. If you want it so badly, you can petition for it in court, because I will not let you take half of what is mine. I will not let you keep my son. I suggest you take a deep breath and truly think about what you want."

"You are not taking my son away from me," was all Agatha could manage, grinding the words out through tears that now streamed down her face.

"Do you think I want to do that?" Tessa's tone completely changed now; she spoke softly, using the gentle voice Agatha knew so well, and Agatha squeezed her eyes shut to resist its effect. "Do you think I want to take him from you? Of course not. I'm not the monster here, Agatha; you are. You just disappeared. You took him with you. I am simply worried about my wife and my son, but if you refuse to come home, I will be forced to ensure you do. He is my son too."

"Tessa, I don't want anything of yours. I just want the divorce. I want to move on with my life. I'm not trying to run away with Nicholas; I just want my freedom. If you sign the papers, I will come home, and we can discuss how to resolve everything," she managed to counter, forcing a calm, soft tone despite her slight stutter and the tears choking her voice.

"And how exactly do you plan to rebuild your life, Agatha? Are you even listening to yourself? If I block your credit cards right now, what will you do? Beg your mother for cash? I will not allow my son to be supported by your pet hicks."

"Don't you dare talk about my family—"

"I'm talking about our family!" she thundered again. Agatha froze, swallowing hard as she heard Tessa take a deliberate breath. "Our family, my love. Me, you, and our son." Her voice softened again. "I don't know what got into you, what triggered this breakdown, but I want to resolve this the best way. I just want you to come home so we can talk."

"I don't want to talk, not like this, because I don't want to continue. I don't want to be married to you anymore. I don't want to live in the same house as you. I never want to see you again, Tessa. I know you're the mother of my son, but that is it. That is your sole role in my life. I won't accept anything more, ever again."

“You're stressed.” The condescending tone, the feigned affection designed to smooth over her insecurities, was painfully familiar.

Rationally, Agatha knew she shouldn't let it affect her, but she couldn't help it. She felt the immediate tightening in her chest, and her insecurity screamed louder.

"I'm very sorry about your godfather. Benito told me. I imagine you have so much on your mind right now, baby." She desperately wanted to scream at Tessa to never use that term again, but the words wouldn't come. "You didn't need to run away. You didn't need to break down. I would have been understanding. I would have understood your need to be with your family right now. You know I understand you; I am trying to understand you right now. Just breathe, okay?" Agatha inwardly cursed herself for taking a deep breath at that exact moment. "You're stressed, you're feeling a lot, but everything will be fine. We are going to be fine. I understand you need some space right now. I'll give you a few more days, okay?"

"Tessa—" she tried to speak, but was cut off by a drawn-out "shhhhh" from the other end.

"We are going to fix this." Despite the sweet tone, Agatha knew the underlying resolve in her voice. "You need time, and I will give you time. Stay a few more days, clear your head. I'm sure you'll come to your senses. I know you don't want us to fight. I know you don't want to fight with me."

"Tessa..."

"I'll call you later, okay? Think things through, baby. I love you."

The click of the line disconnecting coincided with a violent sob that wracked Agatha's entire body. She let herself fall onto the bed, burying her face in a pillow to muffle her crying.

She was utterly lost, with no idea how to escape the trap she was in.

***

Three Years Ago

Agatha understood Tessa's stance all too well; they had already fought countless times about their son's behavior. Nicholas was a sweet boy, but it was clear he faced significant challenges that required intervention. His failure to express himself, his tendency to clam up, his inability to interact with other children, and the way a simple trip to the mall could trigger explosive fits of rage and tears were not normal.

Nicholas had just turned three. Even as a first-time mother, Agatha knew his difficulties were beyond typical toddler behavior. No other parent they knew struggled this much. They had gone through over twenty-five nannies, none of whom could handle his specific needs and quirks. All Agatha wanted was to return to her career, but as it stood, all her time and attention had to be devoted to Nicholas — she was the only one who could manage him.

She neither could nor wanted to remain a housewife whose life singularly revolved around her son. Agatha loved Nicholas more than anything, but she was an individual, too. Her research confirmed that with proper support and treatment, he would improve, allowing both her and Tessa to return to their normal lives, free from conflict and sacrifice.

Tessa, however, was adamant: she refused to subject Nicholas to any psychological or psychiatric care. She saw no need for it, insisting, "He's just a child, Agatha; sometimes kids are difficult." But Agatha knew better. Beyond the daily exhaustion, she knew Tessa’s denial was actively harming their son.

Without her wife's knowledge, Agatha had scheduled an appointment for Nicholas and spent the afternoon speaking with a psychologist about his behavior, needs, peculiarities, and emotional struggles. He required a neuropsychological evaluation to get a definitive diagnosis, but a degree of autism was highly likely. With the right treatment, he could achieve a calm, socially integrated life. Now, she just had to convince Tessa — the seemingly impossible task.

"You did what?!" Tessa practically screamed, hands on her hips. Agatha let out an exasperated sigh.

"Could you please keep your voice down?" she requested, giving her a look hard enough to make Tessa cross her arms. "I just put him to sleep."

"We discussed this a thousand times! How dare you completely override my decision without talking to me first?"

"Because I knew you would absolutely refuse! This isn't a choice, Tessa; this is a medical issue, a treatment like any other, and one he clearly needs."

"My son is not some kind of retard, Agatha!" she snapped sharply, causing Agatha's eyes to widen. "Everything nowadays is about putting a label on children, for God's sake! They are kids! Yes, he's difficult, but he's a child! You need patience!"

"And where is your patience? You aren't the one spending all day with him. You aren't here to witness what he needs!" At this, Tessa gave a dry, dismissive laugh, shaking her head.

"And why am I not here, Agatha?" she asked rhetorically, arms still crossed. "Do you think I enjoy spending all day at the office, or days on end at the courthouse? I've been working two jobs since you quit to ensure we maintain our lifestyle, so that we, and our son, want for absolutely nothing."

"As if we don't have enough money," Agatha scoffed dismissively. "You don't need two jobs, Tessa, you know that."

"Oh, right, because the bills pay themselves, don't they? The bills, Nicholas’s daycare—which, by the way, is the most exclusive in all of California, and you chose it," Tessa listed, counting on her fingers. "Your new Porsche, my new car, our trip to Hong Kong, your postgraduate degree that you insisted on pursuing even without working. The nannies, the music lessons—because heaven forbid a three-year-old doesn't have an extra activity—the dresses you buy weekly, your wine collection... shall I keep going?"

"Tessa, this isn't about extravagance," Agatha pleaded, sighing. "Why do you think we cycle through nannies so quickly? Why does no one stay? He has genuine behavioral problems, which are manageable and treatable. It will benefit his development and, frankly, our relationship."

"If you can't handle one child, Agatha, go back to work, and I'll stay home with him," Tessa said with a cold shrug. The words felt like a slap.

"Are you suggesting I'm not a good mother to our son?"

"You are the one claiming a three-year-old boy needs psychological help! You sound desperate to be rid of him. Good heavens, Agatha! He's a child!"

"A child who needs psychological care," she emphasized, and Tessa laughed, shaking her head.

"Perhaps you are the one who needs the help."

"What did you just say?"

"He is a child, Agatha." Her expression was now as serious as her tone, and Agatha swallowed hard, shrinking back. "And he is a wonderful child. He’s smart, expressive, talented, and ahead of all his peers. He's a prodigy. Yes, he has boundless energy, he's restless, and he struggles to express himself, but that is simply because he is a child. He is not broken or crazy; he just needs attention."

"Tessa, he need—"

"I am finished arguing about this." Tessa interrupted her, raising a hand to silence her. Agatha took a deep breath, shaking her head. "If you continue this line of thinking, I'll be the one booking you a psychiatrist, or having you committed, I don't know, because you are clearly projecting your own frustrations onto a three-year-old. He needs a good mother, not a doctor."

***

Present Day

Overwhelmed by a torrent of thoughts, Agatha could barely breathe, the stress making the basement feel suddenly claustrophobic. She needed air, clarity, and control. She couldn't let Tessa get into her mind like that.

She needed to talk to Lilia, Benito, Kate — anyone who truly understood her situation. She needed reassurance that she was doing the right thing.

Agatha was just about to slip through the back door when a silhouette in her mother's kitchen made her freeze. She pressed herself against the wall, hidden by the door crack, her heart hammering so hard it felt lodged in her throat. It was Riandra.

Rio, in the flesh: hair pulled back, wearing a red flannel shirt, jeans, and muddy boots, hauling two enormous buckets overflowing with fresh grapes.

Six Years Ago

Even with her sunglasses on, Rio squinted against the sun’s glare. She wrinkled her nose at the salty breeze, which arrived thick with the scent of sweat and sunscreen, accompanied by a cacophony of loud voices and screaming children. God, she hated California.

She couldn't recall exactly when she had last been there. It was likely a span of time somewhere between less than she would have liked and more than she could fathom — time had never been her forte, and the exact gap in her memory remained elusive. If it were up to her, she would never set foot in Los Angeles voluntarily. But business was business, and she was an adult now. Or so she told herself, knowing perfectly well she was here by choice. She had chosen to come; a part of her needed to.

Her professional cover was the grand opening of Casa Vidal, a milestone for the family empire. Years ago, when she’d first dreamed of opening a wine bar in Darby — just a simple place for people to taste the family’s labels — she never imagined it would evolve into a multi-million dollar franchise. Rio had only wanted a distraction, a venture of her own to fuel her passion for winemaking. She loved the raw, unsophisticated nature of large tastings, but the success of Pedra Púrpura had sparked a rare glint of pride in her father’s eyes. So, she refined the concept. Soon, their small vinotecas were popping up across the country and then the world. What began as a hobby was now a lucrative cornerstone of the business, pairing her wines with haute cuisine. Naturally, Los Angeles had to be on the map; it was the ultimate stage for ostentatious high society.

Despite her disdain for the city, she actually liked the local vinoteca. It was airy and open, sitting right on the beach. If not for the snobbery of the clientele, she might have felt at home. She had been there for thirty minutes, echoing a visit from the day before, and she was still receiving the same judgmental looks. In a venue this A-list, her simple tank top, heavy boots, and faded jeans were practically an insult to the patrons. Nobody but the staff knew she owned the place, a fact that amused her more than it should. Their arrogance was ridiculous; she took pride in the success and her father’s approval, but the money was just a byproduct. She did it for the love of the craft.

But the truth was, Casa Vidal wasn’t the reason she was here. Rio nodded to the waiter. "A bottle of Perle D'Or and a pairing appetizer, please." As the waiter retreated, she caught Alex watching her with a suppressed grin. "Is there something on my face, Miss Sunshine?"

Alex just shook her head, hiding her face behind the menu as they both dissolved into laughter.

Their friendship had always been an anomaly. No one expected Agatha’s best friend and Rio to hit it off, yet it had been inevitable. During Agatha’s college years, Alex would often tag along on trips back to Darby. From the moment she stepped onto the ranch, she and Rio had fallen into a rhythm of sibling-like bickering. Agatha found it amusing but never expected it to last; but the truth was that, even without her knowledge or consent, they actually became friends — they had forged a genuine, lasting connection.

Alex had always been the lone impartial judge in the messy Rio-Agatha-Tessa triangle. Though she was the one who originally introduced Agatha to her current wife, her sympathies had secretly leaned the other way. She had spent years mediating that disaster, trying to salvage something good from the wreckage. Even when Agatha finally chose a path — one Alex firmly disagreed with —, her bond with Rio remained. For that, Riandra was profoundly grateful. Alex was a good friend — someone she could be herself with, free from judgment or the fear of being vulnerable. Despite the distance and the silence between visits, Alex was family.

"You look good," Alex said, breaking the silence as she studied her. Rio offered a faint, appreciative nod. "Calm, at peace. It’s good to see you like this."

"Life has been good; I don’t have much to complain about," Rio admitted, and she meant it. "I just got back from France. I was looking at horses and planning a few litters when I got invited to a racing circuit in Amiens." She settled into her chair with a quiet sigh, resting her arm against a white pillar overlooking the beach. "I’m heading back to Darby today to check on the house and run the ranch for a few weeks. Then I'll finish my racing season and head back to France to race."

Alex pressed her lips together, a proud smile tugging at the corners. "Who would’ve thought Rio Vidal would become such a world traveler?"

Rio shrugged, shaking her head. "You know it’s not by choice. I’d rather be at the ranch a thousand times over. But that was my father’s condition, if I wanted the business, I had to take the reins myself." She let her gaze drift toward the horizon, where the blue sea met the sky. "It’s ironic, isn't it?"

"Ironic?" Alex played dumb, propping her face in her hands with an air of mock confusion. "I don’t follow. What’s ironic?"

Rio let out a soft huff. "You know exactly what I mean."

"Actually, I don’t," Alex countered, her grin widening. "Tell me. What’s so ironic, Rio?"

Rio ran her tongue along the inside of her cheek, eyeing her friend's mask of faux innocence. Alex was waiting for her to say it — she always forced Rio to say it out loud. Finally, Rio gave in with a weak laugh.

"It’s ironic that I’m the one jet-setting across the globe while Agatha is stuck in a suburban mansion, in the same city for a decade, married with a baby on the way."

Alex’s expression shifted, her eyebrows rising in deep, firsthand empathy. She opened her mouth to respond, but the waiter arrived, setting down two glasses, the wine, and a platter of cheeses and walnut bread.

"A toast to life," Rio said, grateful for the interruption. Alex nodded and clinked her glass against Rio's.

"What am I drinking?" Alex asked, savoring the first sip as she watched the pale, golden-yellow liquid swirl slowly in her glass, its viscosity catching the light.

"You tell me," Rio challenged, crossing her arms. Alex tilted her head, taking another thoughtful sip as she weighed the flavors.

"I know this is one of your own," Alex said, offering the compliment with a smile. Rio nodded faintly. "White, smooth... is that honey?" Rio nudged her to continue. "It’s sweet, but with a bitter finish."

"Impressive," Rio said, clapping as Alex rolled her eyes. "It’s a white infused with orange blossom, acacia honey, dried apricots, and ripe quinces. The bitterness comes from toasted almonds, though the honey softens the edge. Twelve months of aging in stainless steel tanks."

"It’s incredible, silky, like velvet," Alex hummed in satisfaction. She took another sip, adding suggestively, "I know a certain wine collector who would kill for a bottle of this." Rio rolled her eyes. She knew exactly who she meant.

Agatha.

Rio hadn't been lying; her life was good. Despite those who had doubted her ambition, she had proven more than capable of managing the family empire alongside her father. She had matured and found her purpose where she always knew it lived: on the ranch, watching her family's legacy thrive.

In the early days, back when she was in a rough patch, Rio had found herself in the vineyards and discovered a passion for the art of creation. She lacked formal training and the technical expertise of her father’s food engineers, but she possessed an intuitive nose — a true gift. From her very first wine, Javier knew she was born for it. Eventually, alongside her horses business, she took over as creative director and master blender for Casa Vidal.

She was happy with the direction her life had taken. Of course, she didn't enjoy the constant travel or the countless business meetings she had to endure almost every day, but she had the privilege of spending most of her time on the ranch, at her winery, working every day at what she loved. She still had enough time to race and get her hands dirty in the soil, just as she liked — working side-by-side with most of the ranch hands.

By any objective measure, she had built a perfect life. She had success, respect, and the freedom to pursue her passions. She cherished the quiet afternoons spent working with her father, she had good friends, loved her animals, and took pride in expanding the ranch — growing the crops, sparking new ideas, and creating with her team. She had everything she wanted, yet a persistent sense of isolation remained.

Beyond the ranch, what she loved most in life was her family, but for years she had felt a lingering melancholy at how it had fragmented. She had a family, but she no longer felt like a part of it. Lilia and Evanora were constants, but her mother and Richard were gone. Benito had left for California and never truly came back; even her father, her closest companion, now split his time between the business and Benito’s new life. And then there was Agatha, who had vanished out of her life for seven years.

There was no bitterness in Rio’s loneliness, only a deep, quiet ache. She missed them all. It was why she occasionally fled to California and into Alex’s company. She wanted to know about them, even though she felt that family was no longer hers to hold, she cared for them from a distance — including Agatha.

"She was here yesterday with your father," Alex murmured. Her tone was casual, but her blue eyes studied Rio with a careful, searching intensity.

"I know. I saw them," Rio replied, meeting her gaze. Alex arched an eyebrow while taking a bite of walnut bread. "They didn't see me," Rio clarified with a wry smile, prompting a disappointed sigh from her friend. "I was in a taxi on my way here when I spotted them. I just told the driver to keep going. My father has no idea I’m here; he thinks I’m still in France."

"Things would be much simpler if you just talked to them, wouldn't they?" Alex countered with a pointed look. Rio rolled her eyes, a ghost of a smile touching her lips as she took another sip of wine. "They miss you, Rio. Your brother does and so does she."

"She looks huge," Rio said softly, almost to herself. She smiled at the horizon for a moment before turning back to Alex. "How far along is she?"

"Seven months," Alex replied, dabbing her mouth with a napkin. "It's a boy."

Rio’s smile lingered, her gaze turning distant as she tried to picture Agatha holding a son. She finally let out a long sigh. "How is she?”

"It was rough at first," Alex admitted, shaking her head before taking a sip. "She wasn't sure about this. Tessa was the one who pushed for them to try, and when the first few attempts failed, Agatha took it hard." She paused, seeing Rio nod for her to go on. "But lately, ever since she found out it was a boy, she’s been glowing. I haven’t seen her this happy in years." Alex sighed, "Ironic, isn't it?"

Rio frowned. "What?"

"Agatha never wanted to be a mother. That was a dream of yours. And now..."

"Life is strange," Rio said vaguely. "When you're in your twenties, you think you know it all, that you have it all figured out, that life is just a map you follow. Then you wake up and realize every day is a surprise. Nothing goes the way you imagined."

"Touché," Alex murmured. They clinked glasses again, both taking a long drink.

"But is she happy?" Rio asked, breaking the comfortable silence.

"She is now. She’s so excited about the baby; she’s gone full maternal mode, and honestly, I’m loving it," Alex said with a broad grin, earning a soft laugh from Rio. "I can't pass a baby store without buying something for my godson. He’s going to be the most spoiled kid on Earth."

"Her mother is thrilled. I think she might be coming to Los Angeles soon to stay with her for a while."

"She’ll love it, and it’ll be good for Agatha. At least then she won't be stuck alone with that idiot she married."

"Alex," Rio said reprovingly, but the blonde just shrugged.

"You know I’ve been against that marriage from day one."

"You’re the one who set them up," Rio reminded her. Alex’s eyes widened in mock offense.

"And I’ve tried everything to fix that mistake! You know I have."

"You talk about Tessa like she’s the only one who screwed up," Rio murmured. She had no love for the woman, but she was nothing if not honest. "Agatha is no saint, and I’m the living proof of it."

"You were the only one. She loved you, she still does!"

Rio felt a familiar sharp pang in her chest. No matter how much she built a life she was proud of, some wounds never quite closed.

"She chose to get married," Rio said, her voice quiet but firm. "And she chooses to stay, knowing exactly who her wife is. She’s living the life she picked for herself; it’s no one’s business but hers. If she’s happy, who are we to judge?"

"I can judge just fine, she’s a stubborn fool," Alex grumbled. Rio gave a weak laugh, knowing Alex said the exact same thing to Agatha’s face. "She still loves you, Rio."

Rio took a deep breath, letting out a heavy sigh.

"I’m not going back down that rabbit hole, Alex. I’m old enough to know that love isn't just a feeling; it’s a choice. She made hers." Alex started to interrupt, but Rio held up a hand. "We’ve had this conversation a thousand times. She could have chosen differently, she had every opportunity, but she chose this. That tells me everything I need to know about how she really feels."

"She loves you, and you love her," Alex insisted, pointing a finger at her. Rio didn't argue. She simply refilled her glass.

"Of course I love her," Rio admitted easily. Alex’s eyebrows shot up in surprise. "When you love someone like that, I think it stays with you for life. But it changes. I still care, and I probably always will. I can't just erase what she meant to me, but it’s different now."

"Give me a break, Riandra," Alex scoffed, rolling her eyes. "You love her so much that you subject yourself to the city you hate most in the world once a year just to make sure she’s okay. You love her so much that you haven’t been able to take another woman seriously for more than twelve months." Rio closed her eyes, feeling a tremor of something long-buried stir inside her. "And she loves you more than anyone. She listens to your old tapes when she can't sleep. She collects your wines. She cries about you when she’s had too much to drink, and she pings your family for news because she’s too afraid to ask for you directly. She loves you so much that she’s naming her son after you."

Rio’s eyes snapped open. "She’s what?"

Alex froze, her mouth hanging open as she realized she’d crossed a line. "She's doing what?" Rio pressed.

"What is his name?" Rio’s voice was low, desperate. she leaned across the table. "Tell me, Alex. Now." Alex took a shaky breath. "She won't find out from me. Just tell me."

"Nicholas River Mason," Alex whispered. Rio slumped back into her chair, breathless. "River means Rio in Spanish, doesn't it?"

Rio’s heart hammered against her ribs, and her vision blurred with unshed tears. Her hands shook so violently that the wine wobbled in her glass. She could only stare at Alex, her brown eyes wide with shock.

"She loves you, Rio," Alex said, her voice heavy with sadness.

"I shouldn't have come," Rio whispered, paralyzed. It felt as if her body were made of ice while her blood turned to fire.

"Rio—"

"I—I have to go." Rio fumbled with her glass, setting it down as she scrambled to her feet. "I should go."

"Rio!" Alex reached for her wrist, but Rio pulled away without looking back. She couldn't see anything through the storm raging inside her.

"I’ll call you, I guess," she stammered, already moving. She didn't hear Alex calling her name.

Agatha was having a child. A child that wasn't hers, yet would carry her name. The world felt like it had stopped spinning on its axis.

She needed a drink. Something strong enough to make her forget.

***

Present Day

Wanda sat in the spacious living room, clutching a mug of tea, and let out an irritated sigh as the familiar rumble of the truck finally reached the house. She checked the clock: nearly two-thirty in the morning. Her frustration wasn't aimed at her wife; she trusted Natasha implicitly and knew she wasn't the type to stay out partying. But Wanda knew exactly who was, and her gut told her they were about to have the same old argument.

Minutes later, Natasha stumbled through the door, half-carrying a disheveled, vomit-stained, and completely unresponsive Rio. Wanda stood immediately to help, draping one of Rio’s limp arms over her shoulder to help guide her to the sofa. Rio sank into the cushions like a stone.

It broke Wanda's heart that this scene had become a daily routine. Rio was a wreck — her head lolled over the armrest, her mouth was smeared with vomit, and she was drooling on herself. Her flannel shirt hung open and damp over a stained tank top, and her boots were caked in mud. No matter how angry Wanda felt, the sight of her like this always made her want to cry.

Natasha stood with her hands on her hips, pacing the room in a blur of worry and exhaustion. Wanda took her hand and gently guided her into an armchair. Kneeling on the floor, she rested her arms on Natasha’s knees and looked into her troubled green eyes.

"I’m not upset with you," Wanda said softly. "I know it’s not your fault. It’s no one’s fault."

But Wanda was upset — deeply so. She was devastated to watch Riandra spiral further down every single day.

"I’m sorry," Natasha whispered, her breath heavy. "I know you hate me bringing her here like this, but Olivia is away, Yelena was already gone, and I couldn't just leave her with Bucky."

"Why didn't you just take her home?"

"Lilia is worried enough as it is, and Benito is there," Natasha sighed.

Wanda stood up and perched on the arm of the chair, crossing her arms. "And? Benito is her brother, Natasha. He has a right to know what’s happening."

"Wanda, you know—"

"She needs help," Wanda interrupted firmly, cutting off the protectiveness she knew was actually enabling. "I know she’s grieving, but this isn't an isolated incident. This is her life now." She pointed to the unconscious woman on the sofa. "She drinks from morning until night. It's a disease, Natasha. She’s sick."

"I’m trying to help!" Natasha snapped, her exhaustion boiling over. "Do you think I like being out until three am? Chasing after her to make sure she’s safe? I’ve been trying to help for months!"

"Honey, this isn't helping. We’re just enabling her," Wanda countered. "We give her water and food and make sure she doesn't choke on her own vomit, and then tomorrow she does it all over again. This has gone beyond all limits."

"We’ve talked about this," her wife pleaded, her tone now one of desperation. Wanda sighed heavily; she knew Natasha’s hesitation came from a good place, a place of loyalty, but it wasn't the right thing to do. "She needs support right now. It’s only been a week since her father died."

"She was like this before he died," Wanda said tiredly. "She’s running away, and if no one stops her, she’ll never come back. Deep down, you know that." Tears welled in Natasha's eyes. "I don't mind taking care of her; she's family. But I’m watching the godmother of my children and my wife’s best friend destroy herself. If you care about her, you have to do the right thing."

"And what is that, Wanda?" Natasha asked, her voice cracking.

"I don't want her near my children like this," Wanda said, looking at Rio with disdain. "This is the last time, Natasha."

"Wanda—"

"Next time, you take her home. You talk to her brother, and we figure out how to get her help. I’m done being part of this." Wanda pointed one last time at the figure drooling on the white leather sofa. "I love her too much to let her do this. I care more about her life than whether she’s mad at me for doing something."

Natasha didn't answer. She just bowed her head as tears streamed down her face. Her silence was a heavy, hesitant agreement.

"Clean this up," Wanda murmured as she headed for the hallway. "I don’t want to see mud or vomit in my living room when I wake up."

Once Wanda was gone, Natasha buried her face in her hands. She looked up at her best friend, still lost in a drunken stupor.

"Why are you doing this to us?" she whispered mournfully. "Why are you doing this to yourself?"

***

Rio woke to a wave of nausea and a mounting pressure behind her eyes. As she sat up, a sharp spike of vertigo forced a groan from her lips. She squeezed her eyes shut, rubbing her temples, but the scowl deepened when she finally surveyed her surroundings. She was in Natasha’s living room, not her own bed. Though she had no memory of how she’d gotten there, the sour, bitter stench clinging to her shirt provided all the explanation she needed. It wasn't worth remembering.

She stretched, glancing toward the large window. Judging by the sun’s height and the lingering morning mist, it was barely eight o'clock. At least she was awake early. With a resigned shrug, she began the slow, unsteady trek toward the downstairs bathroom.

This wasn't an unfamiliar scene; every so often, a long night ended on her best friend's sofa. She knew Wanda detested it — Wanda had a puritanical streak when it came to shielding the children from excessive drinking — but Rio found the concern misplaced. Her father had drank his entire life, and she and her brother had turned out fine. People drank; it was just life. To avoid the inevitable lecture, she usually tried to stay away when she was in that state. She hated it when Natasha played the savior; it was always the same tired routine. Rio was a grown woman who could find her own way home, even if she rarely remembered the moment her head actually hit the pillow.

She stepped into the shower, letting the ice-cold spray shock her system back to life. The sluggishness began to recede, replaced by the familiar throb of a migraine and the usual knot in her stomach. She preferred the physical pain; a hangover was a small price to pay for a temporary reprieve from her own mind. For Rio, forgetting was the only way to survive. Switching off and rebooting was the only way to make the noise stop.

Most of the time, her mind felt too large for the universe, a vast, crowded space where the entire world seemed to clamor for her attention. No matter how hard she fought, she couldn't slow the current. Her thoughts were a thundering storm, shifting so rapidly from one thing to the next that her head ached and her hands trembled. Sometimes the mental flood was so overwhelming she lost the ability to speak or express herself at all. She lived in a state of constant mental exhaustion. It was a physical ache that blurred her vision and sent irritation bubbling to the surface. She just wanted to stop thinking — or at least to think slower. She wanted to feel less, or better yet, to feel nothing at all.

She felt assaulted by her own mind. She was constantly overthinking, overfeeling, and utterly powerless to control any of it. It was a draining, agonizing cycle she couldn't explain to anyone because it didn't even make sense to her. Her mind, like the rest of her, was battered and worn. That was why she was so broken.

After her shower, Rio leaned against the counter and stared at her reflection. She saw what she always saw: something spoiled, something broken at the core. That was the truth of her, the version of herself she tried so desperately to hide while pretending she had turned out fine. She was a fraud, and she knew it.

On the counter sat a fresh towel and a change of clean clothes — Wanda’s doing, as usual. Rio offered the empty room a faint, weary smile. She was grateful for them, even if she lacked the words to show it. They were her family, after all.

Stepping out into the hallway, Rio nearly jumped when she found Wanda waiting for her. Wanda’s expression was a cold, unreadable mask that felt anything but welcoming.

"Pancakes are in the kitchen," Wanda murmured, her eyes scanning Rio with obvious distaste. "Eat before the boys wake up."

"And miss out on the big family breakfast?" Rio shot back, forcing a crooked grin. Wanda’s look of disapproval only sharpened, and Rio felt herself swallow hard under the weight of it.

"You stink, Rio," was the blunt reply. Wanda’s face twisted in a grimace as she turned her back and began to walk away.

"Hey, I just showered!" Rio called after her, sounding indignant as she sniffed her borrowed sleeve.

"Try brushing your teeth," Wanda tossed back over her shoulder.

"You don't have to talk to me like that," Rio muttered. The words made Wanda stop in her tracks. She spun around, eyebrows arched in a silent challenge. "Look, I’m sorry about last night. I know you hate this, and I shouldn't have come here in that state. I don't even remember..." She trailed off with a heavy sigh. "I'm just going through a hard time, okay? I’m grieving."

"You’re grieving?" Wanda’s voice was sharp, a cynical huff escaping her as she shook her head. "Just how long have you been grieving now, Rio?"

Wanda walked away without waiting for an answer. Rio remained frozen in the hallway, watching her go as a massive, suffocating weight settled in her chest.

***

The sun warmed her skin as she made her way down the dirt path, nodding to neighbors as she headed home. It was a forty-minute trek from Natasha’s place to the ranch, but Rio didn’t mind; she welcomed the chance to shoot the breeze with familiar faces along the way. Since it was the weekend, there was no reason to rush. Her only plans were to tend to the animals until noon and then find a race to watch — or, better yet, one to enter. For once, the day was free of urgency.

That lingering sense of peace evaporated the moment she spotted the metallic blue Volvo in her driveway. Rio let out a low growl. What kind of playboy drives a Volvo on a ranch? She had momentarily forgotten her brother was still in town, a reality she wasn’t prepared to face today — or any day, if she had her way. She wanted nothing more than for Benito to vanish back to Los Angeles, but the pendejo seemed to be putting down roots instead.

His sudden desire to be present irritated her to no end. It felt like he was there to supervise her, as if she actually craved his company. He had built a life elsewhere years ago; why stay now? With their father gone, he had no reason to visit, and Rio certainly didn't want him around. She had managed perfectly well on her own for years and wasn't about to start needing him now.

She understood, of course, that they were bound by the family legacy. The business was as much his as it was hers, but that didn't justify his intrusion. Benito had never worked the ranch; he had neither the knack nor the stomach for it. His role was administrative — a highly educated man with degrees in economics who handled the money, the contracts, and the lawyers. Rio was the heart of the operation, managing the raw materials, the horses, the vineyard, and the creative vision. He was the paperwork; she was the land. It was a functional arrangement that didn't require him to be physically there.

With their father's passing, Rio knew the workload would increase, but she figured a few new hires could handle the pressure. Benito could oversee the hiring from afar while she leaned on Natasha. The business was rock solid. She wasn't afraid of change, but she found her brother’s presence entirely unnecessary.

Worse still, he showed no signs of leaving. Every day, Lilia was in her ear, reporting that Benito was looking for her, as if she owed him an explanation for her whereabouts. With her father dead and no children of her own, Rio answered to no one — least of all an ungrateful brat who thought two diplomas on a wall made him superior.

"Where were you?" Benito’s voice thundered through the hall the moment Rio stepped inside. She narrowed her eyes and let out a sharp, dry laugh, taking in the sight of her brother standing in the center of the room with his arms crossed. "Our father has been dead for a week, and I—"

"I don't need a reminder. I was there," Rio cut him off, her tone cold enough to make him flinch. "I was with him every single day while you were off in California."

"What is that supposed to mean?" he asked, his brow furrowed.

"I'm just telling the truth," Rio replied, closing the distance between them with long, aggressive strides. She puffed out her chest, noting that despite being eleven years younger, he had several inches on her. "Where were you while he was here, sick? Did you even know, or did you just not care?" She watched with bitter satisfaction as his irritation dissolved into a look of deep, wounded offense.

"You think I didn't know he was sick?" Benito asked, his voice dropping to a low, dangerous level. He narrowed his eyes at her. "Every time he visited LA, I begged him to see a cardiologist. I booked appointments with the best specialists in the country, and he refused to go! He wouldn't admit he needed help. You think being here makes you the only one who cared? I offered to come home, to stay with him, and he told me not to. He said it was better for the business because you couldn't be bothered to travel and handle things."

"Oh, so my being here is the problem now?" Rio shot back with a dry, bitter laugh. "You think I didn't want to travel? I’ve spent my life traveling for this family! If you knew he was failing, why weren't you here? Why didn't you just show up?" she shouted, incensed that he wasn't backing down. "Why didn't you call me about the doctors? Why didn't you—"

"Since when do you even take my calls?" he yelled back. They were nose-to-nose now, screaming into each other's faces. "You don't call, you don't write, you’re a ghost, Riandra! You only talk to who you want, when you want!"

Rio recoiled as if slapped. Benito took a ragged breath, swallowing hard. "You never answered me. For years, you just... ignored me," he whispered, finally looking away. Rio stepped back, a sharp knot tightening in her throat. "I wrote letters, I called, I—" He stopped, shaking his head. "I knew he was sick, Rio. We all did. But nobody thought he was dying. He told me the business was fine, that you were taking over the new wine line and everything was under control. I believed him. Why wouldn't I? You were here. I thought he was safe with you."

"I was here. I’ve always been here," Rio murmured, her breath hitching. "But that doesn't excuse you. You should have been here too." Benito opened his mouth to argue, but she held up a hand. "I’m done. I think we’ve said enough."

"Rio!" he called out as she turned away.

"If you want to talk business, see me on Monday," she threw over her shoulder, marching up the stairs without looking back.

Benito slumped onto the sofa and buried his face in his hands. A knot of unshed tears ached in his throat, a physical weight he couldn't shake. He didn't understand why she was so cruel; he had never wanted this fight.

To him, Riandra was a solid oak door with no key. She opened and closed on her own whims, and every time he tried to reach out, she slammed it in his face. He was a grown man, but around her, he felt like a desperate little boy again, begging for a scrap of attention. He just wanted his sister. He had just lost his father, and he was alone.

"Neno." Lilia’s soft voice broke the silence. She sat beside him and gently stroked his face.

"I’m trying, Lilia," he choked out, shaking his head. "I’m really trying, but she hates me."

Lilia shook her head, resting a hand on his shoulder. "She doesn't hate you, neno," she said, though Benito’s expression remained unconvinced. "She’s just hurt. She’s angry because she missed you, and she wanted you here."

"It sure doesn't feel like it."

"She’s angry because she loves you, and she didn't want to feel alone when she needed you most," Lilia whispered, like a secret. She smiled at him, still seeing the child she had helped raise. "She’s never been good with her feelings. She’s lashing out because you weren't here with her."

"I didn't know she needed me," he said, his voice thick with exasperation. Lilia pulled him into a side hug, though he was now twice her size. "I needed her, too. I still do. But she acts like she has to punish me for wanting a life of my own."

Lilia remained silent, letting him vent.

"She loves this ranch, and I’m glad for her," Benito continued. "But it wasn't my path. Why can't she accept that people want different things? It doesn't make me better or worse than her. We’re just different."

"She’s a hard woman, neno," Lilia sighed. "Things would be easier if she could see it your way, but you can't just force her heart open. I promise you, she loves you. She’s just been reeling ever since your father’s health turned. She’s lost."

"Why does everyone have to walk on eggshells for her? She doesn't have a drop of empathy for anyone else. It's her way or the highway."

"Do you want an eye for an eye?" Lilia asked. He sat in silence for a long moment before finally shaking his head. "Then have patience. She’s difficult, yes, but she has a good heart. She’s just drifting right now. And even if she’d rather die than admit it, she needs you right now."

***

Eleven Years Ago

Rio felt like a fool for feeling this way, yet the sentiment was beyond her control. She felt small and or stupid, but she couldn't see the world any other way. To her, it was baffling that no one else felt the same — that everyone seemed to think this restlessness was right or fair. She couldn’t fathom why anyone would want everything except what they already had. They possessed the world: a thriving business, a vast estate, and a family. Why wasn’t that enough? What could possibly be more important than that?

She understood that sometimes departure wasn't a choice. When her mother died, she had simply vanished from their lives. It was cruel and unfair, but Rio accepted it because it was beyond human control. She knew with absolute certainty that her mother would never have chosen to leave her. What Rio couldn't grasp was why those who did have a choice would ever decide to walk away. She didn't understand the urge to conquer the unknown or build a life in some distant place when everything that mattered was already right there.

For Rio, her home and her family were her entire universe. She had everything she could ever want within the boundaries of the ranch.

Since childhood, Agatha’s obsession with the world beyond had been a mystery to Rio — this relentless drive to see new places and meet new people. To Rio, it was nonsensical to trade a known, shared life for a hollow dream. She never truly believed Agatha would go; she dismissed it as a phase or a series of daydreams. It was a shock when Agatha actually chose a life miles away, trading their history for a stranger’s world, as if the novelty of a new city outweighed everything they felt for each other.

Losing her mother had cost Rio a piece of herself; Agatha’s departure had broken another. But with Benito’s decision to leave, she felt entirely hollowed out, as if her very center had shattered into dust.

Agatha was an outsider in that sense; she had always been a bird meant for flight, even if Rio had struggled to let her go. But Benito was different. He was hers. He was part of her soul. With their mother gone, it was just the three of them — why would he choose to leave?

She wasn’t against his education; she was proud of his ambition. But Montana had excellent universities less than an hour away. He could have stayed home and commuted. He didn’t need to cross state lines or move to Los Angeles to live with Agatha and her wife. He didn’t need to abandon her. Yet he wanted to. He chose it, and he was happy.

Rio felt like she had been dragged back in time, forced to relive the grief of her twenties all over again — the agonizing cycle of trying to understand and accept a loss she didn't want. She was forced to relearn that you cannot force people to stay, even when your heart is breaking to keep them home.

A week had passed since Benito’s departure, and despite her father’s presence, the house felt cavernous. She was utterly alone.

"Are you sure you don’t want to come?" her father asked, snapping his suitcase shut. Rio leaned against the doorframe and shook her head.

"I’m fine here," she said, her smile tight and unconvincing. Her father tilted his head. "You’ll only be gone a week, and someone needs to keep an eye on things."

"Mi cielo, it’s only a few hours by plane and another one on the road," he said with a shrug. "The ranch is in good hands. If anything happens, we’ll be back in a heartbeat."

"I’d rather stay. It’s good practice for me," she replied. Her father arched an eyebrow. "One day you’ll retire, and I’ll be the one running this place."

"I'm not retiring yet," he laughed. "And you already run this place. You know I trust you with my soul."

"I know."

"Are you sure you don't want to go?" he pressed once more. Rio sighed and shook her head.

"You’ve asked a million times, dad. I’m staying."

"It’s a big moment for your brother," Javier said gently. "He’s celebrating an achievement; he really wanted you there."

"I know, and I’m happy for him, but I wouldn't feel right leaving the ranch."

"Riandra—"

"I know, dad. I know it’s only a few hours away, but I can't leave." She stood her ground until Javier let out a resigned sigh. "You're already going; he’ll be happy enough to see you."

"But you’re important to him too, mi cielo. You’re siblings."

Rio ran her tongue along the inside of her cheek and looked away, hugging herself tightly. She didn’t know how to explain that she simply couldn't go — that she wasn't happy for him, that she didn't want Javier to go, and that she wished Benito hadn't left in the first place. She felt like a frustrated child trapped in a grown woman's body.

"Benito is old enough to realize we can't always get what we want," Rio said with a shrug. "Pedra Púrpura opens this week. I’m not stopping the preparations just because he’s having a week of college welcoming parties. His goals matter, but so do mine, and I have work to do."

"I know, and I’m proud of you," Javier said. He stepped closer and kissed the top of her head. "It’s a great idea to explore your new lines, test your new wines. I’m certain it will be a success." Rio managed a small smile, even as her chest tightened. "I know this matters to you, but so does family. He misses you. Postponing the opening by a week wouldn't be the end of the world."

"I'll call him," she promised. When Javier arched a skeptical eyebrow, she added, "What? I will."

"He told me you haven't spoken to him once since he moved out."

Rio rolled her eyes, letting out a sharp breath, even though she had a knot in her throat.

Rio rolled her eyes, masking the knot in her throat with a sharp exhale. "He’s being dramatic. I haven't had a second to breathe. I’m swamped with work and the daily races. I even have a race today." Javier looked unconvinced but gave a slow nod. "I’ll call as soon as I can, I promise."

She flinched slightly when her father took a deep breath. He sighed heavily, his intense gaze locking onto hers. Rio swallowed hard; she had never been a good liar, especially with him. Those brown eyes always seemed to reach the depths of her soul, reading her thoughts before she could even voice them.

Javier stopped in front of her and rubbed her shoulders. His eyes were filled with an infinite, overflowing warmth that made the tension in her heart ease just for a moment. She loved him more than anything.

"Mi cielo, I know this is hard," he said tenderly. Rio looked up, her brow furrowed. "Do you think it isn't hard for me too? You two are everything I have. If I could, I’d keep you both under my wing forever, but we can't cage the things we love."

Rio didn't dare say a word. Her father offered a faint smile. Even though she was nearly thirty, his presence made her feel ten again.

"My greatest joy is having you here, but if you wanted a different life, I would let you go too, because I love you, and I want you to be happy."

"I would never leave," Rio said, her voice thin. "Why would I?"

Javier shrugged. "Sometimes we just want things without needing a reason. Why do you like to race?" Rio remained silent, merely shrugging back. Javier raised an eyebrow as if to say exactly. "Your brother is seventeen. He’s a man now, and it’s time for him to make his own choices, just as you made yours."

"And I’m happy for him."

"I know you are. But I also know you’re sad," he said gently. Rio looked away again. "It’s not forever, mi cielo. He's only a few hours away."

"I know. I just—"

"Loving someone means trying to understand them, my love, even when their choices don't make sense to us. It doesn't change who we are or how we feel. He’s your brother. He loves you, and he misses you."

"I miss him too," Rio whispered. Her father kissed her head and checked his watch.

"There’s still time to pack if you change your mind," he insisted. Rio rolled her eyes.

"I have a race, dad."

"Suit yourself," he said, turning back to the clothes scattered on the bed. "I’m leaving tomorrow morning if you change your mind."

"I thought you were leaving tonight?" she asked, confused.

"I’d rather hit the road in the morning," he said distractedly.

"Can you come to my race today? It’s in about—"

"Riandra, you know how I feel about that," he cut her off, focused on folding his shirts. She nodded, staring at her feet as she swallowed the lump in her throat.

If loving really meant trying to understand, Rio felt like nobody was trying hard enough for her. It seemed that all she had ever done was try to understand them, even when their choices made no sense to her at all.

Chapter End Notes

please let me know what you think, every comment is appreciated <3 thank you so much for reading and i'll see you soon

and just for you know, pedra purpura means purple stone, like an agate (or agata in spanish)

Chapter 13: drowning

Chapter Summary

getting a better feel for rio's feelings

Chapter Notes

trigger warning for substance abuse.

i know it’s a bit of a slow burn, but we’ve only got a few more chapters of development left. i really wanted to flesh out both sides before moving the plot forward, but things are about to pick up, i promise. hope you guys can bear with me!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

"The villain of the movies, just a victim of the times [...] I yearned for you approval 'til I didn't recognize me either [...] I can't claim it as just self-defense, protecting the illusion of my permanent innocence, because who's guilty is besides the point; it has to be me" – Destroyer, Ruel

Rio had lost all track of time. Reality had blurred long before sunset, when the suffocating weight of her thoughts finally dissolved into a warm, drunken haze. She and Bucky had spent the day prowling highway dive bars, betting against the locals on televised horse races before chasing down amateur tracks in nearby towns to keep the rush alive. Now, as dawn threatened to break, they were back from Bell Crossing, howling with manic laughter at the sheer amount of easy money stuffed in their pockets.

“I don’t think we’ve ever made this much cash without you actually getting on a horse,” Bucky chuckled, leafing through a thick stack of checks. “You didn’t even have to break a sweat, Rio!”

Rio didn’t answer. She was too busy trying to quiet the noise in her head. She finished rolling a joint, shook her head as if to dislodge a nagging thought, and took a heavy swig of beer. After lighting up, she took a long, steady drag.

“I’ve been thinking...” she murmured, the words drifting out with a cloud of smoke. She sank deep into the car seat, drained her can, and immediately cracked open another.

Bucky, a square of LSD resting on the tip of his tongue, squinted at her before his face split into a grin. He tucked the tab against the roof of his mouth, groaning in satisfaction as he let it dissolve. “You’ve been thinking? That’s never a good sign.”

“Maybe I should quit racing,” she whispered.

Bucky’s eyes snapped open. He stared at her in total disbelief, but Rio didn't notice — or didn't care. She just kept drinking, her gaze fixed on the dashboard as she stared at the countless broken cracks within herself.

“Quit?” he asked, his voice sharp with sudden urgency. He forced a nervous laugh and took a drink, trying to catch her eye. “Have you lost your mind? Rio, you live for the track.”

“I know I do. I just don't know what the point is anymore,” she said, more to herself than him. She took another hit of the joint, oblivious to the look of pure anguish crossing Bucky's face. “I can’t—I know I can’t stop,” she confessed, handing him the joint. “I need to run. But I don’t want my life to be nothing but the race, forever. I don’t know. Sometimes it just feels... empty.”

“You do way more than just race, Rio,” Bucky said, exhaling a cloud of smoke and looking at her with a smile. “You run the whole ranch, you manage the business, you have the winery. You’ve got so much going on at once that sometimes people wonder how you keep it all together.”

“Because I love everything I do,” Rio replied, though her voice was distant and carried a melancholy that betrayed her words.

“If you love racing, then why stop?”

“I love my horses, Bucky. You know that.” He nodded as she continued, her face twisting into a grimace. “But every time we spend the day at the tracks, I see it through my father’s eyes. To everyone else, those animals are just money-making machines. I feel like the more I race, the more I’m just fueling that industry, no matter how much I try to convince myself I’m different.”

“But you are different,” Bucky countered, shifting in the passenger seat to face her. “Ever since you made a name for yourself, you’ve stuck to your guns. You can’t save every horse in the world, Riandra, it’s an industry, and a cold one at that. But you take care of your own, and that has to be enough.”

“I don’t know. I feel like I need to rethink... everything,” she said, missing the way he stiffened at her words. “I need the speed. I need it to think, to breathe, and yet—” She took the joint from him and inhaled deeply. “When I run just for the sake of running, it leaves me hollow.”

“Not everything needs a profound purpose, Rio.”

“I know, but it feels wrong,” she whispered. She stared out at the yellow streetlights, their glow blurred by the steady summer drizzle.

She couldn't find the words for the knot in her chest. Rio had never been good at explaining herself; feelings were messy, and putting them into words felt foolish. She didn't handle emotions — she either buried them or outran them.

Lately, she had been desperate for a distraction, throwing herself into betting, business, and horses to stay away from the ranch. Home used to be her sanctuary during a storm, but now the comfort was gone. Not with Benito living under her roof and Agatha right next door, her son’s laughter echoing across the property.

She wasn't sure why she was opening up to Bucky; he was just as messed up as she was. Maybe it was the alcohol, or maybe it was just the simple, familiar weight of a lifelong friendship.

“I’ve been thinking about my father a lot lately,” she confessed.

Bucky rolled his eyes and flicked the roach out the window. “That’s what’s fucking with your head,” he said, cracking open two more beers and handing her one. She took a long, grateful swig. “You know the old man hated that shit.”

“He wasn’t wrong, though.”

“You’ve been doing this for years, Rio. You’ve already proven you can do it your way. Now that he’s kicked the bucket, are you suddenly looking for redemption?” He caught her gaze with a flicker of disdain, making her shrug. “You aren’t your father, Riandra. You have your own life to live.”

It was a life she wasn't proud of — or at the very least, one that felt utterly purposeless. Without her father, without the anchor of her family, everything felt adrift. She had finally started to carve out her own path and find a flicker of happiness in her choices, only to watch it all slip through her fingers while she stood by, powerless to catch it.

"I know it’s fucked up as hell, and you’ve been in a bad way since he passed," Bucky said, his voice dropping into a rare, tender register. "But he’s the one who’s gone, Rio, not you."

She looked up, meeting his eyes. His expression was dead serious as he squeezed her shoulder in a rare show of comfort. "Don’t spiral into that shit again. We need you here, okay?"

With a lump in her throat and tears stinging her eyes, Rio couldn't bring herself to answer. She took a steadying breath and unlocked the car door.

"Goodnight, Bucky," she muttered.

He knit his brows, looking slighty thrown. "Goodnight? I thought we were gonna—"

"I’m going to Olivia’s," she murmured. Bucky relaxed, letting out a faint, approving laugh. He stepped out of the car but leaned back through the window a second later.

"I hope you fuck until you're back to normal and all that goddamn noise in your head finally shuts up."

"Go fuck yourself, Bucky," Rio rolled her eyes and turned the ignition. He just laughed, blowing her mocking kisses as she sped off down the dirt road.

Rio didn’t want to think, so she didn't. She drove toward the police station, singing at the top of her lungs to drown out the internal chatter, taking occasional sips of her beer. When she finally drifted into a spot across from the station and pulled the handbrake, she frowned. Olivia’s brown Jeep Wrangler was nowhere to be seen.

She grabbed her phone and scrolled to Olivia’s number.

"Officer, I’d like to report a crime!" Rio said the moment Olivia picked up, greeted by a faint chuckle. "Actually, I’m the criminal. I have to confess: I’m drunk behind the wheel. But I’m a repentant soul, and I’m right outside the station waiting for you to cuff me."

"Are you drinking and driving again?"

The sharp edge of disapproval in Olivia’s voice was clear, but Rio pushed past it.

"See? That’s exactly why you need to lock me up! Bring the handcuffs, I’m out here waiting." Rio tried to keep the smile in her voice, but she heard Olivia’s heavy, frustrated exhale.

"You think you’re so fucking funny, don’t you?" Rio gave a weak laugh. "But I’m not in town, remember?"

"I didn't forget, but you said you’d be back today." Rio’s brow furrowed, a fresh wave of disappointment hitting her.

"I was going to be, but the case got complicated. Another child went missing, and it’s likely the same suspect," Olivia explained, her voice thick with exhaustion. Rio gritted her teeth and thumped her forehead against the steering wheel. "I’ll probably be stuck here for another two days to get anything substantial."

"Shit," Rio grumbled, rubbing her temples. She missed Olivia, but more than that, she was desperate for company. She didn't want to be alone, and she certainly didn't want to go home to face her own thoughts.

“I know. I wish I was home.”

"I wish you were here," Rio whispered. Olivia sighed; she knew Rio well enough to hear the cracks in her voice.
