She’s been sober for thirteen years—do you think those were thirteen easy years? That she didn't have relapses? She had to be strong every single day, and she was. And not once were you here. Not for her."

Benito felt his heart sink. Tears flooded his eyes as helplessness took hold — a sharp, painful mix of guilt, sadness, and the deep longing he felt for his sister. He knew Natasha was right, but he was just as prideful as Rio. As much as they missed each other, neither had made a move to bridge the gap. But he should have been there.

"I may not be her blood sister, but she is my best friend," Natasha said, her voice heavy with intensity. "She is the family I chose, the one I built. That’s why I’m here now, why I’ve been here all these years, and why I’m staying. But you are her brother, Benito. You need to be here, too, because she needs you. She’ll never admit it, but she does. She needs her family."

"I’m not leaving," he said firmly, meeting her gaze. "I’m staying right here with her. I won’t let this happen again. I don’t want this wall between us anymore. I need her, too. I’m here now, and I’m not going anywhere."

"Good," Natasha said, her tone finally softening as she squeezed his shoulder and nodded. "She needs you. She needs us."

"But what can we do? I don't—" Benito started, but Wanda interrupted him gently.

"Rio has a habit of running away from herself, of bottling every feeling up inside," she said, looking at him with compassion. Benito sat back down in the armchair as Natasha returned to her wife’s side. "She needs our presence right now. She needs to feel cared for, loved, and appreciated. She carries this melancholy with her, this self-deprecation... she needs affection."

"It’s so bizarre to me, seeing how she views herself," Benito muttered, shaking his head. "She’s the only one who doesn’t see how incredible she is. She keeps saying I feel superior because I went to college, but it’s the exact opposite. I’m fascinated by the knowledge she’s gained just by getting her hands dirty. She knows everything; she’s good at everything. I had to study for years to manage this business, and she just walks into that winery and makes it all happen, leaving a room full of engineers slack-jawed. She’s a force of nature."

"I tell her that all the time," Natasha said, her voice trailing off distantly.

"She acts like nobody cares about her; it doesn’t even make sense," Benito whispered, almost to himself. "Nicholas spends the whole day following her around like a puppy, practically begging for a shred of attention like she’s some kind of superhero, and she doesn’t even realize it."

"Nicholas?" Natasha asked, her brow furrowing. "Agatha’s son?"

Benito nodded. "He spends the day with me sometimes. He’s my godson."

"I know this is none of my business," Natasha began cautiously, leaning forward to rest her elbows on her knees. "But how much longer is Agatha staying in Darby? It’s been weeks..."

Benito tilted his head. He found the question odd and bristled at the hostile undertone — as if Agatha were an unwanted guest.

"She’s staying for a while," he replied, his tone firm and leaving no room for speculation. He exchanged a brief, meaningful look with Lilia, who gave a discreet nod. "Nothing is set in stone yet, but for now, she’s staying."

"Like I said, it’s not my place, but I really don’t think this is good for your sister."

"What?" Benito asked, confused.

"Benny, you aren't the only reason Riandra has been avoiding this house like the plague. She doesn’t want to see Agatha. She’s been spiraling ever since she found out she was back. You know they have a history, and you know it’s... complicated."

"Agatha is family; she needs to be here right now. Rio might be angry and reactive at the moment, but Agatha is important to her, too."

"Your sister only managed to get sober once Agatha was out of her life," Natasha countered.

"No," Benito cut her off immediately. "No, I won’t let you say that. Agatha isn't to blame for that, she didn't—"

"I’m not saying she’s to blame; Riandra is the only one responsible for her own choices. What I’m saying is that the emotional baggage between them was one of the reasons she sank."

Benito looked down, processing her words, but when he faced her again, he shook his head.

"Look, Rio is my sister and I love her more than anything, but Agatha is part of my family, too. She’s like another sister to me—she is my other sister. I know they have a history and that they both hurt each other deeply. I know Rio came out of it shattered, but Agatha was hurt too—badly." As he spoke, Natasha continued to shake her head, but Wanda placed a hand on her arm, a silent plea to dial it back. "Rio is going through a lot, but so is Agatha. I’m here for both of them. This house is home to both of them."

"I don’t see this ending well for Rio," Natasha muttered, clearly unconvinced.

Benito mirrored her shrug, just as certain of his own stance. "I think it’ll be good for both of them. They both need family right now, and that’s exactly what they are: family."

"I don’t think Agatha should know about Rio right now." It was the first time Lilia had spoken, and all eyes turned toward her. "As Benito said, she’s dealing with a lot, and Rio means a great deal to her. I don’t think it’s wise for her to know about this relapse. She didn't handle it well the first time, and I don't think she'd handle it well now, seeing her back in the same hole."

"That is exactly my point," Natasha said, gesturing toward Lilia. "It’s best for them to stay out of each other's way, especially if Agatha is also struggling."

"I agree with you," Benito said, nodding to Lilia. "But not because I think Agatha couldn’t handle it. She's dealing with enough, and Rio needs her space. But with you," he said, looking back at Natasha, "I don’t agree. We’re family, Natasha. All of us."

***

Fifteen Years Ago

Agatha could not hold back her tears as she half-carried, half-guided a stumbling Rio through the hallway of her building. With Rio’s arm draped heavily over her shoulders and her own hand firmly around Rio’s waist, she felt a crushing sense of humiliation. More than anything, she felt like a fool for believing, yet again, that they could ever make it work. Everyone had warned her — the timing was wrong, Rio was unstable — but she hadn't wanted to believe them. Now, she was paying the price.

For nearly four years, Agatha had been caught in an impossible tug-of-war. Following that first bitter vacation in Darby, she had tried to move on, finally giving Tessa a genuine chance. Life with Tessa was easy and pleasant; she was kind, attentive, and they shared so many interests. Day by day, Agatha felt herself growing more attached, and she truly did like her. The problem was that she loved Riandra. No matter how much she liked Tessa — and she truly did — it paled in comparison to the love she held for Rio.

She and Tessa had begun dating a few months after that New Year’s Eve in New York. While she was happy, a heavy weight remained in her chest. She saw those brown eyes in her mind every day, constantly comparing the two women. The more patient and understanding Tessa became, the more Agatha found herself missing Rio’s fire and her refusal to back down. She even missed their friction, the heat of their arguments. Above all, she craved Rio's scent, her voice, and the unique sense of peace she felt only in her embrace.

Agatha had tried to be faithful to Tessa, but her heart consistently betrayed her. Every trip back to Darby inevitably led her back to Rio; the pull was stronger than her, stronger than both of them combined. Even knowing Tessa was waiting for her in Los Angeles, she couldn't bring herself to care in those moments when she had Rio by her side.

She had survived in this limbo for four years, torn between two loves separated by distance and time. In one city, she belonged to one woman; in the other, she belonged to someone else. It wasn't fair to anyone. She felt a deep self-loathing, knowing she was hurting all of them. Even if Tessa had no proof of the infidelity, she saw the light fade from Agatha’s eyes every time she returned from Darby. She noticed the distance, the strangeness, the lingering shadow of another person. Tessa knew about Rio, and Agatha knew she suspected the truth, but neither had ever found the courage to say it aloud.

It took longer than it should have, but Agatha eventually threw reason to the wind. Her heart ached too much to continue ignoring her love for Rio. She ended things with Tessa, confessing the truth: she could no longer stay in a relationship while her heart belonged to someone else. A few months later, she returned to Darby and begged Rio for one more chance. She pleaded for them to try, just this once, to navigate the distance. They didn't need a map for the future or a perfect middle ground; they just needed to find a way to be together.

Agatha was blindsided when everyone — including her godfather — opposed the reconciliation. Her mother argued that Agatha had been away too long and no longer understood who Rio had become. Wanda urged her to reconsider, warning that Rio wasn't in a healthy place. Despite the chorus of caution, Agatha couldn't grasp the weight of their veiled threats. Whenever they were together, Rio seemed perfectly fine; in fact, once they reunited, Rio appeared happier than she had been in years.

The honeymoon phase was flawless. Rio called daily, often more than three times, and they would lose themselves in hours of conversation. Rio spoke of her races and her horses, while Agatha vented about the intensity of law school, her research group, and the relentless pressure of publishing deadlines. Often, they fell asleep on the line; waking up to the sound of Rio’s steady breathing on the other end was the only way Agatha wanted to start her day.

Despite her disdain for California, Rio began visiting every two weeks, staying in Agatha’s room in the apartment she shared with Alex. Agatha found herself neglecting her studies during these visits, skipping classes to maximize every second with Riandra. For her part, Rio seemed to be making a genuine effort to make the relationship work.

However, by the third month, the cracks began to show. Agatha started noticing things that made her stomach churn, and the true meaning of her family's warnings finally began to crystallize.

The frequent calls dwindled. Agatha noticed that whenever she called Rio unannounced, Rio was already intoxicated. She began testing this theory, calling at random hours of the day; every time, Rio answered with a slurred voice. During her visits to California, the drinking was constant. Rio would arrive with a drink in her hand while driving and would have a crate of beer empty only a few hours later. She was drinking every single day. The visits became increasingly rare, usually canceled with excuses about the races, but Javier eventually admitted the truth: Rio was often too drunk to even get behind the wheel.

No one had the courage to say it out loud: Rio was an alcoholic.

Agatha fought against the reality, desperate not to accept it. She made every excuse imaginable — Rio had always liked to drink; even her father was a heavy drinker. It was just a habit, she told herself. But the truth was a bitter pill to swallow. Her family had been right: Rio was in no condition to sustain a relationship. And Agatha, buried under the weight of law school and her own ambitions, was in no position to be a savior — especially not from a distance.  

She had tried with everything in her, but she couldn't live like this any longer.

Her research had just been published in one of the nation’s most prestigious law journals, and her advisors had organized a party to celebrate the milestone. Naturally, Rio was the first person she shared the news with. Agatha pleaded with her to attend, and when she saw her arrive, her heart soared. Rio looked stunning, trading her usual tank tops and jeans for tailored trousers, a crisp white dress shirt, and a black blazer, her hair pulled back into a sleek, sophisticated ponytail. In that moment, Agatha felt like the happiest woman in the world.

But the joy was short-lived. It didn't take long for Rio to find the bar. It would have been one thing to drink socially — it was a celebration, after all — but she didn't stop, throwing back glass after glass until her restraint vanished. Her comments turned sharp and inappropriate as she voiced her disdain for the ostentatious crowd. Her gestures became loud and clumsy, her posture slumped, and Agatha felt a desperate urge to disappear. She could feel the judgmental weight of the gazes from her professors, her advisors, and her boss — who happened to be her ex-father-in-law. Even Tessa was there, watching the scene unfold with a look that bordered on pity.

Less than four hours into the event, Tessa pulled her aside to break the news: Rio was trashed in the bathroom, slumped on the floor and retching. As much as Agatha rushed to her side, her worry was eclipsed by an overwhelming sense of mortification.

It was Tessa who stepped in to help. She guided them through the back exit to avoid a scene and promised to make excuses to the guests. She even drove them back to Agatha’s apartment. When they arrived, Agatha offered a quiet, head-bowed thank you, but Tessa caught her by the wrist.

"She’s an anchor, Agatha. Don’t sink with her," Tessa said, looking her directly in the eye. "You deserve better than this."

Agatha swallowed hard, unable to find the words to defend herself or the woman she loved.

Now, she sat on the bathroom floor in tears, her hands trembling as she held back Riandra’s hair. Rio continued to vomit and babble incoherently, having lost all sense of where she was or what she had done.

***

Agatha was in the living room, clutching a mug of chamomile tea, when Rio finally emerged. She watched in silence as Rio approached with hesitant, heavy steps. Dressed in her pajamas and wearing a kicked-puppy expression, Rio looked smaller than usual. Agatha felt her heart tighten, but she knew she couldn't keep doing this. Not like this.

“You’re mad at me,” Rio murmured, awkwardly scratching the back of her neck.

Agatha let out a weary sigh, setting her mug on the coffee table. “I’m feeling so many things, Rio, that I don’t even know what to call this anymore.”

Rio bit her lip and sat on the far end of the sofa, looking at her sheepishly. “I’m not mad,” Agatha added softly. “I’m sad.”

“I’m sorry. I went overboard.”

“Is that all you have to say? That you went overboard?” Agatha’s voice was low and calm — the kind of calm that comes from pure exhaustion. Rio merely shrugged, and Agatha nodded to herself, the realization sinking in. “You did more than go overboard, Rio. You crossed every line. You went too far.”

“I know, and I’m sorry. It won’t happen again,” Rio said, reaching out to take her hands, but Agatha pulled back.

“It won’t happen again because there won't be a next time. I can’t do this—not with you like this.”

“What are you talking about?” Rio asked, her face twisting in genuine confusion.

“You have a problem, Rio. You’re sick. This is a disease; it’s alcoholism,” Agatha said, her tone careful but firm.

Rio rolled her eyes, letting out a dismissive huff. “Agatha, don’t start. Not you. I don’t have a problem, I just drink a lot, and sometimes I overdo it. Yesterday was a mistake. I shouldn't have done it.” She spoke in a rush, her words tripping over each other as she tried once more to take Agatha’s hand. Agatha retreated again, dodging the touch. A lump formed in her throat as she witnessed the wall of denial. “I know it was an important night for you and I got carried away. I’m truly sorry. It won’t happen again.”

“You can’t even admit it,” Agatha whispered, closing her eyes as her chest ached. “Rio, I can’t be your support system right now. I just can't. This is serious. I’m at a crucial point in my degree, I’m trying to prove myself at work... I can’t manage this. I can't deal with this right now.”

“There’s nothing to deal with, Agatha!” Rio stood up, her voice rising. Agatha just stared at her, her brow furrowed, wanting to cry at the sight of Rio's mounting exasperation. “I’ve just had a lot on my mind! You know I drink when things get heavy. It’s just been a lot! I’m not good with change, and being here every week... I love you, but I hate this place. Between your college and me not even having a job... I feel—It’s not your fault. I’ve just got a lot on my mind. This is a lot, being here every week is a lot, you are a lot. ”

“And how do you expect us to survive like this? You just said our relationship is a lot for you. Did you ever think to tell me that? I didn’t even know you felt this way.”

“I didn't want to talk about it, I just—”

“You don’t have a job now, but you’ll find your way. You're applying to those universities in Montana and—”

“I’m not.” Rio interrupted, hanging her head.

Agatha froze. “But you said—”

“I know, but I’m not, Agatha.”

Agatha felt her eyes well up. She squeezed them shut, feeling as if she had just been punched in the gut. Once again, she was the only one in the relationship — making the plans, carrying the weight, and envisioning a future that Rio refused to commit to.

She swallowed hard. Tessa had been right. She was a fool for believing they could work, for surrendering to a passion that relied solely on a feeling neither of them could let go of. Beyond the love, they simply didn't make sense anymore.

“What are we doing, Rio? Seriously, what are we even doing?”

“Agatha, I love you, I—”

“Seriously, Rio. I can’t do this anymore. We can’t do this anymore.”

Chapter End Notes

first of all: happy holidays! i hope you all had a wonderful christmas and i wish you an even better new year. thank you so much for reading; i love writing, and this story comes from a very special place in my heart, so it means the world to me. i’d love to know what you’re thinking so far, if the writing is okay and what your thoughts and expectations are for the next chapters. i love every single comment and i'm always open to chatting and exchanging ideas with you. see you soon!

Chapter 15: expectations

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

"Honey, whem I'm above the trees, I see this for what it is, but now I'm right down in it and all the years I've given are just things we're dividing up. Showed you all of my hiding spots, I was dancing when the music stopped. And in disbelief, I can't face reinvention. I haven't met the new me yet" — Happiness, Taylor Swift

After tossing and turning for hours, barely catching a wink of sleep, Benito finally gave up. He’d managed three hours at most, but with the sun already beginning to bleed over the horizon, he knew staying in bed was a lost cause. He was bone-weary; the previous day had drained him completely. Seeing his sister in that state had been a cold dose of reality, a stinging reminder that felt like the chafing of an old scar.

Those six years. That was the only way anyone ever described it. No one dared say more; the sadness and darkness of that era were better left buried. He had watched her hit rock bottom, wandering through life like a hollow shell. The drinking, the brawling, the gambling, and the inevitable arrests — those had been the worst years of his life. The mere thought of it happening again made his skin crawl. He would do whatever it took to stop history from repeating itself, even if it meant swallowing every bit of his pride.

He washed his face and stared into the mirror, taking a steadying breath. In his reflection, he saw flashes of his father, but he saw Rio, too. She was a part of him, and despite the friction between them, his love for her was unwavering. He had to be patient; this wasn't about his own lingering resentment — it was about family. He was willing to try.

Benito threw on some shorts, gave his cheeks two sharp slaps to wake himself up, and shuffled toward the dining room. He was still yawning when he found Kate already at the table. He smiled, leaning down to press a tender kiss to her forehead.

"How do you manage to get up this early?" he asked.

Kate just shrugged, her mouth too full of food to answer, which coaxed a weary laugh out of him. "Did you sleep okay?" She nodded, wrapping her arms around his waist for a brief moment.

As he moved to take his seat, she wagged her index finger "no" and pointed toward the window.

"Your sister is out there," she said after swallowing.

Benito’s eyebrows shot up. It was the first morning since his arrival that Rio was actually home.

He headed straight for the kitchen and swung open the porch doors. He let out a heavy exhale at the sight of her. She was hunched over in her usual armchair, elbows on her knees, staring blankly at the horizon. It wasn't even six-thirty, yet she already had a beer in her hand. Judging by the cluster of empty cans at her feet, she was well into her first few of the day.

Benito walked quietly behind the chairs and took his accustomed seat. Between them, the armchair where their father used to sit lay empty — a silent, heavy void. Rio didn't so much as flinch at his arrival. She remained motionless, her gaze lost in the distance. As she took another slow sip of her beer, Benito let out a long, heavy sigh.

"I’m worried about you," he blurted out, his breath hitching. Rio didn't move; she wouldn’t even glance his way. "You gave me a fucking scare yesterday."

"I'm fine. You don't need to worry," she muttered, her eyes fixed on anything but him.

"You didn't look fine to me yesterday," he insisted.

Still staring at the horizon, she let out a sharp, scoffing breath. "Well, I'm fine now," she said, taking another pull of her beer. "You can go back to minding your own business. I don't need a lecture."

"Rio, I’m not here to lecture you or manage your life," Benito muttered, shaking his head as he studied her profile. She remained stubbornly turned away. "I’m here because I care."

"I didn't ask you to care."

"I care anyway. You're my sister." He furrowed his brows, desperate for her to meet his eyes, but she simply set her empty can on the floor and reached into the cooler for another. Benito looked down, defeated. "I’m sorry for not being here when you needed me. I'm sorry I didn't realize your resentment came from my absence. I truly am. But I can't go back and change the past. We both messed up, but does it have to stay this way? It's just you and me now."

For the first time, Rio turned her head. Benito felt his heart tighten under the weight of her stare. He couldn't decipher it — he had no idea what she was thinking—but he felt swallowed by those brown eyes, so hauntingly identical to his own.

Her gaze dropped to the empty chair between them, her brow furrowing with a sudden flash of grief, before she turned back to the horizon.

"What do you want me to say, Benny?" she asked, her voice sounding defeated. She shook her head and looked at him again. "What do you want me to do?"

"Stop pushing me away," he pleaded, his face etched with a silent appeal. Rio met his gaze with a stare that was chillingly expressionless.

"You were the one who put distance between us," she whispered. She let out a ghost of a breath before looking away and taking another drink.

Benito exhaled heavily, closing his eyes for several long seconds. "I know I left, but I never stopped being your brother. You were the one who pushed me away, Rio, and you’re still doing it."

"You never seemed to care about that," she muttered dryly. Even without her looking at him, he could feel the cold severity in her expression. "Why the sudden change of heart?"

"I always cared. I lost count of the times I called, the letters I wrote... I only stopped because every time I showed up, you made it clear you didn't want me around." He waited for her to look at him, but she didn't budge. He lowered his head, the weight of the rejection sinking in. "I just accepted it."

He took a deep breath, watching her nod vaguely while her gaze remained lost in the distance. "But I don't want to accept it anymore, Rio."

"Oh, really?" she mocked, arching an eyebrow as she looked back at him. "And what exactly do you plan to do?"

"Try," he replied with a shrug, noticing her eyes narrow. "I want to fix things between us. I want us to be a family again."

"What family, Benito?" she muttered, rolling her eyes and returning to her beer with a dismissive shake of her head. "Our father is dead. There is no more family." She practically spat the words.

"The two of us are a family," Benito said firmly. This time, she turned her entire body toward him, her stare boring into his very soul. "And it’s not just us. I have Kate and—"

"Your fiancée," she interrupted dryly.

He tilted his head, confused. "How do you know? I didn't—"

"Do you think I'm blind? You think I missed the rock on your finger and the diamond on hers?" she asked, her brow furrowing. Benito nodded, letting out a short, hollow laugh. "You didn't even bother to tell me. I didn't even know who she was before she showed up here."

"I haven't told anyone yet; we just got engaged," he said, looking down. "We were going to throw a party, but then Dad—" He cut himself off, pressing his lips thin and taking a steadying breath. His chest tightened as he watched Rio's jaw clench. "I was going to call. I would have invited you, too, even though I knew you wouldn't come."

"I really wouldn't have," she admitted. The blunt honesty of her expression made him let out a genuine, albeit small, laugh.

Benito fought the urge to smile, feeling a sudden warmth in his chest. For the first time in years, he saw a glimmer of a smile on his sister's lips — a flash of tenderness that stood in stark contrast to her icy words. It was a start.

"If the mountain won't come to Muhammad..." Benito began, his brow knitting in a silent plea for a truce. He watched her face cloud over as she weighed his words. "You asked what I wanted. Honestly, I want you around. I want us to talk and—"

"You’re already talking to me," Rio snapped, arching an eyebrow as she drained her beer. He took a long, heavy breath, watching her immediately crack open another.

He wanted to scold her, to snatch the can from her hand and hurl it away, but he reminded himself to take it one step at a time.

"I want you to be a part of my life," he said, his voice thick with emotion. Rio looked down, a frown deepening on her face. "You haven't even tried to talk to Kate, or get to know her."

Rio shook her head, letting out a huff of disdain before looking back at him.

"And why would I bother getting to know her if you’re just going to leave in a little while? It doesn’t matter, Benito." Her tone was dismissive, but the intensity in her gaze told a different story. "It’s your life, not mine."

Benito licked his lips and shook his head, choosing his words with care. He reminded himself to take it one small step at a time, no matter how agonizingly slow the progress felt.

"You’re right, it is my life," he said, raising his eyebrows pointedly. "But that’s exactly why I want you to know her, because she’s as much a part of my life as you are." Rio continued to stare at him, her expression a blank mask. "Besides, who said I’m leaving?"

For the first time, the armor cracked. Rio’s brow furrowed, her posture tensing as her eyes narrowed with unspoken questions.

"No one had to say it; it’s just what you do. You always leave," she said, her eyes boring into his. The weight of her words felt like a physical pressure against his throat, but he swallowed hard and held her gaze.

"This time, I’m staying."

"Are you?" she mocked. "For how long?"

"I don't have plans to leave," he replied with a shrug, watching her tilt her head as she analyzed his sincerity. "Kate likes it here. She grew up on a farm."

An involuntary smile tugged at his lips when he saw her reaction. Rio’s eyebrows shot up, and a hint of a genuine grin finally touched her face.

"Are you telling me that you, the ultimate playboy, found yourself a farm girl?" she murmured, sounding genuinely impressed. He laughed softly and nodded.

"I’m a farm boy too; the city facade is just a disguise," he said, gesturing to his tattooed torso. Rio rolled her eyes, but the ice was melting. "She grew up on a farm in Poland. Her father is a horse breeder."

Rio’s face lit up, her eyes finally showing a spark of real interest.

"They have hardy horses out there," she noted, gesturing with her beer can. "Riding through the snow builds incredible muscle; they’re solid animals." Benito nodded, encouraged by the softening in her expression. "So, she actually knows her way around a horse?"

"Yeah, she knows her stuff, and she loves them," he said. When she finally smiled, he couldn't help but mirror her. "Every day she’s up before me to ride. She loves heading out before dawn to watch the day break." Rio nodded in approval. "She took a liking to Raio, but since he’s yours, she’s been riding a mare named Cristal."

Rio’s smile broadened. "Cristal is one of my favorites. A good mare." She nodded thoughtfully. "You should take your fiancée to the racetrack sometime," she added with a tilt of her head. It was a start — a real one. "I think she’d enjoy it."

"Why don't you invite her yourself?" he asked. Rio took a slow sip of her beer, exhaling quietly. "I want you to get to know her. I think you’ll like her, but more than that, I want to bring this family back together. I want us all close again."

The smile faded, replaced by that guarded, intense stare.

"I’m hosting a lunch here at the house for everyone," he continued. "And I really want you to be there."

"Who is everyone?" she asked, her brow furrowing.

"Kate, obviously," he said, offering a small smile. "Lilia, Eva, Wanda, Natasha, the boys, Yelena, and—" He stumbled over the next names, swallowing hard before forcing them out. "Agatha and Nicholas." He saw her expression immediately darken. "Everyone, Rio. Our entire family."

"I'll pass," she replied, her voice slipping back into its usual mask of indifference.

Benito let out a heavy sigh, bowing his head for a moment before looking at her again. She had already retreated, propping her elbows on her knees and locking her gaze back on the horizon.

"Rio, this is important to me—"

"I’m not going," she interrupted flatly, not even bothering to look at him.

"Could you at least—"

"How many times do I have to say no? I said I’m not going."

Benito shook his head, burying his face in his hands. "Rio, you’re my sister," he murmured. When he looked up, his face was etched with a silent plea, but her gaze remained distant.

"Yes, I’m aware of that," was her only reply.

"I’ve apologized, and I truly am sorry. I told you I’m staying, and I’ve told you how much it means to me to have you in my life, but I can't do this alone. I can't force you to show up."

"Then don't," she shot back.

It was Benito’s turn to let out a sarcastic little huff, shaking his head.

Benito let out a sharp, sarcastic huff and shook his head. "You know what? Fine. If you don't want to come, don't come," he said firmly, standing up and turning toward the house. "I was just trying to spend time with you, to do something as a family and fix things. But it's up to you."

"Benito." Her voice caught him just as he reached the door. He turned and found her looking at him with an exasperated expression. He kept his face neutral, crossing his arms over his chest. "I’m not coming to your lunch."

"You’ve made that clear," he replied.

Agatha knew her timing had been poor, but as the days passed and the dust began to settle, she felt the ranch slowly drawing her back in. It was a restorative embrace she hadn't realized she needed. Even with her internal compass spinning and the future remains a blur, her strength returned piece by piece, anchored by the hands of those who supported her no matter what.

For the first time in years, she remembered what it felt like to be part of a family, and the safety of it was intoxicating. She could finally breathe. The crushing tightness in her chest — the weight of everything she’d been forced to swallow and endure in solitude — was finally starting to lift.

Benito had always been the brother she never had. Though she was an only child, she could still vividly recall the day he was born; the instant bond had been so strong she’d refused to leave Maria’s side. She had immersed herself in his world, learning to change diapers, hovering during his midnight crying fits, and steadying his tiny hands during his first uncertain steps. When she first left for California, he was only ten, yet he’d written to her almost every day. She had watched him grow from afar with fierce affection, cheering on his studies and eventually opening her home in Los Angeles to him when he chose to follow her path. Benito had walked beside her through every chapter of her life, and while her love for him was unconditional, it didn't stop her from calling him out — especially when he decided to charge ahead with a move as reckless as this engagement.

Agatha loved him like a brother and cherished Kate as if she were her own, but in her eyes, they were two fools acting like impulsive teenagers. Their plan was a ticking time bomb, made even more volatile by the setting of the ranch. Despite a thousand warnings, the pair remained dead-set on their nonsense. At this point, Agatha chose to find the humor in the situation, having already made one thing clear: when Riandra eventually discovered the brilliant motivation behind this sudden wedding, she wouldn’t say a word on their behalf. Agatha wouldn't be coming to their rescue.

And speaking of Rio, she was the topic of the hour. As always, it seemed impossible to escape her presence while on this ranch; she was everywhere, like an omnipresent entity.

Benito had scrambled to put this lunch together to make the engagement official, though it was hardly a secret. The thick, flashy diamond on Kate’s finger had been shouting the news since the moment they arrived. He had gathered everyone he considered family — close friends of his father and the ranch hands who had watched him grow up. The afternoon was relaxed and celebratory, yet beneath the surface, a silent tension lingered. No one spoke of it, but everyone felt the weight of Rio’s absence.

The meal had transitioned into long, smiling conversations about the future, but Agatha noticed how often eyes drifted toward the head of the table. Javier’s chair sat empty, flanked by the vacant seat to its right — a gaping, heavy hole in the gathering. Agatha caught the glances toward Rio’s spot because she was guilty of the same thing. To her, that empty chair was a physical manifestation of what Rio had become in her life: a void. It was a weight she had tried to bury for years, pretending it didn't exist, yet here it was, pressing down on her more heavily than ever.

By late afternoon, the crowd began to thin. Lilia and Evanora left for their daily crochet session, while Natasha and Yelena headed to the stables. Within minutes, only Agatha, Benito, Kate, and Wanda were left on the porch. The twins’ laughter echoed from inside the house, while Nicholas stood apart, leaning against the fence and staring at the horizon, lost in his own world.

"But did she say why she didn't want to come?" Wanda asked, her expression clouded with worry. Benito merely shrugged, his composure catching Agatha off guard.

"She’s racing today," he said, taking a sip of wine while holding Kate’s hand. "I know it’s just an excuse, she’d be here if she wanted to, but I get it."

Agatha, listening in silence, understood too.

Rio had always lived with an intensity that was as beautiful as it was exhausting. Agatha realized that while most people move through life in chapters, leaving the past behind, Rio simply collected every emotion she had ever felt. She lived in a mental landscape where nothing was ever discarded; she didn't know how to let go, how to forgive, or how to forget. Agatha knew her own return to Darby was a burden Rio hadn't asked to carry, a reminder of her brother’s long absence and the fresh sting of their father's death. She didn't blame Rio for staying away. No one chooses their feelings, and in this, Rio wasn't entirely wrong — even if she was a long way from being right.

"It's a difficult time for everyone," Benito continued, nodding his head. "She’s always been very intense, and I can't just expect to demand years of a relationship we didn't have. But I'm trying, and I think she wants to try, too."

"Do you really think so?" Wanda asked, her voice laced with worry. Benito met her gaze with a steady look, a flicker of hope touching his lips.

"I do," he replied. "We talked this morning." Wanda’s eyebrows shot up in surprise, and Agatha turned toward him, her full attention now locked on his every word. "I know she’s struggling, but she—" He paused, searching for the right way to put it. "It’s not like she’s opening up, but she didn’t flat-out reject me, which I think is a huge step forward."

"It really is," Wanda murmured, exhaling softly. "Usually, she just pushes everyone away."

"You and Natasha are the ones who know her best now," Benito admitted with a shrug. "I’m still flying blind. I don't know what to expect."

"I don’t think anyone does," Wanda confessed, taking a thoughtful sip of her wine. "But the fact that she didn’t push you away is huge. She needs this right now, she needs you here."

"I need her too," he said, his smile turning bittersweet. "I told her I was sorry for the distance, for not being here, and that I wanted to bridge the gap between us." Wanda nodded encouragingly. "She didn’t say much, but she finally agreed to some family time."

"Seriously?" Wanda asked, stunned.

"She invited us to Pedra Purpura tomorrow."

"Us?" Kate chimed in.

"Yes, both of us," Benito confirmed with a faint smile, before tilting his head toward Agatha. "You too."

Agatha nearly choked on her wine. "She invited me?" she stammered, shaking her head as a nervous laugh escaped her. Her heart hammered against her ribs. "You must have misheard. There is no way in hell she invited me."

It felt like an impossibility. As far as Agatha knew, Rio would rather take a punch to the jaw than spend five minutes in her company. And while she tried to tell herself that the cruel things Rio had said at Javier’s funeral were just the grief talking, the words still stung like an open wound.

"I told her that spending time together as a family mattered to me," Benito said, a slightly smug grin tugging at his mouth. Agatha narrowed her eyes, instantly suspicious of whatever he had pulled. "It's been too long. We can't change the past, but I want to fix this. I want her to know that no matter what has happened, we’re still family."

"Benito, I don't think—" Agatha started, her voice trailing off, but he dismissed her concerns with a quick wave.

"She didn't love the idea," he admitted, rolling his eyes, which made the story much more believable. "But I told her it mattered to me, and she caved, even if she did it through gritted teeth."

Still unsettled, Agatha took several long, steadying gulps of her wine. "I have never seen Rio give in a day in her life," she murmured, her eyes widening.

The concept of family time with Rio felt like a fever dream. Agatha knew that pendejo well enough to recognize this was yet another one of his brilliant ideas. Still, her heart hammered against her ribs and her palms grew damp at the thought of facing her. What would they even say? Would Rio be different? Could she actually be kind after everything that had happened? Agatha wasn't even sure she would find her own voice, let alone the right words.

"It’s been way too long since any of us really saw Rio," Benito said, his face twisting into a grimace that laid bare his guilt. Wanda nodded silently, her expression pained. "I’m not planning on leaving, and I don’t think you are either," he added with a shrug. "We’re going to have to face this sooner or later. Better tomorrow than dragging it out."

"Are you really planning on staying?" Wanda asked, her eyes searching Agatha’s. Agatha stiffened, her mouth opening and closing as she struggled to find the words.

"I think so. For a while," she finally managed. The shock in Wanda’s green eyes was palpable. "I just need some fresh air," Agatha added with a forced smile, trying to keep the mood light.

"Fresh air and new beginnings," Benito quipped. Agatha shot him a look that could kill, but he just shrugged it off. "I’ve never even been to Pedra Purpura. It’ll be cool, even if the vibes are a little weird at first."

"You’ve never been?" Wanda asked, clearly surprised.

"Never," he admitted, his voice heavy. "She avoided me every time I visited, spending more time at the bar than at home. I never wanted to crash a place that felt so much like her personal sanctuary."

Wanda nodded in understanding before offering a compassionate smile. "I think you’re in for a surprise. It’s the heart of the town, always packed, always lively."

"My father used to talk about Pedra Purpura a lot. I remember when it was about to open," Benito murmured with a nostalgic smile. "He was bursting with pride for her back then, seeing her get back on her feet on her own and running the business. He always said it was a success, and it really was."

"That was her first one, wasn't it?" Agatha asked, her curiosity getting the better of her. "The first of the wine bars?"

"That’s where it all started," Benito confirmed.

"I thought they were all under the Casa Vidal brand," Kate noted. "Why the different name for that one?"

"The name..." Benito’s voice trailed off, becoming vague and uncertain. Agatha looked away the moment she felt the weight of the room’s gaze shift toward her. Great. She cursed silently, wondering why Rio had clung to that name after all these years. "I don't know, I never asked," he stammered. Wanda cleared her throat to break the tension. "But it was the original, the one that sparked the whole chain so she kept it as it was."

"It’s nothing like the others," Wanda added, and Agatha was grateful for the pivot. "It’s simpler, more of a local haunt. It’s got a real bar feel. They even do karaoke."

"No way," Kate laughed. "I pictured something high-end and stiff like all the others."

"No, Rio hates that stuff," Wanda explained. "Pedra Purpura isn’t about the profit; it’s a passion project."

"I’ll admit, I’m actually a little excited," Benito said.

"I think it’ll be great for all of you," Wanda encouraged. "And since it’s a family thing, tell her to bring Olivia. It’s time you all met her."

That Olivia girl. Agatha’s ears perked up, her wine suddenly forgotten.

"Is Olivia her girlfriend?" Kate asked. Agatha’s eyes snapped to Wanda, waiting for the verdict.

"I wouldn’t go that far," Wanda said with a noncommittal tilt of her head. Agatha arched an eyebrow. "Neither of them is looking for anything serious, but they’re good friends. Honestly, Olivia’s been a really grounding influence for her."

Agatha felt a strange relief knowing Rio wasn't alone, that she had found people who looked out for her. She knew how much Rio struggled to open up, and it was good that she had someone she could trust. But the feeling was bittersweet. She used to be that person — the one who knew the map of Rio’s soul. It was heartbreaking to realize they had let a love that deep, the kind where one person understands you better than anyone else in the world, erode until they were just two strangers.

Two strangers who knew absolutely everything about each other and who, once upon a time, had loved each other more than anything. It was a bitter pill to swallow, and Agatha felt the familiar sting of regret deep in her core.

"I’ll leave that one up to Rio," Benito said. "I don’t want to push her. But if Olivia is important to my sister, I’ll meet her when the time is right."

"The fact that she invited us at all is a huge deal," Kate said softly.

"It really is," Wanda agreed, her face bright with optimism. "Especially there. She loves that place; it’s her whole world."

***

Eleven Years Ago

Natasha paced the room, an impressed smile lighting up her face. She was beaming with pride — not just because the project was finally coming together, but because her best friend was finally standing tall, fully embracing her potential.

The road here had been grueling, a path riddled with potholes and far more valleys than peaks, but Natasha had remained by Rio’s side through every mile. She never stopped believing that beneath the layers of bitterness and grief, there was a talented, vibrant soul. It hadn't been easy; watching Rio spiral for six agonizing years had been devastating. Yet, while everyone else let their hope flicker out, Natasha held on. Now, that loyalty was paying off: Rio had finally found her way back.

The tide began to turn two years ago when Rio made the choice to reclaim her life. Natasha knew Agatha’s wedding had been the catalyst; seeing the world move on while she stood still forced Rio to realize she needed to carve out a path of her own — one that wasn't just the wreckage of a past relationship. Admitting she had a problem was a hurdle, but for the first time, Rio reached for help. Supported by Javier’s unwavering belief, she began a grueling journey toward sobriety, marked by months of traveling between towns for weekly Alcoholics Anonymous meetings, and a grueling journey toward sobriety. Today, Rio was more than just sober; she was present, driven, and possessed a zest for life that had been missing for years. To Natasha, the transformation was nothing short of beautiful.

A pivotal moment in Rio’s recovery happened during a casual late-night chat about their futures. With Rio’s involvement in the family business, Natasha now had a job in administration and had shared her dreams of building a home and starting a family. Rio became her fiercest advocate, entrusting her with more responsibility to help her reach those goals. And while Rio wasn't ready for a family of her own, she craved a legacy — something that belonged solely to her. That was the spark that created Pedra Purpura.

It had been years since Natasha had seen that specific fire in her friend’s eyes. Together, they turned sketches into reality, capturing Rio’s vision of a sanctuary where she could both create and unwind. While Rio flourished in the winery’s creative process — obsessing over new blends and artisan catalogs — she wanted a place where people could truly experience her work. Javier, despite his initial worries about her sobriety, fully backed the idea; he knew Rio didn't just want to make wine, she wanted to witness the look of satisfaction on the faces of those who drank it.

Standing within the stone-walled space, Natasha marveled at how perfectly it captured Rio’s essence. From the hand-painted chairs and rustic wooden tables to the vintage jukebox and scattered family photos, the atmosphere was effortlessly cozy. She didn't just hope it would succeed; she knew it would be a hit.

"It looks incredible!" Natasha beamed, turning to Rio, who was busy tucking empty wine bottles into the partition wall — a custom decorative touch that had been her own brainchild.

"I know," Rio replied with a grin. She wiped her hands on her jeans and stepped toward her friend. "I'm actually excited."

"It's going to be a success, I have no doubt," Natasha said. Rio threw a clumsy arm around her shoulders, and for a moment, Natasha leaned her forehead against Rio’s.

"None of this would be happening without you," Rio said, her tone suddenly solemn. Natasha offered a soft smile as their eyes met. "Thank you. For everything."

"I didn't do anything, Rio," Natasha replied modestly, leaning back against the bar and sliding over to make space. "You’re the one who built this."

"I wouldn't have made it without my dad, or without you," Rio murmured with a shrug. Natasha smiled to herself; it wasn't often Rio lowered her guard enough to be so sentimental, and seeing her stripped of her armor was as funny as it was touching. "You’re my right hand in all of this, you know."

"And I'm being very well paid for it," Natasha shot back playfully. It wasn't entirely a joke. Rio laughed and gave a nod. "Honestly, I think I’m making more than I should."

Rio shook her head, giving Natasha’s shoulder an affectionate squeeze. "It’s a fair price for what you do. You’ve seen the numbers on the new wines; you’re as much a part of that success as I am," Rio said firmly. Natasha just smiled. "We’re a team. You handle the books, and I handle the wine."

"The wine is the business," Natasha pointed out with a playful roll of her eyes.

"True, but if I were in charge of the sales side, I’d get fleeced in a heartbeat," Rio laughed, and Natasha couldn't help but agree. "You’ve earned it. More than anyone."

They shared a quiet, meaningful look. Rio wasn't one for long speeches, but the depth of her gratitude was written all over her face. She didn't need to say a word for Natasha to feel it.

"So, how’s the house coming along?" Rio asked abruptly, pivoting the conversation the way she always did when things got too emotional. Natasha’s face lit up instantly.

"It’s almost ready; it looks incredible. I never thought I’d have a big house like this. I’m so excited. I think it’ll be finished this week, probably right along with Pedra Purpura."

"A double grand opening," Rio said with a grin, bumping her shoulder against Natasha’s. "I’m really happy for you."

"I’m happy for you, too."

"But speaking of milestones..." Rio started, her tone turning suggestive as she arched an eyebrow. Natasha rolled her eyes, shaking her head.

"Speaking of what?" she muttered bashfully. Rio wagged an index finger at her, making it clear she wasn't about to let the subject drop.

"You know exactly what I’m talking about."

Her cheeks flushed and her heart did a nervous little dance. Unable to hide her excitement any longer, Natasha reached into her inner jacket pocket and pulled out a small box. She flicked it open with her thumb, watching Rio’s smile stretch wide the moment she saw the ring.

It wasn't ostentatious. Even though she finally had the means to buy a massive diamond, she knew that wasn't Wanda’s style. Wanda had never cared for flashy things; Natasha knew it would be far more precious if it were meaningful. The delicate white gold band, set with a single modest stone, was perfect — a subtle reminder of how it all began, of that invisible thread of fate that had eventually pulled them together.

"It’s beautiful, Natasha. She’s going to love it."

"I hope so," Natasha replied, a nervous tremor in her voice as she fidgeted with the velvet box. "I’m asking her tomorrow. I’m taking her back to the square where we had our first date."

"I don’t know why you’re nervous; it’s obvious she’s going to say yes," Rio said. Natasha let out a small, awkward laugh.

"I know. I'm not worried about that, it’s just..." She trailed off with a shrug, meeting Rio’s curious gaze. "I’m just so happy. So excited. I’m finally at a point in my life where... I don’t know. I’m just happy."

"Me too, my friend," Rio said, her smile deepening.

Rio took a few slow steps back, pacing a small circle in the center of the room before opening her arms wide, a contemplative look on her face.

"Look at us now. Who would’ve thought?"

"I never doubted it," Natasha replied. "I always knew things would work out."

Rio closed the gap between them and gripped Natasha’s shoulders, giving them a meaningful squeeze. Her eyes were full of affection — a testament to the sisterhood they had shared since childhood. Natasha didn't need a mirror to know her own eyes reflected that same fierce pride.

"You’re my best friend," Rio said, staring intently at her. Natasha nodded; the sentiment was entirely mutual. "You believed in me when I was at my absolute worst. You held my hand when no one else would even look at me. You held onto me when I was ready to let go of myself." Natasha felt her eyes start to sting. "We went through so much hell. Honestly, you went through so much hell because of me," Rio added with a self-deprecating roll of her eyes, coaxing a laugh out of Natasha. "We both deserve this now. You deserve this. You showed me that life could actually be good again, and I’ll always be grateful for that. I’ll always have your back. What’s mine is yours. You’re my sister, Natasha."

Tears finally spilled over as Natasha shook her head, pulling Rio into a tight hug. Rio laughed, but she squeezed back just as hard. They had survived so much together, and in that moment, Natasha knew every struggle had been worth it.

"And I’d better be a bridesmaid," Rio teased as they finally pulled apart.

"I wouldn’t have it any other way."

They lingered for a while, lost in conversation about the upcoming wedding and the final touches for Pedra Purpura. Eventually, Rio led her into the office to show off her private cellar. Natasha took in the impressive collection, but as she looked at her best friend, a flicker of genuine worry crossed her face.

"You know, a bar isn’t exactly the conventional choice for a recovering alcoholic," she teased, though her eyes remained serious. Rio just laughed, giving a dismissive shrug.

"It’s not for me," she said sincerely, her gaze sweeping over the shelves with unmistakable pride. "This is my story, my creation. This is what I love, and Pedra Purpura is how I keep doing it the right way. I’m confident, Natasha. I really am."

Natasha believed her; she had seen the transformation firsthand. While everyone had been skeptical about Rio returning to the winery, no one could deny it was where she belonged. Rio had never been good with words, but Natasha found that every bottle Rio produced conveyed exactly what she was feeling — the passion, the inspiration, and the struggle. Javier had kept a watchful eye, and in two years, Rio hadn't faltered once. In the winery, she had found more than a job; she had found her purpose.

"Are you sure about the name, though?" Natasha asked softly, watching as Rio let out a heavy sigh and looked away.

The fact that Rio was still clinging to the past worried Natasha far more than the threat of a relapse. She knew exactly what — and who — had driven Rio to rock bottom for those six long years.

"The sign is already up outside, isn't it?" Rio countered.

"It’s your bar, you can call it whatever you want," Natasha murmured, choosing her words carefully under Rio's sharp gaze. "But this is supposed to be a new beginning. I’m not worried about the alcohol; I’m worried about this," she said, tapping her finger first against Rio’s temple and then over her heart.

Rio’s shoulders slumped as she let out a long breath. "Like I said, Natasha, it’s my story. I love this place, I love my craft, and that’s the foundation of everything: love." Natasha opened her mouth to argue, but Rio cut her off. "I’m not trying to live in the past, and I know that chapter is closed. But love is what inspires me. It’s what drove me to make my very first wine."

"Your bar, your name," Natasha repeated, echoing Rio’s tone with a shrug. "I'm just saying."

"That’s life," Rio said, heading toward the minibar. Natasha arched an eyebrow, watching curiously until Rio turned back with two glasses and a bottle of sparkling water. "Life is a mess, I’m the living proof of that, but that’s just the way it goes. It’s this strange mix of the past and the present that turns into something unrecognizable. We just keep going because we have faith in tomorrow."

"Since when did you become a philosopher?" Natasha laughed, accepting the glass.

"I acknowledge my past. Looking at the path I took to get here, the path we took together, it makes me happy. I like the present, and for the first time, I’m actually excited about the future."

Natasha beamed, her chest swelling with pride.

"To the future," she proposed, clinking her glass against Rio’s.

"To the future," Rio echoed with a smile.

***

Present Day

When Rio arrived home, she let out a sigh of relief. The driveway was empty, the cars were gone, and the house was still — exactly as she wanted it. It was only fair that Benito had his moment; after all, it was his home, too. But she wanted no part of the celebration. She valued her solitude, and it felt as if she hadn’t had a single moment of true peace since her father died.

It was a double-edged sword. As much as she craved isolation, she felt like a hostage to her own mind. Even after a busy day surrounded by friends, winning a race, and celebrating all afternoon, the internal noise persisted — a relentless, crushing static of thoughts. It was agonizing. Every time she touched a fragment of happiness, her mind dragged her back down, leaving her feeling heavy and shackled.

She felt as if she had been dropped into the middle of the ocean with iron weights lashed to her ankles. She could feel herself sinking, the burn in her lungs as they begged for air, but she couldn't break free. She didn't want to drown — not again — but she was trapped. The weight was almost physical, pressing against her heart and mind. She wanted to scream for help, but she couldn't. It felt as though her throat were filled with thorns; they tightened around her and wounded anyone she tried to reach.

She felt utterly lost, and despite the house being full of people, she had never felt more alone.

As Rio crossed toward the kitchen, she caught sight of them through the window. Benito, his fiancée, Wanda, and Agatha were on the porch, laughing and talking over a bottle of wine. The thorns in her throat tightened; the weights on her ankles grew heavier. She felt a sudden, sharp urge to cry. There they were — her family — finding a way to be happy without her. They had always managed to be happy in spite of her, and more than ever, she felt like a burden.

She knew she was to blame. It was her temperament, her choices, the way she pushed people away before they could get close. But the self-awareness didn't stop the ache. It hurt because she didn't know how to be any other way; she didn't know how to make the noise stop. She couldn't even regulate herself — always feeling too much, overthinking everything, carrying too much weight. She just wanted to understand why her mind worked this way.

Her thoughts were screaming, and she wanted to join them, but even if she could find her voice, she didn't know what she would say or who she would scream at.

Rio shook her head, tears blurring her vision. She ducked out of sight of the window and opened the fridge, grabbing a full six-pack of beer. As she closed the door, she found Nicholas standing there, watching her with his wide brown eyes and a shy smile. The sight made her heart ache.

"Hey, cowboy," she said, her voice thick and unsteady. His smile began to broaden, but then his gaze dropped to the beer in her hand.

Rio’s brow furrowed in confusion as she watched his expression wither. He glanced back and forth between her face and the six-pack, his warmth replaced by a sudden, sharp wariness. He took a hesitant step back.

"Is everything okay?" she asked, trying to bridge the gap, but he pulled away abruptly.

She watched as he shook his head and turned his back, nearly running toward the porch doors. Rio let out a heavy, broken sigh, her shoulders slumping in total defeat. The tears were streaming down her face now. She felt like a failure; even the one person who had seemed to like her at the moment didn't want to be around her.
